where \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta+r) = \texttt{pgamma}(D, a, \beta+r, \text{lower}=\text{FALSE})\). This is the tilted Euler-Lotka equation.

4. The exponential tilting mechanism

Comparing the tilted equation with the naive one reveals the key insight. The naive equation uses \(R_{\text{eff}} = R_0[1 - \eta \, \bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D;\, a,\, \beta)]\) and the natural GI. The tilted equation evaluates the survival function at rate \(\beta + r\) instead of \(\beta\).

For \(r > 0\) (growing epidemic), \(\beta + r > \beta\), so the tilted Gamma has a shorter mean (\(a/(\beta+r) < a/\beta\)). This means more of its mass is concentrated below \(D\), so \(\bar{F}(D; a, \beta+r) < \bar{F}(D; a, \beta)\). In words: from the perspective of the growth rate equation, fewer transmissions appear to be averted because the exponential discounting \(e^{-r\tau}\) already downweights late transmissions. The isolation truncation at \(D\) is thus partially redundant with the natural discounting.

This means \(r_{\text{actual}} > r_{\text{naive}}\): the epidemic grows faster than the naive prediction from \(R_{\text{eff}}\) alone.

5. Distorted generation interval density

The effective GI density among surviving transmissions is:

$$w^*(\tau) = \frac{(1 - \eta) g(\tau) + \eta \int_0^{\min(\tau, D)} f_\ell(\tau - \varepsilon) f_\varepsilon(\varepsilon) \, d\varepsilon}{1 - \text{TE}}$$

For the non-isolated fraction \((1-\eta)\), the GI is unchanged. For the isolated fraction, only transmissions with \(\varepsilon < D\) survive, giving a truncated convolution of \(f_\ell\) and the truncated \(f_\varepsilon\). The denominator \(1 - \text{TE} = 1 - \eta \, \bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta)\) normalises to a proper density.

6. Mean generation interval under isolation

For the isolated fraction, the mean generation time of surviving transmissions is:

$$E[\tau^* \mid \text{isolated, surviving}] = E[\ell] + E[\varepsilon \mid \varepsilon < D]$$ $$= \frac{b}{\beta} + \frac{\int_0^D \varepsilon \, f_{\text{Gamma}}(\varepsilon;\, a,\, \beta) \, d\varepsilon}{F_{\text{Gamma}}(D;\, a,\, \beta)}$$

The truncated mean of \(\varepsilon\) uses the identity:

$$\int_0^D \varepsilon \, f_{\text{Gamma}}(\varepsilon;\, a,\, \beta) \, d\varepsilon = \frac{a}{\beta} \, F_{\text{Gamma}}(D;\, a+1,\, \beta)$$

so:

$$E[\varepsilon \mid \varepsilon < D] = \frac{a}{\beta} \cdot \frac{F_{\text{Gamma}}(D;\, a+1,\, \beta)}{F_{\text{Gamma}}(D;\, a,\, \beta)}$$

The overall mean GI under isolation, mixing isolated and non-isolated individuals weighted by their transmission contributions:

$$E[\tau^*] = \frac{(1 - \eta) \cdot \frac{\alpha}{\beta} + \eta \cdot F_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta) \cdot \left[\frac{b}{\beta} + \frac{a}{\beta} \cdot \frac{F_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a+1, \beta)}{F_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta)}\right]}{1 - \eta \, \bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta)}$$

Simplifying:

$$E[\tau^*] = \frac{(1 - \eta) \cdot \frac{\alpha}{\beta} + \eta \left[\frac{b}{\beta} \cdot F_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta) + \frac{a}{\beta} \cdot F_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a+1, \beta)\right]}{1 - \eta \, \bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta)}$$

7. Limiting cases

\(\psi \to 0\) (spike limit)

When \(\psi \to 0\), \(a = \psi\alpha \to 0\) and \(b \to \alpha\). The individual infectious period \(\varepsilon \sim \text{Gamma}(a, \beta)\) degenerates to a point mass at 0. Isolation cannot truncate a zero-duration event: \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; 0, \beta+r) = 0\) for \(D > 0\). The tilted Euler-Lotka reduces to the naive equation. No GI distortion for spikes.

\(\psi = 1\) (smooth limit)

When \(\psi = 1\), \(a = \alpha\) and \(b = 0\). The entire generation interval is the infectious period \(\varepsilon\); there is no latent period. The tilting effect is maximal: \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; \alpha, \beta+r)\) can differ substantially from \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; \alpha, \beta)\). Maximal GI distortion for smooth profiles.

\(\eta = 0\) (no intervention)

With \(\eta = 0\), the bracketed term in the tilted Euler-Lotka is 1, recovering the standard equation \(1 = R_0 (\beta/(\beta+r))^\alpha\). The naive and actual growth rates coincide. No distortion without intervention.

\(D \to \infty\) (late isolation)

As \(D \to \infty\), \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta+r) \to 0\), so no transmissions are averted. The tilted Euler-Lotka again reduces to the standard equation.

\(D \to 0\) (immediate isolation)

As \(D \to 0\), \(\bar{F}_{\text{Gamma}}(D; a, \beta+r) \to 1\) (for \(a > 0\)), so \(1 = R_0 (\beta/(\beta+r))^\alpha [1 - \eta]\), i.e. \(R_{\text{eff}} = R_0(1-\eta)\) and the GI is unchanged (all post-onset transmissions are removed equally in the tilted and untilted versions). In this case, naive and actual agree because truncation at the onset is so aggressive that the GI shape doesn't matter.

8. Growth rate bias

Define:

  • \(r_{\text{naive}} = \beta(R_{\text{eff}}^{1/\alpha} - 1)\) from the standard Euler-Lotka with the natural GI
  • \(r_{\text{actual}}\) = root of the tilted Euler-Lotka: \(R_0 (\beta/(\beta+r))^\alpha [1 - \eta \, \bar{F}(D; a, \beta+r)] = 1\)

The growth rate bias is:

$$\Delta r = r_{\text{actual}} - r_{\text{naive}} \geq 0$$

with equality when \(\psi = 0\), \(\eta = 0\), or \(D \to \infty\). The bias is largest when \(\psi\) is large (smooth profiles), \(\eta\) is large (high intervention coverage), and \(D\) is moderate (truncation in the body of the distribution).

For the relative bias, consider:

$$\text{Relative bias} = \frac{r_{\text{actual}} - r_{\text{naive}}}{r_{\text{naive}}} \times 100\%$$

This quantifies the percentage overestimate of intervention effectiveness when GI distortion is ignored.

Detection Survival and Punctuation: General Theory

2026-03-23

Setup: \(S(\tau)\) is a detection survival function (continuous, \(S(0) = 1\), monotonically decreasing) and \(\{a_\psi(\tau)\}\) is a family of infectiousness profiles indexed by a punctuation parameter \(\psi\), all unimodal and integrating to \(R_0\). More punctuated profiles are narrower/peakier. We want to understand the sign of $$\Delta = \int_0^\infty S(\tau)\bigl[a_{\psi_1}(\tau) - a_{\psi_2}(\tau)\bigr]\,d\tau$$ where \(\psi_1\) is more punctuated than \(\psi_2\).

1. The escaped-transmission functional

The quantity \(\int S(\tau)\,a_\psi(\tau)\,d\tau\) represents the expected infectiousness that escapes detection. Without detection, total infectiousness is \(R_0 = \int a_\psi\,d\tau\). With detection, it is reduced to \(\int S \cdot a_\psi\). Defining \(f_\psi = a_\psi / R_0\) as the normalised density of transmission timing, the escaped fraction is:

$$\frac{1}{R_0}\int S(\tau)\,a_\psi(\tau)\,d\tau = \mathbb{E}_{f_\psi}[S(\tau)]$$

So the question reduces to: how does the expected value of a decreasing function \(S\) change as we concentrate the distribution \(f_\psi\)?

2. Proposition (Single-crossing lemma)

Before considering mean-preserving contractions, there is a simpler result for the case where the difference \(\delta(\tau) = a_{\psi_1}(\tau) - a_{\psi_2}(\tau)\) crosses zero exactly once.

Lemma (Chebyshev-type). Suppose \(\delta(\tau)\) changes sign from \(+\) to \(-\) at a unique crossing point \(\tau^*\), and \(\int \delta\,d\tau = 0\). If \(S\) is decreasing, then $$\int_0^\infty S(\tau)\,\delta(\tau)\,d\tau \geq 0.$$

Proof. On \([0, \tau^*]\), we have \(\delta(\tau) \geq 0\) and \(S(\tau) \geq S(\tau^*)\). On \([\tau^*, \infty)\), we have \(\delta(\tau) \leq 0\) and \(S(\tau) \leq S(\tau^*)\). Therefore:

$$\int_0^\infty S\,\delta\,d\tau \geq S(\tau^*)\int_0^{\tau^*}\delta\,d\tau + S(\tau^*)\int_{\tau^*}^{\infty}\delta\,d\tau = S(\tau^*)\underbrace{\int_0^\infty \delta\,d\tau}_{=\,0} = 0. \quad\square$$

Interpretation: If profile \(\psi_1\) has more early mass and less late mass than \(\psi_2\), with a single crossing, then \(\psi_1\) always has more transmission escaping detection. Monotone decreasing \(S\) always weights early mass more heavily. This is a first-order (location) effect.

But for comparing profiles that differ primarily in spread (not location), the difference \(\delta\) typically has two sign changes: negative in the left tail, positive near the mode, negative in the right tail (or the reverse). This is where Jensen's inequality enters.

3. Proposition (Jensen / convex-order effect)

Proposition. Suppose \(f_{\psi_1}\) and \(f_{\psi_2}\) have the same mean \(\mu\), but \(f_{\psi_1}\) is a mean-preserving contraction of \(f_{\psi_2}\) (i.e., \(f_{\psi_1} \leq_{\text{cx}} f_{\psi_2}\) in convex order, meaning \(\text{Var}_{\psi_1} < \text{Var}_{\psi_2}\)). Then:
  • \(S\) convex on the support \(\Longrightarrow \mathbb{E}_{\psi_1}[S] \leq \mathbb{E}_{\psi_2}[S]\) — punctuation reduces escape, helps detection.
  • \(S\) concave on the support \(\Longrightarrow \mathbb{E}_{\psi_1}[S] \geq \mathbb{E}_{\psi_2}[S]\) — punctuation increases escape, hurts detection.
  • \(S\) linear on the support \(\Longrightarrow \mathbb{E}_{\psi_1}[S] = \mathbb{E}_{\psi_2}[S]\) — punctuation irrelevant.

This follows directly from Jensen's inequality. To leading order in the variance difference:

$$\mathbb{E}_{\psi_1}[S] - \mathbb{E}_{\psi_2}[S] \;\approx\; \tfrac{1}{2}\,S''(\mu)\bigl(\text{Var}_{\psi_1} - \text{Var}_{\psi_2}\bigr)$$

Since \(\text{Var}_{\psi_1} < \text{Var}_{\psi_2}\) (more punctuated = less spread), the sign of \(\Delta\) is determined by \(S''(\mu)\):

$$\boxed{\;\text{sign}(\Delta) = -\text{sign}\bigl(S''(\mu)\bigr)\;}$$

(where \(\Delta > 0\) means the more punctuated profile has more escape).

4. Interpretation via the detection hazard

Writing \(S(\tau) = \exp\!\bigl(-\int_0^\tau h(u)\,du\bigr)\) where \(h(\tau) = -S'(\tau)/S(\tau) \geq 0\) is the detection hazard, the second derivative of \(S\) is:

$$S''(\tau) = \bigl[h(\tau)^2 - h'(\tau)\bigr]\,S(\tau)$$

Since \(S > 0\), the curvature condition becomes:

$$S \text{ convex at } \tau \iff h'(\tau) < h(\tau)^2 \qquad\qquad S \text{ concave at } \tau \iff h'(\tau) > h(\tau)^2$$

In words:

  • \(S\) is convex when the hazard is constant or slowly increasing (detection is "steady-state"). The paradigm case is exponential \(S\) (constant hazard).
  • \(S\) is concave when the hazard is rapidly increasing (detection is "ramping up"). This happens during the onset of a detection regime — e.g., the period where symptom awareness is kicking in, or where test sensitivity is climbing.
Rule of thumb: Punctuation helps detection when the detection hazard is mature (steady or slowly growing), and hurts detection when the hazard is ramping up (rapidly accelerating). The key quantity is whether \(h'/h^2 \lessgtr 1\) at the infectiousness peak.

5. Examples

5a. Constant-hazard detection: \(S(\tau) = e^{-h\tau}\)

\(S'' = h^2 e^{-h\tau} > 0\) everywhere (convex). So punctuation always helps detection. Intuitively: a constant hazard means that each moment of delay costs the same proportional detection rate. Concentrating infectiousness in a short window gives that infectiousness less total "exposure" to the cumulative hazard.

Wait — that intuition may seem backwards; let's be precise. With exponential \(S\) and a symmetric profile centred at \(\mu\):

$$\mathbb{E}[e^{-h\tau}] = e^{-h\mu}\,M_f(h)$$

where \(M_f(h) = \mathbb{E}[e^{-h(\tau - \mu)}]\) is the centred moment generating function. Since \(e^{-hx}\) is convex, \(M_f(h) \geq 1\), with equality iff \(f\) is degenerate. More spread \(\Rightarrow\) higher \(M_f(h)\) \(\Rightarrow\) more escape. So the more punctuated (less spread) profile has less escape — punctuation helps detection. ✔

5b. Step-function detection: \(S(\tau) = \mathbf{1}[\tau < T]\)

\(S\) is flat (linear, with \(S'' = 0\)) everywhere except at \(\tau = T\), where it has a concave kink. The integral is simply:

$$\mathbb{E}_f[S] = F_f(T) = P(\tau < T)$$

For mean-preserving contractions: concentrating the profile around \(\mu\) pulls mass away from the tails. If \(T > \mu\), there is more right-tail mass that gets pulled in below \(T\), so \(F(T)\) increases — punctuation increases escape (hurts detection). If \(T < \mu\), mass gets pulled away from the left tail below \(T\), so \(F(T)\) decreases — punctuation decreases escape (helps detection).

In short: a step-function \(S\) favours the profile whose mass is most concentrated in the pre-detection window. If detection comes after the peak, punctuation is bad for detection (the peak is fully "inside" the window). If detection comes before the peak, punctuation is good (less tail leaks into the early window).

5c. Weibull-type detection: \(S(\tau) = e^{-(\tau/\lambda)^k}\)

The curvature transition occurs where \(S'' = 0\), which gives:

$$\tau^* = \lambda\left(\frac{k-1}{k}\right)^{1/k} \qquad (k > 1)$$

For \(\tau < \tau^*\): \(S\) is concave (ramping-up phase). For \(\tau > \tau^*\): \(S\) is convex (mature phase). So:

  • Infectiousness peak in the early (concave) region: punctuation hurts detection.
  • Infectiousness peak in the late (convex) region: punctuation helps detection.

For \(k = 2\) (Rayleigh-type survival): \(\tau^* = \lambda / \sqrt{2}\). For \(k \leq 1\), \(S\) is convex everywhere — same qualitative behaviour as exponential.

6. The general case: mean shift + spread change

In many parameterisations of punctuation (including the shifted-Gamma model), changing punctuation shifts both the mean and the spread of the individual profile. If profile \(\psi_1\) has mean \(\mu_1\) and variance \(\sigma_1^2\), and profile \(\psi_2\) has mean \(\mu_2\) and variance \(\sigma_2^2\), then to second order:

$$\Delta \approx R_0\Bigl[S'(\bar\mu)\,\underbrace{(\mu_1 - \mu_2)}_{\text{location shift}} + \tfrac{1}{2}\,S''(\bar\mu)\,\underbrace{(\sigma_1^2 - \sigma_2^2)}_{\text{spread change}}\Bigr]$$

where \(\bar\mu = (\mu_1 + \mu_2)/2\). The two effects can reinforce or oppose each other. In the shifted-Gamma model, for a given individual with onset \(l\), the individual profile \(f_\psi(\cdot - l)\) has mean \(l + \psi\alpha/\beta\) and variance \(\psi\alpha/\beta^2\), so more punctuation (lower \(\psi\)) both shifts the mean earlier (towards higher \(S\), increasing escape) and reduces variance (effect depends on \(S''\)).

7. Critical caveat: population-level invariance for exogenous \(S\)

This is crucial for connecting to the punctuation model. In the shifted-Gamma framework, each individual has profile \(a_i(\tau) = R_0\,f_\psi(\tau - l_i)\), where \(l_i \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha, \beta)\) and \(f_\psi = \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\). The population-level profile is:

$$A(\tau) = \mathbb{E}_{l}\bigl[R_0\,f_\psi(\tau - l)\bigr] = R_0 \cdot \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)$$

which is \(\psi\)-invariant (by the Gamma convolution property). Therefore, if \(S(\tau)\) depends only on time-since-infection and is the same for all individuals, then:

$$\mathbb{E}_l\!\left[\int S(\tau)\,a_i(\tau; l)\,d\tau\right] = \int S(\tau)\,A(\tau)\,d\tau$$
Invariance result: If \(S(\tau)\) is exogenous (not coupled to individual biology), the population-level escaped transmission \(\int S\,A\,d\tau\) does not depend on \(\psi\). The individual-level Jensen effects (Section 3) average out perfectly over the distribution of onset times \(l_i\).

This means that for the punctuation parameter \(\psi\) to matter at the population level, we need \(S\) to be endogenous — coupled to the individual's infectiousness profile. Natural mechanisms include:

  1. Symptom-triggered detection: Symptom onset correlates with the infectiousness peak. For a more punctuated individual, symptom onset is more tightly linked to the (narrower) infectious period, creating a different \(S_i(\tau)\) than for a smooth individual.
  2. Viral-load-triggered detection: Test sensitivity depends on viral load, which tracks infectiousness. The effective \(S_i(\tau)\) depends on the shape of \(a_i(\tau)\) itself.
  3. Contact-trace-triggered detection: Detection of individual \(i\) depends on when their infector was detected, which depends on the infector's profile.

In all these cases, \(S_i\) and \(a_i\) are positively correlated (high infectiousness \(\Leftrightarrow\) high detectability), which tends to make detection more effective against smooth profiles (whose detectable window overlaps more with their infectious window).

8. Summary table

Detection regime \(S(\tau)\) curvature Hazard condition Punctuation effect on escape
Constant hazard (exponential) Convex \(h' = 0\) Decreases escape (helps detection)
Slowly increasing hazard Convex \(h' < h^2\) Decreases escape (helps detection)
Rapidly increasing hazard Concave \(h' > h^2\) Increases escape (hurts detection)
Linear \(S\) Zero — No effect (to second order)
Step function at \(T\) Concave kink at \(T\) — Depends on \(T\) vs \(\mu\)

The overarching message: the curvature of the detection survival function at the infectiousness peak determines whether punctuation helps or hurts detection. But at the population level in the shifted-profile model, exogenous \(S\) produces no net effect — the individual-level effects integrate to zero. To get a population-level effect, detection must couple to individual biology.

Mode-Anchored Detection: Breaking the Invariance

2026-03-23

We now consider the case where the detection survival function is anchored to the mode of each individual's infectiousness profile. That is, each individual \(i\) has survival function \(S_i(\tau) = \tilde{S}(\tau - \tau_{\text{mode},i})\), where \(\tau_{\text{mode},i}\) is the time at which individual \(i\)'s infectiousness peaks and \(\tilde{S}\) is a canonical survival function defined relative to the mode.

9. The mode-anchoring transformation

In the shifted-Gamma framework, individual \(i\) has infectiousness profile \(a_i(\tau) = R_0\,f_\psi(\tau - l_i)\), where \(f_\psi = \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\) has mode at \(m_\psi = (\psi\alpha - 1)/\beta\). The individual's peak infectiousness occurs at time:

$$\tau_{\text{mode},i} = l_i + m_\psi$$

With mode-anchored detection, individual \(i\)'s survival function is \(S_i(\tau) = \tilde{S}(\tau - l_i - m_\psi)\). Substituting into the escaped-transmission integral:

$$\int_0^\infty S_i(\tau)\,a_i(\tau)\,d\tau = R_0\int_0^\infty \tilde{S}(\tau - l_i - m_\psi)\,f_\psi(\tau - l_i)\,d\tau$$

Setting \(u = \tau - l_i\):

$$= R_0 \int_0^\infty \tilde{S}(u - m_\psi)\,f_\psi(u)\,du$$
Key cancellation: The onset shift \(l_i\) has dropped out entirely. The escaped transmission depends only on \(\psi\) (which determines both the profile shape \(f_\psi\) and the mode \(m_\psi\)), not on the individual's random onset time. This quantity is identical for all individuals sharing the same \(\psi\), and therefore the population average equals the individual-level quantity. The invariance is broken.

10. The mode-centred residual

Define the mode-centred residual \(\tilde\varepsilon = u - m_\psi\), representing the timing of a transmission event relative to the individual's infectiousness peak. Then:

$$\frac{1}{R_0}\int S_i(\tau)\,a_i(\tau)\,d\tau = \mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon \sim \tilde{f}_\psi}\!\bigl[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\bigr]$$

where \(\tilde{f}_\psi\) is the density of \(\tilde\varepsilon\). The properties of \(\tilde\varepsilon\) are:

  • Mode: \(\tilde\varepsilon\) has its mode at \(0\) by construction.
  • Support: \(\tilde\varepsilon \in [-m_\psi, \infty)\). For \(\psi\alpha > 1\), there is mass to the left of the mode; for \(\psi\alpha \leq 1\), the mode is at the left boundary and \(\tilde\varepsilon \geq 0\).
  • Mean: \(\mathbb{E}[\tilde\varepsilon] = \underbrace{\psi\alpha/\beta}_{\text{mean of } f_\psi} - \underbrace{(\psi\alpha - 1)/\beta}_{m_\psi} = 1/\beta\), which is constant across \(\psi\).
  • Variance: \(\text{Var}(\tilde\varepsilon) = \psi\alpha/\beta^2\), which decreases linearly in \(\psi\) (more punctuated \(\to\) less variance).
  • Skewness: \(\text{skew}(\tilde\varepsilon) = 2/\sqrt{\psi\alpha}\), which increases with punctuation.

The constancy of \(\mathbb{E}[\tilde\varepsilon] = 1/\beta\) is important: it means that as \(\psi\) varies, the mode-centred profile undergoes a mean-preserving contraction. The mean of a Gamma always exceeds its mode by exactly \(1/\beta\), regardless of the shape parameter. So the only effect of changing \(\psi\) (to leading order) is a change in variance.

11. Main result: curvature of \(\tilde{S}\) at the mode

Since \(\tilde\varepsilon\) undergoes a mean-preserving contraction as \(\psi\) decreases, the Jensen result from Section 3 applies directly. Writing the escaped fraction as a Taylor expansion around the mean \(\mu_{\tilde\varepsilon} = 1/\beta\):

$$\mathbb{E}\bigl[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\bigr] = \tilde{S}(1/\beta) + \tfrac{1}{2}\,\tilde{S}''(1/\beta)\,\text{Var}(\tilde\varepsilon) + O\bigl(\text{Kurt} \cdot \text{Var}^2\bigr)$$

Since \(\text{Var}(\tilde\varepsilon) = \psi\alpha/\beta^2\):

$$\boxed{\;\frac{\partial}{\partial\psi}\,\mathbb{E}\bigl[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\bigr] \;\approx\; \frac{\alpha}{2\beta^2}\,\tilde{S}''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right)\;}$$
Direction rule: The sign of the \(\psi\)-effect on escaped transmission is determined by the curvature of \(\tilde{S}\) evaluated at \(1/\beta\) — the mean–mode gap of the individual profile. Specifically:
  • \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) > 0\) (convex): increasing \(\psi\) (less punctuated, more spread) increases escape. Equivalently, punctuation helps detection.
  • \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) < 0\) (concave): increasing \(\psi\) decreases escape. Punctuation hurts detection.
  • \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) = 0\) (linear): no second-order effect of punctuation.
Note: this is the curvature of \(\tilde{S}\) at the mean of \(\tilde\varepsilon\) (i.e., at one mean–mode gap to the right of the mode), not at the mode itself.

12. Why \(1/\beta\), not \(0\)?

It may be surprising that the relevant evaluation point is \(\tilde{S}(1/\beta)\) rather than \(\tilde{S}(0)\). The reason is that the Jensen expansion is around the mean of the distribution, not its mode. For a right-skewed distribution (Gamma), the mean lies to the right of the mode by exactly \(1/\beta\). So the relevant curvature is the curvature of \(\tilde{S}\) at the point "one mean–mode gap to the right of peak infectiousness."

In our parameterisation with \(\beta = \alpha/T\) (where \(T\) is the mean generation interval), the mean–mode gap is:

$$\frac{1}{\beta} = \frac{T}{\alpha}$$

With \(T = 5\) days and \(\alpha = 10\): the gap is \(0.5\) days. So we evaluate \(\tilde{S}''\) half a day after peak infectiousness. As \(\alpha \to \infty\) (the GI distribution sharpens), this gap shrinks to zero and we recover evaluation at the mode.

13. Geometric interpretation: supra-modal mass

The supra-modal mass \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > 0) = P(\text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta) > m_\psi)\) gives a concrete geometric quantity related to the step-function limit of \(\tilde{S}\). Recall from earlier work:

\(\psi\alpha\)Supra-modal massInfra-modal mass
\(\leq 1\)1.0000.000
20.7360.264
50.6160.384
100.5710.429
\(\to\infty\)0.5000.500

For a step-function \(\tilde{S}\) that drops from 1 to 0 at \(\tilde\varepsilon = 0\) (i.e., instantaneous detection at the mode), the escaped transmission equals the infra-modal mass. This is strictly increasing in \(\psi\alpha\), confirming that more punctuated profiles escape less under mode-triggered detection. The step function has a concave kink at 0, but since all the mass to the left of 0 survives and all the mass to the right doesn't, what matters is how much mass is packed before vs after the mode — and spike profiles pack almost everything after.

But notice: the prevented transmission (supra-modal mass) is large for all values of \(\psi\alpha\) (always above 50%). This means mode-anchored detection is inherently effective regardless of punctuation. The question is only about the marginal effect of punctuation.

14. The general framework (beyond shifted-Gamma)

The results above rely on two properties of the Gamma distribution:

  1. The mean–mode gap \(1/\beta\) is constant across shape parameters.
  2. The family is ordered by convex order (mean-preserving contraction as shape decreases).

For a general unimodal family \(f_\psi\) with mode \(m_\psi\) and mean \(\mu_\psi\), the escaped transmission under mode-anchored \(\tilde{S}\) is:

$$\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon_\psi)] \;\approx\; \tilde{S}(\mu_\psi - m_\psi) + \tfrac{1}{2}\,\tilde{S}''(\mu_\psi - m_\psi)\,\text{Var}_\psi(\tilde\varepsilon) + \cdots$$

If both the mean–mode gap \(g_\psi = \mu_\psi - m_\psi\) and the variance change with \(\psi\), we have two effects:

$$\frac{d}{d\psi}\,\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon_\psi)] \;\approx\; \tilde{S}'(g_\psi)\,\frac{dg_\psi}{d\psi} + \tfrac{1}{2}\,\tilde{S}''(g_\psi)\,\frac{d\text{Var}_\psi}{d\psi}$$

The first term is a location effect: if the mean–mode gap changes with punctuation, \(\tilde{S}\) is evaluated at a different point, creating a first-order effect. The second term is the familiar curvature/Jensen effect. For the Gamma family, \(dg/d\psi = 0\), so the location effect vanishes and the curvature effect is the whole story.

15. Connection to the detection hazard

Writing \(\tilde{S}(t) = \exp\!\bigl(-\int_0^t \tilde{h}(u)\,du\bigr)\), where now \(\tilde{h}(t)\) is the detection hazard as a function of time-since-mode:

$$\tilde{S}''(t) = \bigl[\tilde{h}(t)^2 - \tilde{h}'(t)\bigr]\,\tilde{S}(t)$$

So the curvature condition at the evaluation point \(t_0 = 1/\beta\) is:

$$\tilde{S}''(t_0) > 0 \;\iff\; \tilde{h}'(t_0) < \tilde{h}(t_0)^2$$

In words: punctuation helps detection when the detection hazard is "mature" (not accelerating too fast) one mean–mode gap after the infectiousness peak.

For the specific mechanisms the user has in mind:

15a. Regular testing in a detectability window

Suppose an individual is detectable in the window \([m - d_l,\; m + d_r]\) (i.e., \(\tilde\varepsilon \in [-d_l, d_r]\)), and tests arrive as a Poisson process with rate \(\lambda\). Then:

$$\tilde{S}(t) = \begin{cases} 1 & t < -d_l \\ e^{-\lambda(t + d_l)} & -d_l \leq t \leq d_r \\ e^{-\lambda(d_l + d_r)} & t > d_r \end{cases}$$

Within the detectability window, \(\tilde{S}\) is exponential — convex. So \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) > 0\) whenever \(1/\beta\) falls within \([-d_l, d_r]\), and punctuation helps detection.

If instead tests arrive at a regular cadence with random phase (uniform on \([0, c]\)), then within the window \(\tilde{S}(t) \approx \max(0, 1 - (t + d_l)/c)\), which is linear: \(\tilde{S}'' = 0\). In this case, the second-order punctuation effect vanishes, and one must look to higher-order terms (skewness).

15b. Symptom-triggered detection

If symptoms appear at time \(m + \delta\) where \(\delta\) has density \(p(\delta)\), and isolation occurs immediately upon symptoms, then:

$$\tilde{S}(t) = P(\delta > t) = 1 - F_\delta(t)$$

so \(\tilde{S}'(t) = -p(t)\) and \(\tilde{S}''(t) = -p'(t)\). Therefore:

$$\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) > 0 \;\iff\; p'(1/\beta) < 0$$

That is, punctuation helps detection iff the symptom-onset density is decreasing at one mean–mode gap after the infectiousness peak. If the symptom density is still ramping up at that point (many people become symptomatic shortly after peak infectiousness), punctuation hurts. If symptoms are most likely at or before the peak and the density is declining by \(1/\beta\), punctuation helps.

16. Summary

Mode-anchored detection breaks the \(\psi\)-invariance because the onset shift \(l_i\) cancels between the detection and infectiousness processes. The escaped transmission becomes:

$$R_{\text{eff}} = R_0 \cdot \mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon \sim \tilde{f}_\psi}\!\bigl[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\bigr]$$

where \(\tilde\varepsilon\) is the mode-centred residual (mean \(1/\beta\), variance \(\psi\alpha/\beta^2\)). The \(\psi\)-sensitivity is:

$$\frac{\partial R_{\text{eff}}}{\partial\psi} \approx \frac{R_0\,\alpha}{2\beta^2}\;\tilde{S}''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right)$$

Punctuation helps detection when \(\tilde{S}\) is convex at \(1/\beta\) (detection hazard mature / slowly growing), and hurts when \(\tilde{S}\) is concave (hazard ramping up). The evaluation point \(1/\beta = T/\alpha\) is the mean–mode gap of the Gamma distribution — typically a fraction of a day for realistic parameters.

Connecting the median–mode and mean–mode results

2026-03-23

The earlier analysis (Sections on "The equivalence point of the overlap rule") found that the spike–smooth crossover for step-function detection occurs at \(D_0^* = \text{median} - \text{mode} \approx 2/(3\beta)\). The current analysis (Section 11) finds that the \(\psi\)-sensitivity of escaped transmission under general \(\tilde{S}\) depends on \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta)\) — the curvature at the mean–mode gap. How do these relate?

17. The two reference points

Both results identify a special point in mode-relative time where punctuation "doesn't matter." But they do so at different levels of generality:

Earlier result Current result
Detection model Step function at \(D_0\): instant isolation at a fixed offset from the mode General smooth \(\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\)
Question asked At what \(D_0\) is escaped transmission \(\psi\)-independent? In which direction does \(\psi\) push escaped transmission?
Answer \(D_0^* = \text{median} - \text{mode} \approx 2/(3\beta)\) \(\text{sign}(\partial R_{\text{eff}}/\partial\psi) = \text{sign}(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta))\)
Reference point Median of \(\tilde\varepsilon\)  (\(\approx 2/(3\beta)\)) Mean of \(\tilde\varepsilon\)  (\(= 1/\beta\))
Exactness Exact (for step-function \(\tilde{S}\)) Second-order approximation (for smooth \(\tilde{S}\))

18. Why the median is exact for step functions

For a step function \(\tilde{S}(t) = \mathbf{1}[t < D_0]\), the escaped transmission is simply the CDF:

$$\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)] = P(\tilde\varepsilon < D_0) = \tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)$$

The crossover requires \(\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)\) to be independent of \(\psi\). By definition, the median is the unique point where \(\tilde{F}_\psi = \tfrac{1}{2}\) for any distribution. So if the median–mode gap is approximately \(\psi\)-independent (as it is for the Gamma), then \(\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0^*) \approx \frac{1}{2}\) for all \(\psi\), and the crossover is exact.

By contrast, the mean does not split the distribution 50-50. For a right-skewed distribution like the Gamma:

$$P(\tilde\varepsilon > 1/\beta) = P\!\bigl(\text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta) > \text{mean}\bigr)$$

ranges from \(e^{-1} \approx 0.368\) (exponential, \(\psi\alpha = 1\)) to \(0.5\) (Gaussian limit, \(\psi\alpha \to \infty\)). The CDF at the mean is not \(\psi\)-independent — so \(D_0 = 1/\beta\) is not the exact crossover for step-function detection.

19. Why the mean appears in the Taylor expansion

The current result uses the cumulant expansion of \(\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)]\) around the mean of \(\tilde\varepsilon\):

$$\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)] = \tilde{S}(\mu) + \underbrace{\tilde{S}'(\mu) \cdot 0}_{\text{1st central moment}} + \frac{1}{2}\tilde{S}''(\mu)\,\sigma^2 + \frac{1}{6}\tilde{S}'''(\mu)\,\kappa_3 + \cdots$$

where \(\mu = 1/\beta\), \(\sigma^2 = \psi\alpha/\beta^2\), and \(\kappa_3 = 2\psi\alpha/\beta^3\) is the third central moment. The mean is the natural expansion point because the first-order term vanishes, making the second-order (variance) term the leading \(\psi\)-dependent contribution.

Taking the \(\psi\)-derivative:

$$\frac{\partial}{\partial\psi}\,\mathbb{E}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)] = \frac{\alpha}{\beta^2}\left[\frac{1}{2}\,\tilde{S}''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right) + \frac{1}{3\beta}\,\tilde{S}'''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right) + \cdots\right]$$

The leading term gives the direction rule from Section 11: \(\text{sign} = \text{sign}(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta))\). But the third-order correction (weighted by the skewness \(\kappa_3/\sigma^3 = 2/\sqrt{\psi\alpha}\)) shifts the effective crossover.

20. The skewness correction: from mean to median

Setting the full \(\psi\)-derivative to zero (including the third-order term) gives the crossover condition:

$$\frac{1}{2}\,\tilde{S}''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right) + \frac{1}{3\beta}\,\tilde{S}'''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right) \approx 0$$

For a sigmoid-like \(\tilde{S}\) transitioning at some point \(D_0\), the second-order-only condition \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta) = 0\) gives \(D_0 = 1/\beta\). Including the third-order term shifts the solution to a smaller value. For a sharp sigmoid, the corrected crossover converges to:

$$D_0^* \;\to\; \frac{2}{3\beta} \qquad\text{(the median–mode gap)}$$

recovering the exact step-function result. The gap between the two reference points is:

$$\underbrace{\frac{1}{\beta}}_{\text{mean–mode}} - \underbrace{\frac{2}{3\beta}}_{\text{median–mode}} = \frac{1}{3\beta}$$

This is precisely one-third of the mean–mode gap, consistent with Pearson's classical relation:

$$\text{mean} - \text{mode} \approx 3\,(\text{mean} - \text{median})$$

which gives \(\text{median} - \text{mode} \approx \frac{2}{3}(\text{mean} - \text{mode})\).

21. Unified picture

The three reference points of the Gamma profile, in mode-relative coordinates:

$$\underbrace{0}_{\text{mode}} \quad<\quad \underbrace{\frac{2}{3\beta}}_{\substack{\text{median} \\ \text{exact crossover}}} \quad<\quad \underbrace{\frac{1}{\beta}}_{\substack{\text{mean} \\ \text{Jensen reference}}}$$

For any detection survival function \(\tilde{S}\):

  • Exact crossover (step-function \(\tilde{S}\)): punctuation helps detection iff \(D_0 < \frac{2}{3\beta}\). This is where \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > D_0) = \frac{1}{2}\), independent of \(\psi\).
  • Second-order direction rule (smooth \(\tilde{S}\)): punctuation helps detection iff \(\tilde{S}''\!\!\left(\frac{1}{\beta}\right) > 0\) (convex at the mean). This is a quick diagnostic that gets the direction right for smooth \(\tilde{S}\), but slightly overestimates the "neutral zone."
  • Third-order refinement: including the Gamma's right-skewness pulls the effective evaluation point from \(\frac{1}{\beta}\) toward \(\frac{2}{3\beta}\), sharpening the prediction.

Practical rule: For right-skewed infectiousness profiles (the biologically realistic case), the Jensen approximation at the mean is conservative — it predicts a slightly wider regime where punctuation helps detection than actually exists. The median gives the tighter, more accurate boundary. For our standard parameters (\(\beta = 2\)): the mean gives \(0.5\) days; the median gives \(0.33\) days; the true crossover is in between, closer to the median.

22. When does the distinction matter?

The gap between mean and median (\(1/(3\beta) = T/(3\alpha)\)) is small when \(\alpha\) is large (sharp generation interval). For our parameters (\(T = 5, \alpha = 10, \beta = 2\)):

$$\frac{1}{3\beta} = \frac{1}{6} \text{ day} \approx 4 \text{ hours}$$

So the mean–mode diagnostic (\(1/\beta = 0.5\) days) and the exact median–mode crossover (\(2/(3\beta) = 0.33\) days) differ by only 4 hours. For a smooth detection survival \(\tilde{S}\) that transitions over a time scale much longer than 4 hours, evaluating \(\tilde{S}''\) at either point gives essentially the same answer. The distinction only matters when \(\tilde{S}\) changes rapidly near the mode — i.e., when the detection process is sharp enough to be well-approximated by a step function, which is exactly when the median result becomes the right one to use.

In other words: the two reference points are complements, not competitors. Use the mean (\(1/\beta\)) for smooth \(\tilde{S}\) where the curvature diagnostic is natural. Use the median (\(2/(3\beta)\)) for sharp \(\tilde{S}\) where the step-function crossover is the right framework. They agree within \(T/(3\alpha)\) days.

Step functions vs smooth \(\tilde{S}\): which is the right framework?

2026-03-23

At the individual level, detection happens at a specific time — after which the person isolates (perfectly, for now). So the individual's "survival function" is literally a step function: \(S_i(\tau) = \mathbf{1}[\tau < \tau_{\det,i}]\). The smooth \(\tilde{S}\) is an average over the randomness in detection timing. Which framework should we use?

23. The two representations are mathematically equivalent

For individual \(i\) with mode-anchored detection time \(D_{0,i}\) (relative to their mode), the escaped transmission is:

$$J_i = R_0 \int_{\tilde\varepsilon < D_{0,i}} \tilde{f}_\psi(\tilde\varepsilon)\,d\tilde\varepsilon = R_0\,\tilde{F}_\psi(D_{0,i})$$

The population average over the randomness in \(D_0\) is:

$$\bar{J} = R_0\,\mathbb{E}_{D_0}\!\bigl[\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)\bigr] = R_0\int \tilde{F}_\psi(d)\,g(d)\,dd \qquad\qquad \textbf{[Step-function form]}$$

where \(g\) is the density of \(D_0\). Alternatively, defining the smooth survival function \(\tilde{S}(t) = P(D_0 > t) = 1 - G(t)\) and integrating against the infectiousness profile:

$$\bar{J} = R_0\,\mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon}\!\bigl[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)\bigr] = R_0\int \tilde{S}(t)\,\tilde{f}_\psi(t)\,dt \qquad\qquad \textbf{[Smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) form]}$$

These are identical by Fubini's theorem:

$$\mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon}[\tilde{S}(\tilde\varepsilon)] = \mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon}\!\bigl[P(D_0 > \tilde\varepsilon)\bigr] = \mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon}\!\bigl[\mathbb{E}_{D_0}[\mathbf{1}_{D_0 > \tilde\varepsilon}]\bigr] = \mathbb{E}_{D_0}\!\bigl[\mathbb{E}_{\tilde\varepsilon}[\mathbf{1}_{\tilde\varepsilon < D_0}]\bigr] = \mathbb{E}_{D_0}\!\bigl[\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)\bigr]$$

So the two representations always give the same numerical answer. The choice between them is about which source of randomness to average over first.

24. Why the step-function framework is more fundamental here

Although mathematically equivalent, the step-function / \(D_0\) representation has three advantages for understanding the \(\psi\)-effect:

24a. It separates the two sources of randomness

The escaped transmission depends on the interaction of two random variables:

  • \(\tilde\varepsilon \sim \tilde{f}_\psi\): when an individual transmits (relative to their mode). This is the biological source of randomness, controlled by \(\psi\).
  • \(D_0 \sim g\): when an individual is detected (relative to their mode). This is the detection source of randomness, independent of \(\psi\).

The step-function framework keeps these separate: \(\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)\) is the biological quantity, and averaging over \(g\) adds the detection noise. The smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) framework pre-averages over \(D_0\), collapsing the detection randomness into the shape of \(\tilde{S}\).

24b. The crossover is transparent

In the step-function framework, for each realisation of \(D_0\), there is a clean crossover at \(D_0^* = \text{median} - \text{mode} \approx 2/(3\beta)\): for \(D_0 < D_0^*\), spikes escape more; for \(D_0 > D_0^*\), smooth escapes more. The population-level \(\psi\)-effect is simply a matter of how much of the \(D_0\) distribution falls on each side:

$$\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{D_0}[\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)]}_{\text{escaped fraction}} \quad\text{favours spikes} \iff \text{most of } g(D_0) \text{ is to the left of } D_0^*$$

This is immediate and visual. The smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) framework gives the same answer via the curvature diagnostic \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta)\), but that's a less transparent encoding of the same fact.

24c. It naturally handles the "variance helps spikes" effect

The earlier analysis (Sections on "Random detection times") showed that noise in \(D_0\) shifts the effective crossover rightward:

$$D_0^{*,\text{eff}} \approx \frac{2}{3\beta} + c\,\sigma^2_{D_0}$$

This is a statement about the distribution of \(D_0\), not about the shape of \(\tilde{S}\). It says: even if the average detection time is at the nominal crossover, randomness in detection timing tips the balance toward spikes (because spikes benefit asymmetrically from lucky early detections). In the smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) framework, this result is buried in the curvature of \(\tilde{S}\) and harder to interpret.

25. When is the smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) framework useful?

The smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) representation is still valuable in specific situations:

  • Parametric convenience: If the detection process gives \(\tilde{S}\) a nice closed form (exponential, Weibull, etc.), the curvature diagnostic \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta)\) is a quick one-line computation.
  • Complex detection processes: When \(D_0\) doesn't have a simple distribution (e.g., it depends on multiple competing processes — symptom onset, scheduled tests, contact tracing — and isolation occurs at the first trigger), it may be easier to characterise the resultant \(\tilde{S}\) directly than to derive the distribution of \(D_0\).
  • Imperfect isolation: If isolation is not all-or-nothing (e.g., transmission is reduced but not eliminated), the individual response is no longer a step function. Then \(\tilde{S}\) becomes the natural framework, encoding both the timing and the degree of isolation.

26. Synthesis: the \(D_0\) framework as the "master" approach

For mode-anchored detection with perfect isolation:

The step-function / \(D_0\) framework is the more fundamental representation. The escaped transmission is:

$$R_{\text{eff}} = R_0\,\mathbb{E}_{D_0}\!\bigl[\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)\bigr]$$

The \(\psi\)-effect is determined by where the distribution of \(D_0\) sits relative to the crossover \(D_0^* \approx 2/(3\beta)\):

  • \(\mathbb{E}[D_0] \lesssim 2/(3\beta) + c\,\sigma^2_{D_0}\): spikes escape more (punctuation helps detection)
  • \(\mathbb{E}[D_0] \gtrsim 2/(3\beta) + c\,\sigma^2_{D_0}\): smooth escapes more (punctuation hurts detection)

The smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) representation (\(\tilde{S}(t) = P(D_0 > t)\)) gives the same answer via \(\tilde{S}''(1/\beta)\), and is useful as a computational shortcut or when detection is complex. But the conceptual content lives in the \(D_0\) framework: the median–mode gap is the crossover, and detection noise favours spikes.

The smooth-\(\tilde{S}\) framework becomes the natural one when we relax perfect isolation — then the individual response is no longer a step function and \(\tilde{S}\) genuinely describes a graded process.

Why the median? Clarifying the crossover logic

2026-03-23

27. The crossover requires two properties simultaneously

We seek \(D_0^*\) such that \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > D_0^*)\) is the same for all \(\psi\). This requires:

  1. A known value of the survival function. We need \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > D_0^*) = c\) for some constant \(c\), for all \(\psi\). The median has this by definition: \(P(X > \text{median}(X)) = \tfrac{1}{2}\) for any continuous distribution. No other summary statistic (mean, mode, etc.) has an analogous "by definition" property linking it to a fixed survival probability.
  2. A \(\psi\)-independent location. The median of \(\tilde\varepsilon\) must sit at (approximately) the same point for all \(\psi\). For the Gamma, the median–mode gap \(\approx 2/(3\beta)\) is approximately shape-independent. This is a specific property of the Gamma family, not a universal truth.

The mean–mode gap satisfies (2) — it is exactly \(1/\beta\) for all \(\psi\alpha\) — but fails (1). At the mean, \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > 1/\beta) = P(\text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta) > \text{mean})\) ranges from \(e^{-1} \approx 0.368\) (exponential) to \(0.5\) (Gaussian limit). The mean is at a fixed location, but the survival function takes different values there for different \(\psi\), so it is not a crossover.

28. The median is the unique such quantile for the Gamma

Could some quantile other than the median have a \(\psi\)-independent mode-gap? In principle, any quantile \(Q_p\) satisfying \(P(\tilde\varepsilon > Q_p - m_\psi) = 1 - p\) would give a crossover at level \(1 - p\), provided \(Q_p - m_\psi\) is \(\psi\)-independent. But for the Gamma, the median is the only quantile with this property.

The Wilson–Hilferty approximation gives the \(p\)-th quantile of \(\text{Gamma}(a, 1)\) as:

$$Q_p(a) \;\approx\; a - \tfrac{1}{3} + z_p\sqrt{a} + \tfrac{z_p^2}{3} + O(a^{-1/2})$$

where \(z_p = \Phi^{-1}(p)\) is the standard normal quantile. Subtracting the mode \((a - 1)\):

$$Q_p - \text{mode} \;\approx\; \frac{2}{3} + z_p\sqrt{a} + \frac{z_p^2}{3}$$

The \(\sqrt{a}\) term makes this strongly \(a\)-dependent for any \(z_p \neq 0\). The unique solution to \(z_p = 0\) is \(p = 1/2\): the median. So the median is singled out as the only Gamma quantile whose mode-gap is approximately shape-independent.

29. Generality beyond the Gamma

For a general unimodal family \(\{f_\psi\}\), the existence of a clean crossover is not guaranteed. The earlier log-normal counterexample (Section on "Counterexample: the log-normal has no clean crossover") showed that the median–mode gap can vary by nearly \(3\times\) across shapes, so no single \(D_0^*\) exists.

A rough sufficient condition for the median to work: the family should have (a) a \(\psi\)-independent mean–mode gap and (b) Pearson's relation \(\text{mean} - \text{mode} \approx 3(\text{mean} - \text{median})\) holding reasonably well. Together these give median \(- \text{ mode} \approx \frac{2}{3}(\text{mean} - \text{mode}) = \text{const}\). The Gamma satisfies both; the log-normal violates (b) at low shape parameters.

When no clean crossover exists, one falls back on the general \(D_0\) framework: study \(\mathbb{E}_{D_0}[\tilde{F}_\psi(D_0)]\) and its \(\psi\)-dependence directly, without relying on a universal crossover point.

Pairwise comparison of two profiles

2026-03-23

Instead of seeking a universal crossover that works for all \(\psi\) simultaneously, compare two specific profiles with shapes \(a_1 = \psi_1\alpha\) and \(a_2 = \psi_2\alpha\) (with \(a_1 < a_2\), so \(\psi_1\) is more punctuated). This relaxes the demanding requirement that the median–mode gap be \(\psi\)-independent, and works for any distribution family.

30. The pairwise crossover always exists

For any two continuous unimodal profiles centred at their modes, the mode-centred survival functions \(\tilde{S}_1(D_0)\) and \(\tilde{S}_2(D_0)\) both satisfy \(\tilde{S}_i(D_0) \to 1\) as \(D_0 \to -\infty\) and \(\tilde{S}_i(D_0) \to 0\) as \(D_0 \to +\infty\). Since the more punctuated profile has more supra-modal mass (i.e., \(\tilde{S}_1(0) > \tilde{S}_2(0)\)) but thinner tails (\(\tilde{S}_1(D_0) < \tilde{S}_2(D_0)\) for large \(D_0\)), the difference \(\Delta(D_0) = \tilde{S}_1(D_0) - \tilde{S}_2(D_0)\) must cross zero at least once. By the intermediate value theorem, a pairwise crossover \(D_0^*\) exists for any pair of unimodal profiles, regardless of the distribution family.

This is weaker than (and doesn't require) the universal crossover. The log-normal has no universal crossover, but any two log-normal profiles still have a pairwise one.

31. Normal approximation for the pairwise difference

The Wilson–Hilferty approximation gives the mode-centred Gamma survival as:

$$\tilde{S}(D_0;\, a) \;\approx\; \Phi\!\left(\frac{2/(3\beta) - D_0}{\sqrt{a}/\beta}\right) = \Phi\!\left(\frac{D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0}{\sigma_a}\right)$$

where \(D_0^{\text{univ}} = 2/(3\beta)\) is the universal crossover and \(\sigma_a = \sqrt{a}/\beta\) is the "detection-relevant scale" of the profile. In this approximation, the mode-centred survival is a probit (normal CDF) centred at \(D_0^{\text{univ}}\) with scale \(\sigma_a\).

The pairwise difference is therefore:

$$\Delta(D_0) = \Phi\!\left(\frac{D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0}{\sigma_{a_1}}\right) - \Phi\!\left(\frac{D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0}{\sigma_{a_2}}\right)$$

Since \(a_1 < a_2\), we have \(\sigma_{a_1} < \sigma_{a_2}\): the more punctuated profile has a steeper probit (sharper transition from "all escapes" to "nothing escapes").

32. The factorisation near the crossover

Near the crossover (\(D_0 \approx D_0^{\text{univ}}\)), both probit arguments are near zero. Linearising \(\Phi\) around 0:

$$\Delta(D_0) \;\approx\; \varphi(0)\left(\frac{D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0}{\sigma_{a_1}} - \frac{D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0}{\sigma_{a_2}}\right) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\,(D_0^{\text{univ}} - D_0)\!\left(\frac{1}{\sigma_{a_1}} - \frac{1}{\sigma_{a_2}}\right)$$

Substituting \(\sigma_a = \sqrt{a}/\beta\):

$$\boxed{\;\Delta(D_0) \;\approx\; \frac{\beta}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{a_1}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{a_2}}\right)\!\left(\frac{2}{3\beta} - D_0\right)\;}$$

This is the key result. The pairwise difference factorises into:

  1. A biological sensitivity factor \(\;\displaystyle\frac{1}{\sqrt{a_1}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{a_2}} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_1\alpha}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_2\alpha}}\;\) that depends only on the two profiles being compared.
  2. A detection offset factor \(\;2/(3\beta) - D_0\;\) that depends only on the detection timing.

The sign is determined by the detection offset factor: \(\Delta > 0\) (punctuation has more supra-\(D_0\) mass, i.e., detection averts more from the punctuated profile) when \(D_0 < 2/(3\beta)\), and vice versa. The crossover is at \(D_0^* = 2/(3\beta)\) to this order, recovering the universal result.

33. Population-level effect: biology × detection

For a random detection time \(D_0 \sim g\), the population-level difference in escaped fraction between profiles \(\psi_1\) and \(\psi_2\) is:

$$\mathbb{E}_{D_0}[\Delta(D_0)] \;\approx\; \frac{\beta}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_1\alpha}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_2\alpha}}\right)\!\left(\frac{2}{3\beta} - \mathbb{E}[D_0]\right)$$

Factorisation theorem (leading order). The difference in escaped transmission between two punctuation levels factorises as:

$$R_{\text{eff}}(\psi_1) - R_{\text{eff}}(\psi_2) \;\approx\; R_0 \cdot \underbrace{\frac{\beta}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\!\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_1\alpha}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{\psi_2\alpha}}\right)}_{\text{biological sensitivity}} \cdot \underbrace{\left(\frac{2}{3\beta} - \mathbb{E}[D_0]\right)}_{\text{detection gap}}$$

where \(\mathbb{E}[D_0]\) is the mean detection time relative to the mode.

Interpretation:

  • The biological sensitivity is always positive (since \(a_1 < a_2 \Rightarrow 1/\sqrt{a_1} > 1/\sqrt{a_2}\)). It measures how different the two profiles are in their "detection-relevant sharpness." It scales as \(1/\sqrt{a}\), so most of the sensitivity is between low shape parameters (spike-like profiles); the difference between \(a = 50\) and \(a = 100\) is tiny.
  • The detection gap \(2/(3\beta) - \mathbb{E}[D_0]\) is the signed distance from the mean detection time to the crossover. Positive means detection happens before the crossover (punctuation helps); negative means after (punctuation hurts).
  • The product determines which profile escapes more. If the detection gap is positive, the more punctuated profile (\(\psi_1\)) escapes more — it has more supra-\(D_0\) mass — so more is averted from it, meaning punctuation helps detection.

34. Large shape parameters: proportionality to \(\psi_2 - \psi_1\)

For large shape parameters (\(a_1, a_2 \gg 1\)), the biological sensitivity simplifies. Writing \(\bar{a} = \sqrt{a_1 a_2}\) (geometric mean):

$$\frac{1}{\sqrt{a_1}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{a_2}} = \frac{\sqrt{a_2} - \sqrt{a_1}}{\bar{a}} \;\approx\; \frac{a_2 - a_1}{2\bar{a}^{3/2}} = \frac{(\psi_2 - \psi_1)\alpha}{2(\psi_1\psi_2)^{3/4}\alpha^{3/2}}$$

So for two profiles that are both moderately concentrated (large \(a\)):

$$\text{biological sensitivity} \;\propto\; \psi_2 - \psi_1$$

The difference in escaped transmission is approximately linear in the punctuation difference \(\psi_2 - \psi_1\). This breaks down for small \(a\) (spike-like profiles), where the \(1/\sqrt{a}\) nonlinearity matters — most of the sensitivity is concentrated in the spike end of the spectrum.

35. The pairwise crossover location

The leading-order factorisation places the pairwise crossover at \(D_0^{\text{univ}} = 2/(3\beta)\), the same as the universal crossover. At the next order (including the correction from the Wilson–Hilferty expansion), the pairwise crossover shifts slightly:

$$D_0^*(a_1, a_2) \;\approx\; \frac{2}{3\beta} + \frac{1}{\beta}\cdot\frac{a_1 + \sqrt{a_1 a_2} + a_2}{27\,a_1\,a_2}$$

The correction is \(O(1/(\beta a_1 a_2))\) and is always positive (the pairwise crossover is slightly to the right of the universal one). Numerically, for our standard parameters (\(\alpha = 10, \beta = 2\)):

Pair \((a_1, a_2)\) Formula Numerical \(D_0^*\)
\((1, 10)\)0.3600.351
\((1, 5)\)0.3650.353
\((3, 10)\)0.3460.340
\((5, 10)\)0.3420.338
Universal0.333—

The formula captures the right trend (pairwise crossovers are above the universal 0.333, and closer to it when both shapes are large), though it slightly overestimates for small \(a_1\) where the normal approximation is less accurate.

36. Summary: advantages of the pairwise framework

Comparing two specific profiles \(\psi_1\) and \(\psi_2\) instead of all \(\psi\) at once yields three simplifications:

  1. Existence is guaranteed: A pairwise crossover exists for any two unimodal profiles (by IVT), with no distributional assumptions.
  2. Factorisation: The population-level \(\psi\)-effect separates into (biology) \(\times\) (detection), allowing independent analysis of the infectiousness profile and the detection mechanism.
  3. Linearity in \(\Delta\psi\): For moderately concentrated profiles, the effect is approximately proportional to \(\psi_2 - \psi_1\), with a coefficient that depends on the detection mechanism through the single number \(\mathbb{E}[D_0] - 2/(3\beta)\).

The universal crossover (\(D_0^* = 2/(3\beta)\) for all \(\psi\)) is a special feature of the Gamma family. The pairwise crossover is more general and more practical: it asks "for this specific comparison, when does punctuation matter?" rather than "for all comparisons simultaneously."

Domain of validity: breakdown for very spiky profiles

2026-03-23

37. The spike limit

As \(a = \psi\alpha \to 0\), the individual profile \(\text{Gamma}(a, \beta)\) concentrates all its mass at 0 (which is the mode for \(a \leq 1\)). The mode-centred survival becomes a step function:

$$\tilde{S}_{\text{spike}}(D_0) = P\!\bigl(\text{Gamma}(a, \beta) > D_0\bigr) \;\xrightarrow{a \to 0}\; \mathbf{1}[D_0 \leq 0]$$

For any \(D_0 > 0\), the spike's survival is essentially zero — all transmission has already fired. Meanwhile, a smooth profile (say \(a_2 = 10\)) still has \(\tilde{S}_2(0) \approx 0.571\) of its mass above the mode. So at \(D_0 = 0\):

$$\Delta(0) = \underbrace{\tilde{S}_1(0)}_{\to\, 1} - \underbrace{\tilde{S}_2(0)}_{\approx\, 0.571} \;\approx\; 0.43$$

while at any \(D_0 > 0\):

$$\Delta(D_0) = \underbrace{\tilde{S}_1(D_0)}_{\to\, 0} - \underbrace{\tilde{S}_2(D_0)}_{> 0} \;<\; 0$$

The pairwise crossover is forced to \(D_0^* \to 0\) (the mode itself), not \(2/(3\beta)\). In the spike limit, the crossover collapses to the mode because the spike has no spread at all — there is no "tail" to place on either side.

38. Where the approximation breaks

The Wilson–Hilferty / normal approximation treats the Gamma as approximately Gaussian, which requires the shape parameter \(a\) to be at least moderately large. For \(a < 1\), the Gamma has a pole at 0 (density \(\to \infty\)) and is nothing like a Gaussian. The 2/(3\beta) crossover is a moderate-to-large \(a\) result.

Empirically, the approximation works surprisingly well down to \(a = 1\) (exponential distribution). The earlier numerical results show:

\(a_1\) (vs \(a_2 = 10\)) Pairwise \(D_0^*\) Relative error vs \(2/(3\beta)\)
50.3381.5%
30.3402.1%
10.3515.4%
\(\to 0\)\(\to 0\)\(\to 100\%\)

So the approximation degrades gracefully: it is excellent for \(a \geq 3\), reasonable for \(a = 1\), and fails qualitatively only for \(a \ll 1\). In our parameterisation with \(\alpha = 10\), this means the 2/(3\beta) result is reliable for \(\psi \gtrsim 0.1\) and breaks for \(\psi \ll 0.1\).

39. The qualitative picture across the full range

The pairwise crossover \(D_0^*(a_1, a_2)\) as \(a_1\) varies (with \(a_2\) fixed at a moderate value) behaves as:

  • \(a_1 \to 0\) (spike): \(D_0^* \to 0\). The spike puts everything at the mode; any detection after the mode catches all of the spike's transmission but only a fraction of the smooth's. Punctuation always helps for any \(D_0 > 0\).
  • \(a_1\) moderate (\(\gtrsim 1\)): \(D_0^* \approx 2/(3\beta)\). The normal approximation holds and the universal crossover is accurate. The factorisation theorem (Section 33) applies.
  • \(a_1 \to a_2\): \(D_0^*\) is undefined (the profiles are identical; no crossover exists). The difference \(\Delta(D_0) \to 0\) uniformly.

Summary: The \(2/(3\beta)\) crossover is a good approximation when both shape parameters are at least \(\sim 1\). For very spiky profiles (\(a \ll 1\)), the crossover moves toward 0, and the spike regime is even more favourable for detection than the \(2/(3\beta)\) result suggests — the approximation is conservative. The qualitative conclusion (punctuation helps detection when \(D_0\) is small) is robust across the full range; only the precise crossover location shifts.

Exact monotonicity analysis: when does punctuation help or hurt?

2026-03-23

40. Setup: exact analysis without approximations

Define the mode-centred survival function for a Gamma(\(a, \beta\)) profile:

$$h(a, t) \;=\; P\!\bigl(\text{Gamma}(a, 1) > (a - 1) + t\bigr), \qquad a \geq 1$$

where \(t = \beta D_0\) is the detection delay (measured from the mode) in units of \(1/\beta\). The shape parameter \(a = \psi\alpha\) controls punctuation: small \(a\) = spiky, large \(a\) = smooth.

The question: is \(h(a, t)\) monotone in \(a\)? If \(h\) is decreasing in \(a\), then reducing punctuation (increasing \(a\)) always reduces escaped transmission — "punctuation helps." If \(h\) is increasing in \(a\), punctuation hurts.

41. Three regimes

Exact numerical computation (no approximations, using the full Gamma CDF) reveals three clean regimes:

Theorem (exact, for \(a \geq 1\)). Let \(h(a, t) = P(\text{Gamma}(a,1) > a - 1 + t)\). Then:

  1. \(t < 2/3\): \(h\) is strictly decreasing in \(a\). Punctuation always helps.
  2. \(2/3 < t < t^*\): \(h\) is non-monotone — it first decreases, reaches an interior minimum at some \(a_{\min}(t)\), then increases. Intermediate punctuation is worst.
  3. \(t > t^*\): \(h\) is strictly increasing in \(a\). Punctuation always hurts.

where \(t^* \approx 0.736\) (in units of \(1/\beta\)), so in real time: \(D_0^{**} = t^*/\beta\).

The lower boundary is exactly \(t = 2/3\), which corresponds to the universal crossover \(D_0^* = 2/(3\beta)\) from the median-mode gap theory (Sections 27–29). This is not a coincidence: at \(t = 2/3\), \(h(a, 2/3) \to 1/2\) for all large \(a\), and the convergence is from above — so \(h\) is strictly decreasing but the limit is the same for all \(a\). Pushing \(t\) just above \(2/3\) causes the curves for different \(a\) to cross.

42. The non-monotone band: structure and magnitude

In the non-monotone band \(t \in (2/3,\; 0.736)\), the interior minimum migrates:

\(t\) \(a_{\min}\) \(h(a_{\min})\) \(h(1)\) (exponential) Dip from \(h(1)\)
0.6776.20.49890.50830.94 pp
0.6902.80.49610.50160.55 pp
\(\ln 2 \approx 0.693\)2.50.49520.50000.48 pp
0.7101.60.48990.49160.17 pp
0.7201.30.48610.48680.07 pp
0.7301.10.48180.48190.01 pp

As \(t \to 2/3\) from above, the minimum moves to large \(a\) and the dip vanishes. As \(t \to t^*\), the minimum collapses to \(a = 1\) and the dip also vanishes. The maximum dip is less than 1 percentage point and occurs at \(t \approx 0.68\) with \(a_{\min} \approx 6\).

43. Special role of \(\ln 2\) and the exponential distribution

The value \(t = \ln 2\) (i.e., \(D_0 = \ln 2/\beta\)) has a special meaning: when \(a = 1\), the Gamma reduces to the Exponential distribution, and:

$$h(1, t) = e^{-t} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad h(1, \ln 2) = \tfrac{1}{2}$$

So \(D_0 = \ln 2/\beta\) is the median of the exponential distribution (measured from its mode at 0). The fact that \(\ln 2 \approx 0.693\) sits inside the non-monotone band \((0.667,\; 0.736)\) means that detection at the exponential's median exhibits non-monotone behaviour — the exponential is not the worst shape, despite having \(h = 1/2\).

Compare the two key values of \(t\):

Value \(t\) Meaning
\(2/3\)0.6667Median–mode gap (large \(a\) limit); lower boundary of non-monotone band
\(\ln 2\)0.6931Median of the exponential (\(a = 1\)); inside non-monotone band
\(t^*\)0.736Upper boundary of non-monotone band

44. Why the band is narrow: a heuristic

The non-monotone band has width \(t^* - 2/3 \approx 0.07\) in units of \(1/\beta\). With our default parameters (\(\beta = 2\), i.e. \(T = 5\) days, \(\alpha = 10\)), this corresponds to a real-time window of just \(0.07/2 \approx 0.035\) days — about 50 minutes.

The narrowness has an intuitive explanation. The universal crossover at \(D_0 = 2/(3\beta)\) works because the median–mode gap of the Gamma is nearly \(\psi\)-independent (Wilson–Hilferty, Section 28). The gap is not exactly constant — it has residual \(\psi\)-dependence of order \(O(1/a)\) from the skewness correction. The non-monotone band is precisely the range where this small \(O(1/a)\) correction is large enough to reverse the dominant trend. The band is narrow because the correction is small.

45. Practical interpretation

For practical purposes, the monotone picture is essentially correct:

  • Detection before the mode + \(2/(3\beta)\): punctuation helps (monotonically).
  • Detection after the mode + \(2/(3\beta)\): punctuation hurts (monotonically, with a tiny non-monotone correction in a band of width \(\sim 0.07/\beta\), amounting to < 1 percentage point).

The non-monotone band is:

  • Narrow — width \(\approx 0.07/\beta\) in real time
  • Shallow — the dip is at most ~1 percentage point
  • Located at the crossover — precisely where the effect of punctuation changes sign, so the escaped transmission is near \(R_0/2\) anyway

The qualitative story — punctuation helps fast detection, hurts slow detection, with a crossover near the median–mode gap — is exact in the monotone regions and accurate to within 1 pp in the non-monotone band.

46. Full picture including \(a < 1\)

For \(a < 1\) (very spiky profiles), the Gamma mode is 0 and the density has a pole at 0. In this regime:

$$h(a, t) = P(\text{Gamma}(a, 1) > t), \qquad a < 1$$

This is strictly increasing in \(a\) (a heavier-tailed distribution has more probability above any fixed threshold). So for \(a \in (0, 1)\), \(h\) is always increasing. Combined with the \(a \geq 1\) analysis:

  • For \(t < 2/3\): \(h\) increases on \((0,1)\), reaches maximum at \(a = 1\) (where \(h(1,t) = e^{-t}\)), then decreases for \(a > 1\). The function \(a = 1\) is a global maximum.
  • For \(2/3 < t < t^*\): \(h\) increases on \((0,1)\), then decreases to an interior minimum, then increases again. But the overall trend is still from low (\(a \ll 1\)) to \(\sim 0.5\) (\(a \to \infty\)).
  • For \(t > t^*\): \(h\) is increasing for all \(a > 0\). Punctuation always hurts, even in the extreme spike regime.

However, the \(a < 1\) regime (\(\psi < 1/\alpha\)) represents profiles so spiky that they are essentially delta functions. In the epidemiological context, this is the limit of "superspreading events" where all of a person's infectiousness is concentrated in a window shorter than \(1/\beta = T/\alpha\). For our default parameters (\(T = 5\), \(\alpha = 10\)), this means \(\psi < 0.1\), corresponding to infectious windows of less than half a day. This regime is of limited practical relevance for most respiratory pathogens.

Lognormal infectiousness profiles: comparison with Gamma

2026-03-23

47. Setup: lognormal analogue

Consider a lognormal infectiousness profile \(X \sim \text{Lognormal}(\mu, \sigma^2)\) in place of the Gamma. The key statistics are:

$$\text{mode} = e^{\mu - \sigma^2}, \qquad \text{median} = e^{\mu}, \qquad \text{mean} = e^{\mu + \sigma^2/2}$$

The parameter \(\sigma\) controls punctuation: larger \(\sigma\) = spikier peak and heavier tail (analogous to smaller \(a\) in the Gamma). The mode-centred prevented fraction is:

$$h(\sigma, t) = P(X > \text{mode} + t)$$

To make a fair comparison with the Gamma, we impose the same constraint: mean − mode = 1 (the analogue of the Gamma's constant \(1/\beta\)). This requires:

$$e^{\mu}\!\left(e^{\sigma^2/2} - e^{-\sigma^2}\right) = 1 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \mu(\sigma) = -\ln\!\left(e^{\sigma^2/2} - e^{-\sigma^2}\right)$$

48. The critical difference: median–mode gap stability

The entire Gamma monotonicity story (Sections 40–46) rested on one fact: the median–mode gap is nearly constant in the shape parameter \(a\). For the lognormal, compute the gap under the mean − mode = 1 constraint:

$$\text{median} - \text{mode} = e^{\mu}\!\left(1 - e^{-\sigma^2}\right) = \frac{1 - e^{-\sigma^2}}{e^{\sigma^2/2} - e^{-\sigma^2}}$$

This function starts at \(2/3\) for small \(\sigma\) and decreases monotonically toward 0:

Median − mode gap
Distribution CV = 0.1 CV = 0.3 CV = 0.5 CV = 1.0 Range
Gamma 0.667 0.671 0.671 0.693 0.026
Lognormal 0.665 0.652 0.624 0.493 0.667

The Gamma's median–mode gap varies by only 0.026 across its entire range. The lognormal's varies by 0.667 — a 25× larger variation. This is the root cause of the structural difference.

49. Monotonicity results for the lognormal

Exact numerical computation of \(h(\sigma, t)\) for the constrained lognormal yields:

Theorem (lognormal, mean − mode = 1).

  1. \(t \geq 2/3\): \(h\) is monotonically decreasing in \(\sigma\). More punctuation ⇒ less prevention ⇒ punctuation always hurts.
  2. \(t < 2/3\): \(h\) is non-monotone in \(\sigma\): it first increases (punctuation helps for moderate \(\sigma\)), reaches a maximum at some \(\sigma_{\max}(t)\), then decreases (extreme punctuation hurts).

There is no region where punctuation monotonically helps across all \(\sigma\).

Compare with the Gamma (Section 41):

Detection delay \(t\) Gamma (\(a \geq 1\)) Lognormal
\(t < 2/3\) Monotone (punctuation helps) Non-monotone (helps then hurts)
\(2/3 < t < t^*\) Non-monotone (narrow band) Monotone (punctuation hurts)
\(t > t^*\) Monotone (punctuation hurts)

The structures are almost mirror images. The Gamma has a clean "punctuation helps" zone; the lognormal does not. The lognormal has a clean "punctuation hurts" zone for all \(t > 2/3\); the Gamma needs to get past a narrow non-monotone band before reaching its "punctuation hurts" zone.

50. Why the reversal?

The lognormal's heavy right tail is the culprit. As \(\sigma\) grows:

  • The mode concentrates near 0 (like the Gamma spike), putting most infectious mass close to the mode.
  • But the lognormal tail remains heavy: \(P(X > x) \sim x^{-1}\) for large \(x\), compared to the Gamma's exponential decay \(P(X > x) \sim e^{-x}\).

For the Gamma spike (\(a \to 0\)): all mass concentrates at the mode. Any detection after the mode catches everything. So the prevented fraction \(h \to 0\) for any \(t > 0\), and the spike is the easiest to detect.

For the lognormal "spike" (large \(\sigma\)): the mode concentrates near 0, but the heavy tail keeps substantial mass far from the mode. Detection at mode + \(t\) catches the mass near the mode but misses the tail. As \(\sigma\) grows, the tail becomes heavier, and more infectiousness escapes — eventually overwhelming the benefit of concentrating mass near the mode.

Formally: the lognormal median–mode gap shrinks as \(\sigma\) grows, meaning the individual crossover point moves closer to the mode. For large enough \(\sigma\), a fixed detection delay \(t\) that was previously on the "punctuation helps" side of the crossover finds itself on the "hurts" side.

51. The value \(2/3\) is still special

Despite the structural difference, the boundary \(t = 2/3\) appears in both distributions. Why?

In the smooth limit (small \(\sigma\), large \(a\)), any unimodal distribution with mean − mode = 1 approaches a normal shape. The normal approximation gives:

$$\text{mean} - \text{mode} \approx 3(\text{mean} - \text{median})$$

so median − mode \(\approx 2/3 \times\) (mean − mode) = \(2/3\). This is the Pearson relation, valid for all mildly skewed distributions. Since 2/3 is the shared limiting median–mode gap, it's the shared transition point in the smooth limit.

The difference is in what happens away from the smooth limit:

  • Gamma: The median–mode gap barely changes (it actually increases slightly, from 2/3 to ln 2 ≈ 0.693). So the transition region hardly moves.
  • Lognormal: The gap collapses toward 0. The transition region sweeps through, destroying the "punctuation helps" monotonicity.

52. What makes the Gamma special?

The Gamma's remarkable stability of the median–mode gap comes from the Wilson–Hilferty approximation (Section 28). For \(\text{Gamma}(a, 1)\):

$$\text{median} - \text{mode} \approx \frac{2}{3} + \frac{z_{1/2}^2}{3} + z_{1/2}\sqrt{a} + \cdots$$

Since \(z_{1/2} = 0\) (the median is the \(p = 1/2\) quantile and \(\Phi^{-1}(1/2) = 0\)), all \(\sqrt{a}\)-dependent terms vanish, leaving:

$$\text{median} - \text{mode} = \frac{2}{3} + O(1/a)$$

The leading correction is only \(O(1/a)\), so the gap is nearly constant for all \(a \gtrsim 1\). No other standard distribution family shares this combination of:

  1. a one-parameter shape family (indexed by \(a\)) with mean − mode independent of \(a\),
  2. a cube-root normalisation (Wilson–Hilferty) that kills the \(\sqrt{a}\)-dependence in the median.

Summary: The lognormal does not have a "punctuation always helps" region. For \(t < 2/3\), moderate punctuation helps but extreme punctuation hurts (due to the heavy tail). For \(t \geq 2/3\), punctuation always hurts. The clean monotonicity result from the Gamma is a consequence of the Gamma's uniquely stable median–mode gap and cannot be expected to hold for other distribution families.

Corrected full picture: the three regimes for all \(a > 0\)

2026-03-23

53. Including \(a < 1\) and detection before the mode

Sections 40–46 focused on \(a \geq 1\) (i.e., \(\psi \geq 1/\alpha\)), where the Gamma mode exists at \(a - 1 > 0\). But two extensions matter:

  • Very spiky profiles (\(a < 1\)): The Gamma mode is at 0, the density has a pole at 0, and the profile is extremely concentrated near the origin.
  • Negative \(t\): Detection before the mode (\(D_0 < 0\)). This is natural if, for example, an infected person is detected through contact tracing before reaching peak infectiousness.

Recall the definition:

$$h(a, t) = P\!\bigl(\text{Gamma}(a, 1) > \max(a-1, 0) + t\bigr)$$

For \(a < 1\): \(h(a, t) = P(\text{Gamma}(a, 1) > t)\), which is strictly increasing in \(a\) (the distribution acquires heavier tails as \(a \to 1\)).

For \(a \geq 1\): \(h(a, t) = P(\text{Gamma}(a, 1) > a - 1 + t)\), with the monotonicity depending on \(t\) as analysed in Section 41.

54. The corrected three-regime theorem

Exact computation over all \(a > 0\) and all \(t \in \mathbb{R}\) gives:

Theorem (full Gamma, all \(a > 0\)).

  1. \(t \leq 0\) (detection at or before the mode): \(h(a, t)\) is monotonically non-increasing in \(a\). Punctuation always helps. For \(t < 0\), the monotonicity is strict.
  2. \(0 < t < t^* \approx 0.735\) (detection shortly after the mode): \(h(a, t)\) is non-monotone in \(a\). It increases on \((0, 1)\) (extreme spikes get worse), peaks near \(a = 1\), then eventually converges to \(\sim 1/2\).
  3. \(t > t^* \approx 0.735\) (detection well after the mode): \(h(a, t)\) is monotonically increasing in \(a\). Punctuation always hurts.

This corrects the earlier Section 41, which restricted to \(a \geq 1\) and found a "punctuation always helps" region for \(t < 2/3\). Including \(a < 1\) widens the non-monotone band from \((2/3,\; 0.736)\) to \((0,\; 0.735)\).

55. Structure of the non-monotone region

The non-monotone band \(0 < t < t^*\) has two sub-regions with different character:

Sub-region Behaviour for \(a < 1\) Behaviour for \(a \geq 1\) Source of non-monotonicity
\(0 < t < 2/3\) \(h\) increasing \(h\) monotonically decreasing The \(a < 1\) spike regime only
\(2/3 < t < 0.735\) \(h\) increasing \(h\) non-monotone (interior min) Both \(a < 1\) and the \(a \geq 1\) interior minimum

For \(0 < t < 2/3\), the non-monotonicity is entirely due to the extreme-spike regime \(a < 1\). Within the practically relevant range \(a \geq 1\), punctuation still monotonically helps. The sub-region \(2/3 < t < 0.735\) has genuine non-monotonicity even within \(a \geq 1\), but the dip is at most ~1 pp (Section 42).

56. Interpretation: the practical story

The three regimes have a clean epidemiological interpretation:

  1. Detection before the mode (\(t < 0\)): punctuation always helps.
    If detection (e.g., contact tracing, pre-symptomatic testing) occurs before the peak of infectiousness, more concentrated profiles are always easier to contain. The spike hasn't fired yet, and a more concentrated spike means more of the total infectiousness still lies in the future. This holds for all \(a > 0\) without exception.

  2. Detection shortly after the mode (\(0 < t \lesssim 2/(3\beta)\)): punctuation mostly helps.
    For profiles with \(a \geq 1\) (the epidemiologically relevant range for most pathogens), punctuation still monotonically helps. The only exception is the extreme-spike regime \(a < 1\), where the profile is so concentrated that it has already transmitted everything by the time detection occurs. In practice, this represents infectious windows shorter than \(1/\beta = T/\alpha\) and is rarely relevant.

  3. Detection well after the mode (\(t \gtrsim 0.735/\beta\)): punctuation never helps.
    Once detection falls sufficiently far past the peak, more concentrated profiles have already fired their entire payload. The smooth profile, with its lingering tail, still has something left to prevent. Punctuation unambiguously hurts.

The crossover between regimes 2 and 3 is at \(t^* \approx 0.735/\beta\). In real time with \(\beta = 2\): \(D_0^{**} \approx 0.37\) days ≈ 9 hours after the peak. The earlier \(2/(3\beta) \approx 0.33\) days (8 hours) crossover from the \(a \geq 1\) analysis is contained within this, separated by only about one hour.

57. Revised comparison: Gamma vs Lognormal

With the full picture, the Gamma and lognormal are actually more similar than the \(a \geq 1\) analysis suggested:

Detection delay \(t\) Gamma (all \(a > 0\)) Lognormal (all \(\sigma > 0\))
\(t \leq 0\) Monotone (punctuation helps) Monotone (punctuation helps)
\(0 < t < 2/3\) Non-monotone (extreme spikes only) Non-monotone (helps then hurts)
\(t \geq 2/3\) Non-monotone for \(t < 0.735\), then monotone hurts Monotone (punctuation hurts)

Both distributions share the \(t \leq 0\): punctuation always helps result. Both become non-monotone for \(t > 0\). The difference is quantitative: the Gamma's non-monotonicity for \(0 < t < 2/3\) is confined to the extreme-spike tail (\(a < 1\)), while the lognormal's non-monotonicity extends into moderate shapes. This traces back to the Gamma's uniquely stable median–mode gap.

Universality of "punctuation helps before the mode"

2026-03-23

58. The question

Section 54 showed that for the Gamma, \(h(a, t)\) is monotonically non-increasing in \(a\) for all \(t \leq 0\): punctuation always helps when detection occurs at or before the mode. Sections 47–52 showed the same holds for the lognormal. Is this universal for all unimodal distributions?

59. No: a counterexample

Consider the asymmetric double-exponential family, with mode at 0:

$$f_c(x) = \frac{c}{c+1} \begin{cases} e^{x} & x < 0 \\ e^{-cx} & x \geq 0 \end{cases}$$

This is unimodal at \(x = 0\) for all \(c > 0\). As \(c\) increases:

  • The peak density \(f_c(0) = c/(c+1) \to 1\) increases (more peaked).
  • The right tail thins (\(\text{rate } c \to \infty\)).
  • The variance decreases (from \(5.0\) at \(c = 0.5\) to \(1.0\) as \(c \to \infty\)).
  • The distribution genuinely concentrates around the mode.

But the left tail density \(f_c(x) = [c/(c+1)]e^{x}\) for \(x < 0\) increases with \(c\) (the prefactor grows). The prevented fraction:

$$h(c, t) = 1 - \frac{c}{c+1}\,e^{t} \qquad (t < 0)$$

is decreasing in \(c\): more concentration leads to less prevention. Explicitly:

\(c\) \(f_c(0)\) Variance \(h(c, -0.5)\)
10.5002.0000.697
50.8331.0400.495
100.9091.0100.449
500.9801.0000.405

The distribution is indisputably concentrating (variance halves, peak doubles), yet punctuation hurts for \(t < 0\).

60. What goes wrong

The asymmetric exponential concentrates by shrinking only the right tail. The left tail rate (= 1) is fixed. Since the peak density must increase to preserve normalisation, the left-tail density — \([c/(c+1)]e^{x}\) — is amplified. More probability mass accumulates to the left of the mode, exactly where a threshold at \(t < 0\) can "see" it.

In short: concentration that removes the right tail while amplifying the left tail hurts prevention for \(t < 0\).

61. Why it works for infectiousness profiles

The Gamma and lognormal don't have this pathology because they have non-negative support (\(X \geq 0\) a.s.). For such distributions, the mode-centred residual \(\varepsilon = X - m\) satisfies \(\varepsilon \geq -m\). As punctuation increases and the mode \(m\) decreases toward 0, the left tail of the residual shrinks:

\(a\) (Gamma shape) Left tail extends to \(P(\varepsilon < -0.1)\)
0.5 (spiky)0 (no left tail)0.000
100.000
2\(-1\)0.228
5\(-4\)0.352
10\(-9\)0.399
20 (smooth)\(-19\)0.430

As \(a\) decreases (more punctuated), the left tail shortens and vanishes: the support boundary \(-(a-1)\) moves toward 0. Once \(m < |t|\), the threshold \(m + t\) drops below 0 and \(h = P(X > m + t) = 1\): perfect prevention.

This is the opposite of the counterexample, where the left tail was anchored at \(-\infty\).

62. A sufficient condition

Sufficient condition for "\(t < 0 \Rightarrow\) punctuation helps":

  1. The distribution has a fixed lower bound on its support (e.g., \(X \geq 0\)), and
  2. The mode moves toward that bound as punctuation increases.

Together, these ensure that the left tail of the mode-centred residual shrinks with punctuation. For \(t < 0\), a shorter left tail means less mass below the threshold and more prevention.

Both conditions are naturally satisfied for infectiousness profiles:

  • Infectiousness has non-negative support (\(X \geq 0\) a.s.).
  • A more punctuated profile concentrates its mass closer to the onset of infectiousness, pushing the mode toward 0.

This is far weaker than requiring a specific distributional family. Any non-negative unimodal family where the mode approaches the lower support boundary with increasing punctuation will have "punctuation always helps" for \(t < 0\).

63. Summary: what is and isn't universal

Result Scope
\(t \leq 0\): punctuation always helps Unimodal distributions with non-negative support, where mode → lower bound with punctuation. Not universal for arbitrary unimodal distributions on \(\mathbb{R}\).
\(t = 2/3\) crossover Pearson-relation universal in the smooth limit (any mildly-skewed family with mean − mode = const). Only the Gamma retains monotonicity away from the smooth limit.
\(t > t^*\): punctuation always hurts Verified for Gamma (\(t^* \approx 0.735\)) and lognormal (\(t^* = 2/3\)). The threshold \(t^*\) is family-specific.

Random detection times

2026-03-23

64. Setup

Replace the deterministic detection delay \(D_0\) with a random detection time \(D\) (measured from the mode), drawn from some distribution with density \(f_D\) and survival function \(S_D(t) = P(D > t)\). A unit of infectiousness at time \(\varepsilon\) after the mode escapes detection iff it occurs before detection: \(\varepsilon < D\). The prevented fraction is:
