$$H(a) = P(\varepsilon > D) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \underbrace{P(\varepsilon > d)}_{h(a,\,d)}\; f_D(d)\, dd = \mathbb{E}_D\!\bigl[h(a, D)\bigr]$$

This is a weighted average of the deterministic prevented fraction \(h(a, d)\) over the detection time distribution. Since the deterministic \(h(a, d)\) is decreasing in \(a\) for \(d < 0\) and increasing for \(d > t^*\approx 0.735\), the sign of the random \(H(a)\) depends on which region the detection mass favours.

65. E[D] < 0 is not sufficient

A detection distribution with negative mean does not guarantee that punctuation helps. Counterexample: a bimodal detection time \(D = 0.4\cdot\delta(-10) + 0.6\cdot\delta(5)\) has \(\mathbb{E}[D] = -1\), but:

\(a\) 151050100
\(H(a)\) 0.4040.4330.4660.5450.547

\(H\) is increasing in \(a\) — punctuation hurts. The 60% mass at \(d = 5\) (well past the crossover \(t^* = 0.735\)) overwhelms the 40% at \(d = -10\).

66. What matters: mass past the crossover, not the mean

For a Normal detection distribution \(D \sim N(\mu, 0.3^2)\) (a reasonably concentrated detection window), the tipping point between "punctuation helps" and "punctuation hurts" occurs near \(\mu \approx 0.75\):

\(\mu\) (mean detection) \(\mathbb{E}[D]\) \(P(D > 0.735)\) Punctuation effect
\(-0.50\)\(-0.50\)0.000Helps
\(0.00\)\(0.00\)0.007Helps
\(0.25\)\(0.25\)0.053Helps
\(0.50\)\(0.50\)0.217Helps
\(0.75\)\(0.75\)0.520Hurts
\(1.00\)\(1.00\)0.812Hurts

The tipping point occurs when roughly half the detection mass falls past \(t^* \approx 0.735\). Notably, punctuation still helps at \(\mathbb{E}[D] = 0.50\) (detection mean well past the mode), because only 22% of detections occur past the crossover.

67. Why E[D] is the wrong quantity

The prevented fraction \(H(a) = \mathbb{E}[h(a, D)]\) depends on \(D\) through the full distribution, not just its mean. The issue is that \(h(a, d)\) is not linear in \(d\) — it's a survival function with a specific shape. Two detection distributions with the same mean but different shapes (e.g., concentrated vs bimodal) can give opposite results.

The relevant quantity is instead the fraction of detection probability in the "punctuation hurts" region: \(P(D > t^*)\). When this is small, the "punctuation helps" integrals dominate, regardless of \(\mathbb{E}[D]\).

68. A convexity perspective

There is a connection to the Jensen/curvature framework from Section 9. By Fubini, the escaped fraction can also be written as:

$$\text{Escaped}(a) = \mathbb{E}_\varepsilon\!\bigl[S_D(\varepsilon)\bigr]$$

where \(S_D(t) = P(D > t)\) is evaluated at the random residual \(\varepsilon\) (which has mean \(1/\beta = 1\) and variance \(a\)). The Jensen inequality gives:

  • If \(S_D\) is convex over the support of \(\varepsilon\): \(\mathbb{E}[S_D(\varepsilon)] \geq S_D(1)\). More variance (less punctuation) ⇒ more escaped ⇒ punctuation helps.
  • If \(S_D\) is concave: the opposite ⇒ punctuation hurts.

Since \(S_D''(t) = -f_D'(t)\):

Rule of thumb: Punctuation helps when the detection density \(f_D\) is decreasing over the relevant range (making \(S_D\) convex). This includes:

  • Exponential detection (\(f_D(t) = \lambda e^{-\lambda t}\), constant hazard rate)
  • Any detection mechanism where early detection is more likely than late detection

Punctuation hurts when the detection density is increasing (detection becomes more likely over time — e.g., a purely symptom-driven mechanism where symptoms build gradually).

However, this is a global convexity condition. In practice, \(S_D\) is rarely globally convex or concave (it has a flat region at 1 for \(t < \min(\text{supp}(D))\) and at 0 for \(t > \max(\text{supp}(D))\)). The deterministic crossover analysis (Sections 40–46), integrated over the detection distribution, gives more precise answers than the Jensen bound alone.

69. Sufficient conditions

Condition on \(D\) Guarantees
\(P(D \leq 0) = 1\) (all detection before mode) Punctuation always helps (for non-negative-support profiles)
\(P(D \leq 2/(3\beta)) = 1\) Punctuation helps for \(a \geq 1\) (Gamma-specific)
\(P(D > t^*) \lesssim 0.5\) (less than half past crossover) Punctuation typically helps (empirical rule for unimodal \(f_D\))
\(f_D\) decreasing on support of \(\varepsilon\) Punctuation helps (Jensen/convexity). Can hold even for \(\mathbb{E}[D] > 0\).
\(\mathbb{E}[D] < 0\) alone NOT sufficient (counterexample in Section 65)

70. Walkthrough: why the first-derivative term vanishes

In the expansion \(h(\psi) \approx S_\psi(\mu) + \tfrac{\sigma^2}{2} S''_\psi(\mu)\), what are we expanding around, and why does the first-derivative term disappear?

We are expanding the survival function \(\tilde S_\psi(x)\) around the mean of the detection distribution, \(\mu = \mathbb{E}[D_0]\). Here is the step-by-step derivation.

Step 1: Write \(h\) as an expectation

The expected fraction averted is

$$h(\psi) \;=\; \mathbb{E}_{D_0}\!\bigl[\tilde S_\psi(D_0)\bigr] \;=\; \int \tilde S_\psi(x)\, g(x)\,\mathrm{d}x$$

where \(D_0 \sim G\) with density \(g\), mean \(\mu\), and variance \(\sigma^2\). This is just a weighted average of the deterministic survival function over all possible detection times.

Step 2: Taylor-expand \(\tilde S_\psi(x)\) around \(x = \mu\)

Expand the integrand in a Taylor series around the point \(\mu\):

$$\tilde S_\psi(x) \;=\; \tilde S_\psi(\mu) \;+\; \tilde S'_\psi(\mu)\,(x - \mu) \;+\; \tfrac{1}{2}\,\tilde S''_\psi(\mu)\,(x - \mu)^2 \;+\; O\!\bigl((x-\mu)^3\bigr)$$

Step 3: Take expectations term by term

Substitute into the integral and take expectations:

\begin{align} h(\psi) &= \mathbb{E}\!\bigl[\tilde S_\psi(D_0)\bigr] \\[6pt] &\approx\; \tilde S_\psi(\mu)\,\underbrace{\mathbb{E}[1]}_{=\,1} \;+\; \tilde S'_\psi(\mu)\,\underbrace{\mathbb{E}[D_0 - \mu]}_{=\,0} \;+\; \tfrac{1}{2}\,\tilde S''_\psi(\mu)\,\underbrace{\mathbb{E}\!\bigl[(D_0 - \mu)^2\bigr]}_{=\,\sigma^2} \end{align}

Step 4: Why the first-derivative term vanishes

The first-derivative coefficient is

$$\tilde S'_\psi(\mu) \cdot \mathbb{E}[D_0 - \mu]$$

and \(\mathbb{E}[D_0 - \mu] = \mathbb{E}[D_0] - \mu = \mu - \mu = 0\) by the definition of \(\mu\) as the mean. This is not a special property of \(\tilde S\) — it holds for the Taylor expansion of any smooth function evaluated at a random variable and expanded around that variable's mean. The first central moment is always zero.

Summary. The expansion is a standard "delta method" calculation:

  1. We want \(\mathbb{E}[f(X)]\) where \(X\) has mean \(\mu\) and variance \(\sigma^2\).
  2. Taylor-expand \(f\) around \(\mu\): \(f(X) \approx f(\mu) + f'(\mu)(X - \mu) + \tfrac{1}{2}f''(\mu)(X-\mu)^2\).
  3. Take expectations: \(\mathbb{E}[f(X)] \approx f(\mu) + \tfrac{\sigma^2}{2}f''(\mu)\).
  4. The first-derivative term drops because \(\mathbb{E}[X - \mu] = 0\) (always, by definition).
  5. The second-derivative term is the Jensen correction: it captures the curvature of \(f\), and its sign depends on whether \(f\) is convex (\(f'' > 0\), expectation exceeds \(f(\mu)\)) or concave (\(f'' < 0\), expectation falls below \(f(\mu)\)).

In our case \(f = \tilde S_\psi\), and whether \(\tilde S''_\psi(\mu) \gtrless 0\) depends on whether \(\mu\) is past the mode (density falling → \(S''\!> 0\), convex) or before the mode (density rising → \(S''\!< 0\), concave).

71. Does variation help before the mode and hurt after?

Does variation in detection only help (and help punctuated profiles more) when \(\mu < 0\), and hurt when \(\mu > 0\)?

No — the signs are actually reversed from this intuition, and the relative effect (spike vs smooth) has additional structure. There are two separate questions here:

  1. Absolute effect: does variation increase or decrease \(h\) (the fraction averted)?
  2. Relative effect: does variation favour spikes or smooth profiles?

A. The absolute effect: determined by curvature of \(\tilde S\)

From the delta-method expansion, the leading correction is \(\tfrac{\sigma^2}{2}\tilde S''_\psi(\mu)\). Since \(\tilde S'' = -\tilde f'\):

Detection mean \(\mu\) Density slope \(\tilde f'(\mu)\) \(\tilde S''(\mu)\) Curvature Effect of variation on \(h\)
\(\mu < 0\) (before mode) \(> 0\) (rising toward peak) \(< 0\) Concave Hurts (reduces \(h\))
\(\mu > 0\) (after mode) \(< 0\) (falling from peak) \(> 0\) Convex Helps (increases \(h\))

Physical intuition:

  • \(\mu < 0\) (detection before mode): you're already catching most transmission. Variation introduces some unlucky late detections that miss more — the downside exceeds the upside. Variation hurts.
  • \(\mu > 0\) (detection after mode): you're already missing a lot. Variation gives some lucky early detections that catch more — the upside exceeds the downside. Variation helps.

This is just Jensen's inequality: when \(\tilde S\) is concave (before the mode), \(\mathbb{E}[\tilde S(D_0)] < \tilde S(\mu)\); when convex (after), \(\mathbb{E}[\tilde S(D_0)] > \tilde S(\mu)\).

B. The relative effect: who benefits more?

The change in the spike advantage due to variation is:

$$\Delta_{\text{var}} = \bigl[h_{\text{spike}}(\mu,\sigma) - h_{\text{smooth}}(\mu,\sigma)\bigr] - \bigl[h_{\text{spike}}(\mu,0) - h_{\text{smooth}}(\mu,0)\bigr]$$

This has a more complex sign pattern than the absolute effect:

\(\mu\) Spike \(h\) change Smooth \(h\) change Net: variation favours…
\(-2\)\(\approx 0\) (spike already at \(h = 1\))\(-0.004\) (concave)Spikes (+0.004)
\(-0.5\)\(-0.034\) (kink effect)\(-0.001\)Smooth (\(-0.033\))
\(0\)\(-0.150\) (devastating kink)\(\approx 0\)Smooth (\(-0.150\))
\(0.5\)\(+0.027\)\(+0.001\)Spikes (+0.027)
\(1.0\)\(+0.044\)\(+0.002\)Spikes (+0.042)
\(2.0\)\(+0.018\)\(+0.002\)Spikes (+0.016)

(Spike: \(a = 1\); Smooth: \(a = 10\); \(\sigma = 0.5\))

C. Why the mode is a danger zone for spikes

The surprising result is that near \(\mu = 0\) (detection right at the mode), variation devastates spikes. Here's why:

At \(\mu = 0\) deterministically, the spike averts everything (\(h_{\text{spike}} = 1.00\), since all of a concentrated profile's mass sits at or just past the mode). The smooth profile averts only 59% (\(h_{\text{smooth}} = 0.59\)). The spike has a huge advantage.

But the spike's survival function has a sharp kink at \(d = 0\): it equals 1 for all \(d \leq 0\) and drops as \(e^{-d}\) for \(d > 0\). Any rightward noise sends detection past the spike's burst — and the spike's transmission is all-or-nothing, so even slight rightward shifts lose nearly everything. With \(\sigma = 0.5\), the spike drops from \(h = 1.00\) to \(h = 0.85\), a loss of 15 percentage points.

The smooth profile, by contrast, has a gently curving \(\tilde S\) near \(d = 0\), so it barely notices the variation (\(h\) changes by < 0.1%).

D. Synthesis

Three regimes for the relative effect of detection variation:

  1. \(\mu \ll 0\) (detection well before mode): Variation slightly favours spikes. Smooth profiles are hurt by concavity; spikes are insulated (already at \(h \approx 1\)).
  2. \(\mu \approx 0\) (detection near the mode): Variation strongly favours smooth profiles. The spike's kink makes it catastrophically vulnerable to rightward noise.
  3. \(\mu > 0\) (detection after mode): Variation favours spikes. Both profiles benefit from convexity, but the spike benefits far more (steeper curvature). This is the regime analysed in the "variance helps spikes" section.

The operationally relevant regime is typically (3): detection happens some time after peak infectiousness, and the question is whether the spike's concentrated burst has already been missed. In this regime, noise in detection timing reliably favours spikes — they benefit more from lucky early catches. The "variance helps spikes" result holds where it matters most, but it is not universal across all \(\mu\).

Does \(\psi\) affect the final size distribution? Miller's test-individual framework and its limits

2026-03-25

1. Miller's result: the expected final size is \(\psi\)-independent

Miller's test-individual argument is beautifully simple. Take a random test individual \(u\) and prevent it from transmitting. In a well-mixed population of size \(N\), each infected person transmits to \(u\) with probability \(\mathcal{R}_0/N\), independently. If a fraction \(\pi\) of the population is ultimately infected, then \(u\) escapes all \(\pi N\) potential transmissions with probability:

$$P(u \text{ escapes}) = \left(1 - \frac{\mathcal{R}_0}{N}\right)^{\pi N} \xrightarrow{N \to \infty} e^{-\mathcal{R}_0 \pi}$$

Since \(P(u \text{ infected}) = \pi\) by consistency, we get the classical final size relation:

$$\pi = 1 - e^{-\mathcal{R}_0 \pi}$$

The key insight Miller emphasises (p. 2): "The derivation does not depend on the dynamics of the infection process, but just the final probability that one individual will transmit to another if infected." In our model, each infector makes \(N \sim \text{Pois}(\mathcal{R}_0)\) attempts targeting uniformly random individuals. The probability any given person is targeted is \(\mathcal{R}_0/N\), regardless of \(\psi\). So the expected final size is \(\psi\)-independent. Full stop.

2. Why Miller's framework can't capture the variance

Miller's derivation assumes the final size concentrates. Quoting (p. 3): "If it makes sense to discuss the final size of the epidemic, then the variation in the final sizes of an ensemble of epidemic realizations must be small compared to the final size."

This is a law-of-large-numbers statement. It tells us \(Z_N / N \xrightarrow{p} \pi\) as \(N \to \infty\). But it doesn't tell us about the \(O(\sqrt{N})\) fluctuations around \(\pi\) — and that's exactly where \(\psi\) lives.

3. The W time-shift doesn't help either

Our branching-process approximation gives stochastic trajectories of the form:

$$C(t) \approx N \cdot z_{\det}(t + \tau), \quad \tau = -\frac{\log W}{r}$$

where \(z_{\det}\) is the deterministic epidemic fraction and \(W\) captures the stochastic head-start. Since \(\text{Var}(W)\) depends on \(\psi\), the timing of the epidemic depends on \(\psi\). But as \(t \to \infty\), \(z_{\det}(t + \tau) \to \pi\) regardless of \(\tau\). The time shift delays or advances the epidemic but doesn't change where it ends up. So the \(W\) framework captures variation in timing but not in final size.

4. Where the variance actually comes from

The final size variance comes from demographic stochasticity throughout the epidemic — the \(O(\sqrt{N})\) fluctuations in the depletion trajectory \(S(t)\). By CLT-type results for stochastic epidemics (Barbour 1975, Andersson & Britton 2000), we expect:

$$\sqrt{N}\left(\frac{Z_N}{N} - \pi\right) \xrightarrow{d} \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma^2)$$

where \(\sigma^2\) depends on \(\psi\). The mechanism: consider how individual-level transmission feeds into the variance of the infection process.

For a smooth profile (\(\psi = 1\)): person \(i\)'s infection attempts occur at times \(\tau_1, \ldots, \tau_N\) drawn independently from \(\text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\). Each attempt encounters the susceptible fraction \(S(\tau_j)\) at its own time. The outcomes are nearly independent, giving low variance in person \(i\)'s total contribution.

For a spike profile (\(\psi = 0\)): person \(i\)'s attempts all occur at time \(\tau \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\). All attempts encounter the same \(S(\tau)\). If \(\tau\) happens to fall when \(S\) is high, all attempts benefit; if \(S\) is low, all suffer. This positive correlation inflates the variance of the per-person transmission contribution.

More precisely, the per-person infection count \(\nu_i\) (successful transmissions from person \(i\)) has the same mean under both profiles — approximately \(\mathcal{R}_0 \cdot S(t_i)\) where \(t_i\) is person \(i\)'s infection time. But the variance of \(\nu_i\) is higher for spike profiles because:

$$\text{Var}(\nu_i \mid \text{spike}) \approx \underbrace{\mathcal{R}_0 \cdot S}_{\text{Poisson variance}} + \underbrace{\mathcal{R}_0^2 \cdot \text{Var}(S(\tau))}_{\text{synchronisation penalty}}$$ $$\text{Var}(\nu_i \mid \text{smooth}) \approx \underbrace{\mathcal{R}_0 \cdot S}_{\text{Poisson variance}}$$

The extra term for spike profiles arises because all attempts see the same random \(S(\tau)\), while for smooth profiles the variation in \(S(\tau_j)\) across attempts averages out.

These per-person variance contributions accumulate over the \(\sim N\pi\) infectors throughout the epidemic, giving \(\text{Var}(Z_N) = O(N)\) with a prefactor that increases as \(\psi \to 0\).

5. What would an analytical approach look like?

To actually compute \(\sigma^2(\psi)\), one would need a diffusion approximation (system-size expansion) of the stochastic epidemic around the deterministic trajectory. The idea:

  1. Write the infection process as \(C(t) = N z_{\det}(t) + \sqrt{N} \cdot \xi(t)\) where \(\xi\) is the fluctuation process.
  2. Derive the SDE for \(\xi(t)\), whose diffusion coefficient involves the per-person offspring variance — which depends on \(\psi\).
  3. Propagate to \(t \to \infty\) to get \(\text{Var}(\xi(\infty)) = \sigma^2(\psi)\).

This is a meaningfully harder calculation than either the Miller final-size relation or the W-distribution calculation, because it requires tracking the full epidemic trajectory, not just the early-phase branching process or the final state. It's a viable path but goes beyond what the test-individual framework can offer.

6. Summary

FrameworkWhat it captures\(\psi\)-dependent?
Miller test individualExpected final size \(\pi\)No
W time-shiftEpidemic timing variabilityYes
Diffusion approximationFinal size variance \(\sigma^2\)Yes (needed)

The punchline: the expected final size is \(\psi\)-independent by Miller's argument (and this is exact, not an approximation). The final size variance is \(\psi\)-dependent, but capturing it requires a diffusion/CLT approach that tracks demographic stochasticity throughout the epidemic — not just the early-phase branching process or the final transmission probability. The test-individual framework elegantly explains why the mean doesn't depend on \(\psi\), but its very premise (that the final size concentrates) is what we'd need to relax to study the variance.

Could we compute the effective overdispersion of secondary infections as a function of ψ, and use that to approximate the final size variance? (cf. Lloyd-Smith et al. 2005)

Effective overdispersion from punctuated infectiousness

This is a productive approach. We can derive an effective negative-binomial dispersion parameter \(k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t)\) that varies both with the punctuation parameter and with the stage of the epidemic. The key result is clean and connects directly to the diffusion coefficient needed for the final size variance.

1. Setup

Consider person \(i\), infected at time \(t\). They generate \(N_i \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0)\) infection attempts. Each attempt \(j\) occurs at time \(t + l_i + \epsilon_j\), where:

$$l_i \sim \text{Gamma}\bigl((1-\psi)\alpha,\; \beta\bigr) \qquad \text{(shared shift)}$$ $$\epsilon_j \sim \text{Gamma}\bigl(\psi\alpha,\; \beta\bigr) \qquad \text{(independent jitter)}$$

Each attempt contacts a uniformly random individual. In a population of size \(N\) with deterministic susceptible fraction \(S(t)\), the probability that attempt \(j\) succeeds is:

$$p(l_i, \epsilon_j) \;=\; \frac{S(t + l_i + \epsilon_j)}{N}$$

The total number of successful secondary infections is:

$$Z_i = \sum_{j=1}^{N_i} B_j, \qquad B_j \sim \text{Bernoulli}\bigl(p(l_i, \epsilon_j)\bigr)$$

with \(B_j\) conditionally independent given the timing realizations (since they contact different random targets).

2. Mean offspring count (ψ-independent)

The mean number of secondary infections is:

$$E[Z_i] = E[N_i] \cdot E_\tau\!\left[\frac{S(t + \tau)}{N}\right] = R_0 \,\bar{p}(t)$$

where \(\tau = l + \epsilon \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\) regardless of \(\psi\), and \(\bar{p}(t) = E_\tau[S(t+\tau)/N]\). Since the marginal distribution of \(\tau\) is the same for all \(\psi\), the mean offspring count is \(\psi\)-independent — consistent with the Miller final size result.

3. Offspring variance: the tower calculation

Define the conditional mean success probability given the shared shift:

$$\bar{p}(l) \;=\; E_\epsilon\!\left[\frac{S(t + l + \epsilon)}{N}\right], \qquad \epsilon \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)$$

This is the per-attempt success probability, averaged over the jitter \(\epsilon\), for a person whose shared shift takes value \(l\). Now we apply the law of total variance in stages.

Step 1: Variance given \(N_i = n\) and \(l_i = l\)

Conditioning on the shared shift \(l\), the attempts have independent jitters \(\epsilon_j\). By the same tower argument applied to \(\epsilon\):

$$\text{Var}(Z_i \mid N_i = n,\, l_i = l) = n\,\bar{p}(l)\bigl(1 - \bar{p}(l)\bigr)$$

Proof sketch: Given \(l\), the \(B_j\) are conditionally independent Bernoullis with random probabilities \(p(l, \epsilon_j)\). The variance decomposes as:

$$n\,E_\epsilon[p(1-p)] + n\,\text{Var}_\epsilon(p) = n\bigl(\bar{p}(l) - E[p^2] + E[p^2] - \bar{p}(l)^2\bigr) = n\,\bar{p}(l)(1-\bar{p}(l))$$

So given the shared shift, the offspring count has a binomial-like variance — the independent jitters "smooth out" any within-person variation from the changing susceptible fraction.

Step 2: Integrate over \(l\)

Using \(E[Z_i \mid l] = R_0\,\bar{p}(l)\) and \(\text{Var}(Z_i \mid l) = R_0\,\bar{p}(l)\) (the latter from combining Step 1 with the Poisson \(N_i\)):

$$\text{Var}(Z_i) = E_l\bigl[R_0\,\bar{p}(l)\bigr] + \text{Var}_l\bigl[R_0\,\bar{p}(l)\bigr]$$
$$\boxed{\text{Var}(Z_i) \;=\; R_0\,\bar{p} \;+\; R_0^2\,\text{Var}_l\!\bigl(\bar{p}(l)\bigr)}$$

where \(l \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha,\,\beta)\).

The first term, \(R_0\bar{p} = E[Z_i]\), is the Poisson baseline. The second term is the synchronization-induced overdispersion.

4. Limiting cases

  • \(\psi = 1\) (smooth): The shared shift has shape \((1-\psi)\alpha = 0\), so \(l = 0\) a.s. Then \(\bar{p}(l)\) is constant, \(\text{Var}_l = 0\), and \(\text{Var}(Z_i) = R_0\bar{p}\). Exactly Poisson — no overdispersion at any stage of the epidemic.
  • \(\psi = 0\) (spike): The shared shift is \(l \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\) (the full generation interval), and jitter vanishes (\(\epsilon = 0\)), so \(\bar{p}(l) = S(t+l)/N\). The overdispersion is \(R_0^2 \text{Var}_l(S(t+l)/N)\), which depends on how much the susceptible fraction varies over the timescale of the generation interval.
  • Early epidemic (\(S \approx N\)): \(\bar{p}(l) \approx 1\) for all \(l\), so \(\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l)) \approx 0\). All \(\psi\) values give Poisson offspring in the early phase. This confirms that the branching process approximation (with Poisson offspring) is valid for all \(\psi\).
  • Mid-epidemic (\(S\) changing rapidly): The variance \(\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))\) is largest, since different values of \(l\) map to very different susceptible fractions. This is where punctuation has its greatest effect.

5. Effective negative binomial dispersion

The negative binomial with mean \(\mu\) and dispersion \(k\) has variance \(\mu + \mu^2/k\). Matching to our result:

$$R_0\bar{p} + R_0^2\,\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l)) = R_0\bar{p} + \frac{(R_0\bar{p})^2}{k_{\text{eff}}}$$

gives the effective dispersion parameter:

$$\boxed{k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t) \;=\; \frac{\bar{p}(t)^2}{\text{Var}_l\!\bigl(\bar{p}(l;\, t)\bigr)}}$$

where the dependence on \(\psi\) enters through both:

  1. The distribution of \(l \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha, \beta)\) — smaller \(\psi\) means more variable shifts
  2. The smoothing in \(\bar{p}(l) = E_\epsilon[S(t+l+\epsilon)/N]\) — smaller \(\psi\) means less smoothing by jitter

Interpretation: \(k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t)\) measures how much the shared shift \(l\) creates variability in the "effective environment" faced by a person's infection attempts. When \(\psi \to 0\), the environment is fully determined by a single random time point; when \(\psi \to 1\), attempts independently sample the susceptible pool at different times, averaging out environmental variation.

6. Computing \(k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t)\) numerically

All ingredients are available from existing code:

  1. The deterministic trajectory \(S(t)\) comes from renewal_epidemic()
  2. For each time \(t\), compute \(\bar{p}(l) = E_\epsilon[S(t + l + \epsilon)/N]\) by numerical integration (or Monte Carlo) over \(\epsilon \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\)
  3. Compute \(\bar{p}(t) = E_l[\bar{p}(l)]\) and \(\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))\) by integrating over \(l \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha, \beta)\)
  4. Take the ratio \(k_{\text{eff}} = \bar{p}^2 / \text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))\)

This gives a time-varying effective dispersion that starts at \(k_{\text{eff}} \to \infty\) (Poisson, early epidemic), decreases to a minimum around the epidemic peak (maximum overdispersion), and increases again as the epidemic wanes.

7. Connection to final size variance

The offspring variance \(\text{Var}(Z_i)\) is exactly the quantity that enters the diffusion coefficient in the system-size expansion. The idea (Ethier & Kurtz 1986, Barbour 1975):

Write the cumulative incidence as \(C(t) = N\,c_{\det}(t) + \sqrt{N}\,\xi(t)\), where \(c_{\det}\) is the deterministic (renewal equation) trajectory and \(\xi\) is the fluctuation process. The variance of \(\xi\) evolves as:

$$\frac{d}{dt}\text{Var}(\xi) \;\approx\; j_{\det}(t) \cdot \frac{\text{Var}(Z_i)}{E[Z_i]} \;=\; j_{\det}(t)\left(1 + \frac{R_0\,\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))}{\bar{p}(t)}\right)$$

where \(j_{\det}(t)\) is the deterministic incidence rate. The final size variance is then:

$$\text{Var}(C(\infty)/N) \;\approx\; \frac{1}{N} \int_0^\infty j_{\det}(t)\left(1 + \frac{R_0\,\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l;\,t))}{\bar{p}(t)}\right) dt$$

The first term (integrating \(j_{\det}\)) gives the Poisson contribution to final size variance — this is the same for all \(\psi\). The second term is the \(\psi\)-dependent overdispersion contribution:

$$\Delta\sigma^2(\psi) \;=\; \frac{R_0}{N}\int_0^\infty j_{\det}(t)\,\frac{\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l;\,t))}{\bar{p}(t)}\,dt$$

This integral weighs the instantaneous overdispersion by the incidence rate. Since overdispersion peaks near the epidemic peak (where \(S\) changes most rapidly) and incidence is also highest there, the contributions reinforce.

8. Summary and next steps

QuantityFormula\(\psi\)-dependent?
Mean offspring \(E[Z_i]\)\(R_0\,\bar{p}(t)\)No
Offspring variance\(R_0\bar{p} + R_0^2\text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))\)Yes
Effective dispersion \(k_{\text{eff}}\)\(\bar{p}^2 / \text{Var}_l(\bar{p}(l))\)Yes
Early epidemic\(k_{\text{eff}} \to \infty\) (Poisson for all \(\psi\))No
Final size meanMiller: \(\pi = 1 - e^{-R_0\pi}\)No
Final size varianceDiffusion integral of overdispersionYes

Key insight: Punctuation (\(\psi < 1\)) creates overdispersion in the offspring distribution, but only during the phase of the epidemic when the susceptible fraction is changing. In the early (branching process) phase, all \(\psi\) give Poisson offspring. The effective dispersion \(k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t)\) quantifies this: it starts infinite, drops to a minimum near the epidemic peak (especially for small \(\psi\)), and rises again. This time-varying overdispersion integrates to give the \(\psi\)-dependent final size variance via the diffusion approximation.

Implementable next steps:

  1. Numerically compute \(k_{\text{eff}}(\psi, t)\) for each pathogen using the renewal equation trajectory — plot this as a function of epidemic progress
  2. Evaluate the diffusion integral to predict \(\text{Var}(Z_\infty)\) as a function of \(\psi\)
  3. Compare the predicted final size variance with the empirical variance from simulations
Does the exponential delay in fig_te_testing_delay integrate out? i.e., would a fixed delay of \(1/\lambda\) give the same TE curves?

Does the delay distribution integrate out?

No. A fixed delay of \(1/\lambda\) gives different TE curves from an \(\text{Exp}(\lambda)\) delay with the same mean. The key reason is Jensen's inequality: the survival function \(S_\psi\) is not linear, so \(\mathbb{E}[S_\psi(t_0 + \delta)] \neq S_\psi(t_0 + \mathbb{E}[\delta])\) in general.

Setup

With detection at time \(t_0\) (relative to the infectiousness profile), the TE contribution is:

  • Fixed delay \(\delta_0 = 1/\lambda\):   \(S_\psi(t_0 + 1/\lambda)\)
  • Random delay \(\delta \sim \text{Exp}(\lambda)\):   \(\mathbb{E}_\delta[S_\psi(t_0 + \delta)]\)

Jensen's inequality says these are equal iff \(S_\psi\) is linear (affine) on the support of \(t_0 + \delta\). Since \(S_\psi(t) = 1 - F_{\text{Gamma}(\alpha\psi,\,\beta)}(t)\) is generally nonlinear, they differ.

Direction of the bias

The second derivative of \(S_\psi\) is \(S''_\psi(t) = -f'_\psi(t)\), where \(f_\psi\) is the Gamma density. For shape \(\alpha\psi > 1\), the density is unimodal, so:

  • Before the density mode: \(f'_\psi > 0\), so \(S''_\psi < 0\) (concave). Jensen gives \(\mathbb{E}[S(t_0+\delta)] < S(t_0 + 1/\lambda)\).
  • After the density mode: \(f'_\psi < 0\), so \(S''_\psi > 0\) (convex). Jensen gives \(\mathbb{E}[S(t_0+\delta)] > S(t_0 + 1/\lambda)\).

So the direction of the discrepancy depends on where isolation falls relative to the infectiousness mode. Early isolation (concave region): the random delay reduces TE relative to fixed. Late isolation (convex region): the random delay increases TE relative to fixed. The integrated TE over detection times averages over both regions, and the net effect depends on parameters.

The spike case is the starkest example

For \(\psi \to 0\), \(S_0(t)\) is a step function: \(S_0(t) = \mathbf{1}(t < 0)\). The distinction is clearest here:

  • Fixed delay: \(S_0(t_0 + 1/\lambda) = \mathbf{1}(t_0 < -1/\lambda)\). This is still a step function, just shifted left by \(1/\lambda\).
  • Random delay: \(\mathbb{E}[S_0(t_0 + \delta)] = P(\delta < -t_0) = \begin{cases} 0 & t_0 \geq 0 \\ 1 - e^{\lambda t_0} & t_0 < 0 \end{cases}\)

The fixed delay shifts the step; the random delay smooths it into an exponential ramp. For \(t_0 \in (-1/\lambda,\, 0)\), the fixed delay gives \(S = 0\) (the delay pushed isolation past the spike) while the random delay gives \(S = 1 - e^{\lambda t_0} > 0\) (there's still a chance the delay was short enough). Conversely, for \(t_0 < -1/\lambda\), the fixed delay gives \(S = 1\) while the random delay gives \(S < 1\) (some probability the delay was long enough to miss).

Upshot

The distribution of the delay does not integrate out. The random delay smooths the TE curves relative to a fixed delay of the same mean. The effect is most dramatic for spike profiles and diminishes as \(\psi \to 1\) (where \(S_\psi\) becomes smoother and more nearly linear over the scale of the delay). If you wanted to verify numerically, you could add a fixed-delay comparison to section 3 — just evaluate \(S_\psi(u - d_{\text{pre}} + \text{mode} + 1/\lambda)\) in place of the closed-form expectation.

Mathematical accuracy review of writeup/main.tex (2026-04-10)

Review of main.tex and its inputs (model.tex, uncontrolled.tex, controlled.tex, identifiability.tex, supplementary.tex) against the derivations themselves. Findings are grouped by severity.

A. Substantive mathematical errors

A.1 Spike profile mass in eq:a_seir_spike (model.tex)

The SEIR parameters in the manuscript are:

  • latent period \(x_i \sim \mathrm{Exp}(\gamma)\) — so \(\gamma\) is the latent rate,
  • infectious period \(y_i \sim \mathrm{Exp}(\alpha)\) — so \(\alpha\) is the recovery rate,
  • infectiousness level during the infectious period \(=\beta\).

Integrating the population-level \(A(\tau)\) given in eq:A_seir,

$$ R_0 = \int_0^\infty \beta\,\frac{\gamma}{\gamma-\alpha}\bigl(e^{-\alpha\tau}-e^{-\gamma\tau}\bigr)\,d\tau = \beta\,\frac{\gamma}{\gamma-\alpha}\left(\frac{1}{\alpha}-\frac{1}{\gamma}\right) = \frac{\beta}{\alpha}. $$

So for SEIR, \(R_0=\beta/\alpha\). For the spike profile to have the same individual reproduction number as the smooth profile (\(\nu_i = R_0\) for all \(i\)), its total mass must equal \(R_0=\beta/\alpha\). The writeup currently has

$$ a_i(\tau) \;=\; \frac{\beta}{\gamma}\,\delta_{t_i}(\tau), $$

which gives \(\nu_i=\beta/\gamma\neq R_0\) whenever \(\gamma\neq\alpha\). This is a typo: the coefficient should be \(\beta/\alpha\), not \(\beta/\gamma\). (Equivalently, write it as \(a_i(\tau)=R_0\,\delta_{t_i}(\tau)\) with \(t_i\sim g(\tau)\), which is unambiguous.)

A.2 Time-shifted Gamma in eq:gamma_timeshift (model.tex)

As written,

$$ b_i(\tau)=\begin{cases}0 & \tau\le l_i\\ f_\epsilon(\tau) & \tau > l_i\end{cases}, \qquad f_\epsilon \sim \mathrm{Gamma}(\psi\alpha,\beta),\;\; l_i \sim \mathrm{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha,\beta). $$

This is not a valid density: the integral over \(\tau>l_i\) of a \(\mathrm{Gamma}(\psi\alpha,\beta)\) pdf evaluated at \(\tau\) (not at \(\tau-l_i\)) is \(1-F_\epsilon(l_i)\neq 1\). Also, the text explicitly describes \(b_i\) as "a shifted Gamma distribution preceded by a latent period \(l_i\)," and the Gamma-additivity argument (used throughout the supplement and for \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\)) only works if the lag beyond \(l_i\) is itself Gamma, i.e. \(\tau-l_i\mid l_i\sim\mathrm{Gamma}(\psi\alpha,\beta)\). The correct expression is

$$ b_i(\tau)=\begin{cases}0 & \tau\le l_i\\ f_\epsilon(\tau-l_i) & \tau > l_i\end{cases}. $$

The supplementary derivation of \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\) already uses the correct decomposition \(\tau_j=l_i+\epsilon_j\), so this is a notational slip in model.tex, not a flaw in the downstream math.

B. Internal notation inconsistencies (the user asked specifically about this)

B.1 identifiability.tex uses \(\kappa\) instead of \(\psi\)

The rest of the .tex uses \(\psi\) for the punctuation parameter, but identifiability.tex (§"Identifiability and inference…") repeatedly refers to "\(\kappa\) is shared across individuals and where \(\kappa\) comes from a beta distribution…". The user flagged that \(\kappa\) is legacy notation from findings.md and should be \(\psi\) inside the .tex. Rename all \(\kappa\)→\(\psi\) in this file.

B.2 Symbol collisions inside model.tex

Several symbols are overloaded within the same file:

  1. \(\alpha\): SEIR recovery rate (in eq:a_seir_stepwise/eq:A_seir) and Gamma shape parameter of the generation interval (in eq:gamma_gi). These are physically different objects.
  2. \(\beta\): SEIR "infectiousness level" and Gamma rate parameter \(=\alpha/\mu\).
  3. \(\gamma\): SEIR latent rate.
  4. \(\epsilon\): the Gamma lag \(\epsilon_j\) / the density \(f_\epsilon\) in eq:gamma_timeshift and the cosine amplitude in the periodic contact model \(c_i(t)=1-\epsilon\cos(2\pi t)\).
  5. \(t_i\): the location of the spike on the \(\tau\)-axis in eq:a_seir_spike and person \(i\)'s infection time on the calendar in eq:abc, eq:A_def_general, eq:ABC. These are fundamentally different variables.

At minimum, I'd recommend:

  • rename SEIR-section \((\alpha,\beta,\gamma)\) to something like \((\gamma_R,\beta_c,\gamma_L)\) or footnote that these symbols are reused locally for the SEIR example only;
  • rename the cosine amplitude \(\epsilon\) to something else (say \(A\) or \(a\))… but then \(A\) already denotes the population profile, so perhaps \(\varepsilon_c\) or \(\eta\);
  • replace \(t_i\) in eq:a_seir_spike with \(\tau_i^*\) or similar, reserving \(t_i\) for calendar infection time.

B.3 eq:A_def_general: argument mismatch

Written as

$$ A(t_i,\tau)=E_i[a_i(\tau)]=E_i[\nu_i b_i(\tau)]\,E_i[c_i(t_i+\tau)]. $$

The LHS is a function of \((t_i,\tau)\) but the inner expectation drops \(t_i\) from \(a_i\). It should read \(A(t,\tau)=E_i[a_i(t,\tau)]\), where \(t\) is a fixed calendar infection time (not a person-indexed \(t_i\), since we are averaging over \(i\)). Then \(E_i[c_i(t+\tau)]=C(t+\tau)\). The same \(t_i\)-as-fixed-time abuse appears in eq:ABC.

B.4 "mean latent period \(\gamma=\)XX, mean infectious period \(\alpha=\)XX" (caption of Fig. seir_sims)

Given the rest of the text, \(\gamma\) and \(\alpha\) are rates, so the mean latent period is \(1/\gamma\) and the mean infectious period is \(1/\alpha\). The caption should either swap the identification (rates) or put \(1/\gamma\) and \(1/\alpha\) in the placeholders.

B.5 Density vs. random-variable notation

eq:gamma_gi writes "\(g(\tau)\sim\mathrm{Gamma}(\alpha,\beta)\)". Strictly, \(g\) is a density, not a random variable. Prefer "let \(g(\tau)\) be the \(\mathrm{Gamma}(\alpha,\beta)\) pdf". Minor.

C. Derivations that I verified and that check out

C.1 Euler–Lotka (supplementary.tex, eq:euler_lotka)

$$ 1 = R_0\int_0^\infty e^{-r\tau}\frac{\beta^\alpha}{\Gamma(\alpha)}\tau^{\alpha-1}e^{-\beta\tau}d\tau = R_0\left(\frac{\beta}{\beta+r}\right)^{\!\alpha} = \frac{R_0}{(1+z)^\alpha}, $$

with \(z=r/\beta\). Correct. Implies \(r=\beta(R_0^{1/\alpha}-1)=(\alpha/\bar g)(R_0^{1/\alpha}-1)\), matching eq:r_from_params.

C.2 \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\) derivation

Starting from the distributional fixed point \(W\stackrel{d}{=}\sum_{j=1}^{\chi_i}e^{-r\tau_j}W_j\) and applying the law of total variance conditional on \((\chi_i,\tau_1,\dots,\tau_{\chi_i})\),

$$ \mathrm{Var}[W]=\mathrm{Var}[W]\cdot \mathbb{E}\!\left[\sum_j e^{-2r\tau_j}\right] + \mathrm{Var}\!\left[\sum_j e^{-r\tau_j}\right]. $$

With \(\tau_j=l_i+\epsilon_j\) and \(\rho_k=(\beta/(\beta+kr))=1/(1+kz)\):

$$ \mathbb{E}\!\left[\sum_j e^{-2r\tau_j}\right]=R_0\,\rho_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\rho_2^{\psi\alpha}=R_0\rho_2^\alpha, $$ $$ \mathbb{E}\!\left[\Bigl(\textstyle\sum_j e^{-r\tau_j}\Bigr)^{\!2}\right] =\underbrace{R_0\rho_2^\alpha}_{j=k\text{ terms}}+\underbrace{R_0^2\,\rho_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\rho_1^{2\psi\alpha}}_{j\neq k\text{ terms, share }l_i}. $$

The factor \(\rho_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\) (not \(\rho_1^{2(1-\psi)\alpha}\)) is the crucial consequence of \(l_i\) being shared across all offspring of \(i\); this shared latent period is exactly what makes \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\) depend on \(\psi\). Substituting \(R_0=(1+z)^\alpha\), \(\rho_1=1/(1+z)\), \(\rho_2=1/(1+2z)\) reproduces

$$ \mathrm{Var}[W]=\frac{\left(\frac{1+z}{1+2z}\right)^{\!\alpha}+\left(\frac{(1+z)^2}{1+2z}\right)^{\!(1-\psi)\alpha}-1}{1-\left(\frac{1+z}{1+2z}\right)^{\!\alpha}}. $$

Correct. Non-negativity of the denominator follows from \(\rho_2<\rho_1\) (i.e. \(R_0\rho_2^\alpha0\). I also checked the ψ→1 limit collapses the numerator to \(R_0\rho_2^\alpha\) (positive, as expected).

C.3 Monotonicity of \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\) in \(\psi\)

The base \(\sigma=(1+z)^2/(1+2z)\) satisfies \(\sigma>1\) for \(z>0\) since \((1+z)^2=1+2z+z^2>1+2z\). So \(\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\) is decreasing in \(\psi\), i.e. \(\psi\to 0\) (spike) maximizes \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\) and \(\psi\to 1\) (smooth) minimizes it. Matches the manuscript's claim.

C.4 Mode of the Gamma approximation to \(\varsigma\)

With \(E[W]=1\) and the stated \(E[W^2]\), conditioning on survival (\(E[W\mid\text{surv}]=1/p\), \(E[W^2\mid\text{surv}]=E[W^2]/p\)) gives

$$ \mathrm{CV}^2[W\mid\text{surv}]=p\,E[W^2]-1 = \frac{p\left(\tfrac{(1+z)^2}{1+2z}\right)^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1-\left(\tfrac{1+z}{1+2z}\right)^{\alpha}}-1, $$

and the Gamma-mode \((k-1)/\lambda=(1-\mathrm{CV}^2)/p\) followed by \(\mathrm{Mode}[\varsigma]=(1/r)\log(\mathrm{mode}[W\mid\text{surv}])\) gives

$$ \mathrm{Mode}[\varsigma]=\frac{1}{r}\log\!\left(\frac{2\left[1-\left(\tfrac{1+z}{1+2z}\right)^\alpha\right]-p\left(\tfrac{(1+z)^2}{1+2z}\right)^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{p\left[1-\left(\tfrac{1+z}{1+2z}\right)^\alpha\right]}\right). $$

Matches eq:mode_timeshift. Good. The cautionary note that "mode \(\to -\infty\) when the numerator \(\le 0\)" is correct and is exactly where the Gamma approximation loses its interior mode (\(k<1\)).

C.5 Monotonicity of \(\alpha\log\sigma\) in \(R_0\) and in \(\alpha\)

Defining \(\mu=R_0^{1/\alpha}\), so that \(\sigma=\mu^2/(2\mu-1)\) and \(\alpha\log\sigma=2\log R_0 - \alpha\log(2R_0^{1/\alpha}-1)\):

$$ \frac{\partial(\alpha\log\sigma)}{\partial R_0}=\frac{2}{R_0}-\frac{2R_0^{1/\alpha-1}}{2R_0^{1/\alpha}-1}=\frac{2(\mu-1)}{R_0(2\mu-1)}. $$

Positive for \(\mu>1\) (i.e. \(R_0>1\)). ✓

For \(\alpha\), define \(h(\mu)=(2\mu-1)\log(2\mu-1)-2\mu\log\mu\). Then \(h(1)=0\) and

$$ h'(\mu)=2\log(2\mu-1)+2-2\log\mu-2=2\log\!\left(2-\frac{1}{\mu}\right)>0\text{ for }\mu>1, $$

so \(h(\mu)>0\) for \(\mu>1\), which implies \(\partial(\alpha\log\sigma)/\partial\alpha<0\). Matches the supplement.

C.6 Euler–Lotka / \(z\) identities

\(\rho_k=1/(1+kz)\), \(R_0\rho_1^\alpha=1\), \(R_0\rho_2^\alpha=((1+z)/(1+2z))^\alpha\), and \(R_0^2\rho_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\rho_1^{2\psi\alpha}=((1+z)^2/(1+2z))^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\). All consistent with how they're used in the Var/Mode derivations.

D. Issues needing attention that aren't formal errors but I flag them

D.1 The \(k(\psi)\) equation in uncontrolled.tex (overdispersion with periodic contacts)

Currently:

$$ k=\frac{2}{\bar c}\,A^{2}\!\left(1+\frac{\omega^{2}}{r^{2}}\right)^{\!\psi\alpha} $$

Several problems:

  1. \(A\), \(\bar c\), and \(\omega\) are never defined in the main text. I assume \(A\) is the cosine amplitude (currently called \(\epsilon\) in the periodic contact model) and \(\omega\) is the angular frequency (\(2\pi\) for a daily period). \(\bar c\) presumably is \(E_t[c]=1\), so it collapses out.
  2. A scratch expression at the bottom of the same file has \(2/\bar c^{2}\), not \(2/\bar c\). One of the two is wrong, and without the derivation being in the supplement I can't tell which.
  3. The exponent \(\psi\alpha\) (rather than the \((1-\psi)\alpha\) that appears in the \(W\) results) is consistent with the intuition in the paper: for the periodic-contact overdispersion story, what matters is how tightly the offspring \(\epsilon_j\) samples the contact clock, which is governed by the Gamma\((\psi\alpha,\beta)\) piece (not the shared \(l_i\)).
  4. The derivation for this should be written out in the supplement before this equation can be trusted. Right now it is just asserted.

D.2 Independence/factorization assumption in eq:A_def_general

The text says "assume that \(c_i\) is independent of \(\nu_i\) and \(b_i\), but allow \(\nu_i\) and \(b_i\) to depend on one another," then writes

$$ E_i[\nu_i b_i(\tau)c_i(t_i+\tau)]=E_i[\nu_i b_i(\tau)]\,E_i[c_i(t_i+\tau)]. $$

This requires \(c_i(t_i+\tau)\) to be independent of \((\nu_i,b_i)\). If \(t_i\) is person \(i\)'s infection time on the calendar, and \(t_i\) can correlate with anything about \(i\), then this factorization has a hidden assumption: either \(c_i\) is literally deterministic (e.g. the periodic model) or \(t_i\) is independent of \((\nu_i,b_i,c_i)\). Probably fine for your use cases, but worth a sentence making it explicit.

D.3 Reference contact definition

"\(E_t[c_i(t)]=1\)" is strictly a time-average, not a probabilistic expectation — the manuscript says "for a randomly chosen time \(t\)," which is OK but might read more cleanly as \(\lim_{T\to\infty}T^{-1}\int_0^T c_i(t)\,dt=1\) (or, for stationary random \(c_i\), the stationary mean). For the periodic model, \(T^{-1}\int_0^T (1-\epsilon\cos(2\pi t))\,dt\to 1\). ✓

D.4 Placement of the supplementary §"The modulating role of \(\alpha\log\sigma\)"

The supplementary subsections "Monotonicity in \(R_0\)" and "Monotonicity in \(\alpha\)" differentiate eq:alpha_log_sigma, but that equation is defined after them in the file. Reordering so the definition precedes the derivatives would help the reader.

E. Minor / typographical

  • model.tex: "the {\bf Supplementary Methods}" and "{\bf Table XX}", "{\bf Figure XX}" placeholders scattered throughout. (Known TODOs, not math.)
  • uncontrolled.tex: "simultions" → "simulations"; "deatils" → "details".
  • supplementary.tex, §"Monotonicity in \(R_0\)": "\(\frac{d}{dR_0}(2R_0^{1/\alpha})=(2/\alpha)R_0^{1/\alpha-1}\)" — correct.
  • supplementary.tex, §"Mode of the two-moment Gamma approximation…": "\(E[W^n\mid\text{extinct}]=0\) almost surely since the epidemic fails to establish" — this is true because \(W=\lim e^{-rt}Z(t)=0\) on the extinction event; \(W\) is zero there deterministically, not just in expectation. The text could be sharpened to say \(W=0\) on extinction.
