F. Summary

The core derivations — Euler–Lotka, \(\mathrm{Var}[W]\), \(E[W^2]\), \(\mathrm{Mode}[\varsigma]\), and the \(R_0\)/\(\alpha\) monotonicity of \(\alpha\log\sigma\) — are correct. I did not find any error in the supplement's algebra. The substantive issues are two definitional typos in model.tex:

  1. \(a_i(\tau)=(\beta/\gamma)\delta_{t_i}(\tau)\) should be \(a_i(\tau)=(\beta/\alpha)\delta_{t_i}(\tau)\) (i.e. \(R_0\,\delta_{t_i}(\tau)\));
  2. \(b_i(\tau)=f_\epsilon(\tau)\) for \(\tau>l_i\) should be \(b_i(\tau)=f_\epsilon(\tau-l_i)\).

Everything that flows from these inside the supplement already assumes the corrected forms, so only the main-text statements need to be fixed; the downstream results are unaffected.

The biggest notation issue is that identifiability.tex still uses \(\kappa\) where the rest of the .tex uses \(\psi\). That needs a global \(\kappa\to\psi\) substitution in that file.

Secondary notation issues to consider at your discretion: the SEIR \((\alpha,\beta,\gamma)\) symbols collide with the Gamma generation-interval \((\alpha,\beta)\) symbols; the cosine amplitude \(\epsilon\) collides with the Gamma lag \(\epsilon_j\); and \(t_i\) is used for two different things (spike location on the \(\tau\)-axis vs. calendar infection time).

Parameterizing the Pareto contact model to have mean 1 (2026-04-10)

The question: in the "Pareto" contact model, each person's contact modulator \(c_i(t)\) switches at Poisson\((\lambda)\) times to a new level \(c^*\) drawn independently from a Pareto distribution. For this to be consistent with the reference contact process \(c_i(t)\equiv 1\) against which \(R_0\) and \(\nu_i\) are defined, we need the time-average of \(c_i(t)\) to equal 1. For a Poisson-switching process with iid level draws, the long-run time-average equals \(\mathbb{E}[c^*]\):

$$ \lim_{T\to\infty}\frac{1}{T}\int_0^T c_i(t)\,dt \;=\;\frac{\mathbb{E}[\,c^*\cdot\text{(length of one sojourn)}\,]}{\mathbb{E}[\,\text{length of one sojourn}\,]} \;=\;\mathbb{E}[c^*], $$

since the sojourn lengths are iid \(\mathrm{Exp}(\lambda)\) with mean \(1/\lambda\) and independent of the levels. So \(\mathbb{E}[c^*]=1\) is the constraint, and \(\lambda\) cancels out of the mean-1 requirement (it controls how often the contact state turns over, not the long-run mean).

Pareto Type I (the "classic" Pareto)

Pareto Type I has pdf

$$ f(c\mid\alpha_{\!P},\,c_{\min}) \;=\;\frac{\alpha_{\!P}\,c_{\min}^{\alpha_{\!P}}}{c^{\,\alpha_{\!P}+1}},\qquad c\ge c_{\min}>0,\ \alpha_{\!P}>0. $$

Two parameters: the shape \(\alpha_{\!P}\) (the tail index, subscripted with \(P\) to distinguish it from the Gamma shape \(\alpha\) used for the generation interval) and the scale \(c_{\min}\), which is the hard floor. When the moments exist,

$$ \mathbb{E}[c^*]=\frac{\alpha_{\!P}\,c_{\min}}{\alpha_{\!P}-1}\quad(\alpha_{\!P}>1), \qquad \mathrm{Var}[c^*]=\frac{c_{\min}^{\,2}\,\alpha_{\!P}}{(\alpha_{\!P}-1)^2(\alpha_{\!P}-2)}\quad(\alpha_{\!P}>2). $$

Setting the mean equal to 1:

$$ \frac{\alpha_{\!P}\,c_{\min}}{\alpha_{\!P}-1}=1 \;\Longleftrightarrow\; \boxed{\,c_{\min}=\frac{\alpha_{\!P}-1}{\alpha_{\!P}}=1-\frac{1}{\alpha_{\!P}}\,}. $$

This requires \(\alpha_{\!P}>1\) (otherwise the mean is infinite and the constraint cannot be satisfied). The mean-1 Pareto is therefore a one-parameter family indexed by the shape \(\alpha_{\!P}>1\), with \(c_{\min}\) pinned by the constraint. Substituting \(c_{\min}=1-1/\alpha_{\!P}\) into the variance formula collapses it to

$$ \mathrm{Var}[c^*]=\frac{1}{\alpha_{\!P}\,(\alpha_{\!P}-2)}\qquad(\alpha_{\!P}>2). $$

So \(\alpha_{\!P}\) alone controls the tail heaviness:

  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\to\infty\): \(c_{\min}\to 1\), \(\mathrm{Var}\to 0\), distribution degenerates at 1 (reference process recovered).
  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\to 2^+\): variance \(\to\infty\), mean still 1.
  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\in(1,2]\): finite mean, infinite variance (heavy enough tail that \(c^2\) is not integrable).
  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\le 1\): infeasible.

Tail behavior. The survival function is \(P(c^*>c)=(c_{\min}/c)^{\alpha_{\!P}}=\left((1-1/\alpha_{\!P})/c\right)^{\alpha_{\!P}}\) for \(c\ge c_{\min}\), so the tail decays as a power law \(c^{-\alpha_{\!P}}\). Smaller \(\alpha_{\!P}\) means a heavier right tail, which is the mechanism by which rare-but-very-high contact levels drive temporal superspreading in the epidemic simulations.

Practical caveat (the floor). Because \(c_{\min}=1-1/\alpha_{\!P}>0\), Type I enforces a hard floor on the contact process — nobody ever has zero contact. For \(\alpha_{\!P}=2\) the floor is 0.5, for \(\alpha_{\!P}=10\) it is 0.9. If you want the model to be able to reach zero (e.g. "fully isolated" stretches), a Lomax / Pareto Type II is the alternative: support \([0,\infty)\), density \(\alpha_{\!P}\lambda_{\!P}^{\alpha_{\!P}}/(c+\lambda_{\!P})^{\alpha_{\!P}+1}\), mean \(\lambda_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P}-1)\); setting the mean to 1 gives \(\lambda_{\!P}=\alpha_{\!P}-1\), again a one-parameter family. Lomax has a strictly heavier effective tail at the same nominal shape, but loses the minimum-contact-rate interpretation.

Recommendation

Start with Pareto Type I and \(\alpha_{\!P}>2\) (so the variance is finite), with \(c_{\min}\) fixed at \((\alpha_{\!P}-1)/\alpha_{\!P}\). That gives a single tunable parameter \(\alpha_{\!P}\in(2,\infty)\) with a clean "heaviness dial" interpretation, finite variance \(1/(\alpha_{\!P}(\alpha_{\!P}-2))\), and well-behaved second moments for any analytical derivation of \(k(\psi,\alpha_{\!P})\) you'll do in the overdispersion section. If you later want to push into the infinite-variance regime for illustration, \(\alpha_{\!P}\in(1,2]\) is still available, but anything involving \(\mathrm{Var}[c^*]\) will need to be treated separately there.

Pareto contact model — interpretation in actual-contacts space

Setup. The contact modulator \(c_i(t)\) is a proportional deviation from the reference process \(\bar{c}\), so the actual number of contacts for person \(i\) at time \(t\) is \(\bar{c}\,c_i(t)\). With \(\bar{c}\approx 5\), an "actual contacts" value of 1 corresponds to \(c_i=0.2\), and an "actual contacts" value of 50 corresponds to \(c_i=10\). The question is: what achievable ranges does the mean-1 Pareto family support, and does it include contact patterns ranging from ~1 to ~50?

1. The mean-1 Pareto Type I family is restrictive.

Under the Type I parameterization, \(c^*\ge c_{\min}=1-1/\alpha_{\!P}\). Multiplying by \(\bar{c}=5\), the hard floor in actual contacts is

$$\bar{c}\cdot c_{\min} \;=\; 5\left(1 - \frac{1}{\alpha_{\!P}}\right)\;\in\;\left(2.5,\,5\right)\quad\text{for}\quad \alpha_{\!P}\in(2,\infty).$$

So a minimum as low as 1 contact is simply not reachable with mean-1 Pareto Type I — the floor is pinned above \(\bar{c}/2\). Concretely (verified by quantile formula \(c_q = c_{\min}(1-q)^{-1/\alpha_{\!P}}\), multiplied by \(\bar{c}=5\)):

\(\alpha_{\!P}\)minq5q25medianq75q95q99q99.9SD
2.012.512.582.903.555.0111.1524.8478.1035.27 (diverging)
2.102.622.683.003.645.0710.9123.4770.2610.91
2.503.003.063.373.965.229.9418.9347.554.47
3.003.333.393.674.205.299.0515.4733.332.89
5.004.004.044.244.595.287.2810.0515.921.29
10.04.504.524.634.825.176.077.138.980.56

Interpretation: with mean 1 enforced, \(\alpha_{\!P}\) simultaneously governs (i) the minimum (always above \(\bar{c}/2\)) and (ii) the tail heaviness. You can reach actual contacts up to ~25 at 99% and ~70 at 99.9% (for \(\alpha_{\!P}=2.1\)), but you cannot dip below ~2.6. The "1–50 span" is therefore not achievable in Type I — the rigid coupling of min and tail via the mean-1 constraint prevents it.

2. Lomax (Pareto Type II) can achieve the desired range.

The Lomax / Type II distribution has support \([0,\infty)\) with density

$$f_{c^*}(c)\;=\;\frac{\alpha_{\!P}}{\sigma}\left(1+\frac{c}{\sigma}\right)^{-(\alpha_{\!P}+1)},\qquad c\ge 0$$

and mean \(\sigma/(\alpha_{\!P}-1)\), so enforcing mean 1 gives \(\sigma=\alpha_{\!P}-1\). Quantile: \(c_q=(\alpha_{\!P}-1)[(1-q)^{-1/\alpha_{\!P}}-1]\). Under the mean-1 constraint, \(\mathrm{Var}[c^*]=\alpha_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P}-2)\) for \(\alpha_{\!P}>2\). Same table, with \(\bar{c}=5\):

\(\alpha_{\!P}\)minq5q25medianq75q95q99q99.9SD
2.010.000.130.782.085.0217.3744.87151.970.89 (diverging)
2.100.000.140.812.155.1417.4043.79142.122.91
2.500.000.160.912.405.5617.3639.82111.411.18
3.000.000.171.012.605.8717.1436.4290.008.66
5.000.000.211.182.976.3916.4130.2459.626.46
10.00.000.231.313.236.6915.7226.3244.795.59

With Lomax at \(\alpha_{\!P}\approx 3\), for example: q25=1.0, median=2.6, q95=17, q99=36, q99.9=90. The bulk of the distribution sits in the 1–17 range and the upper tail reaches 36–90. This is much closer to the "1 to 50" range. The trade-off is that Lomax does not have a hard floor — about 25% of observations dip below 1 actual contact (and ~10% below 0.5), which corresponds to quiet periods (sleeping, solitude, weekend at home) when the contact level is far below average.

3. What the Pareto model is actually doing in contact-space.

In both cases, the contact process for person \(i\) is a piecewise-constant stochastic process:

  • At each switching epoch (Poisson rate \(\lambda\)), a new level is drawn from \(c^*\) and held until the next epoch.
  • The long-run time average of \(\bar{c}\,c_i(t)\) equals \(\bar{c}\,E[c^*]=\bar{c}\), so each person's expected contact rate, averaged over time, equals the reference \(\bar{c}\). In this sense, everyone is "the same on average".
  • What varies is the time spent at each level. A person whose current level is 10 (50 actual contacts) is in a temporary high-contact regime — a house party, a crowded meeting — while a person at level 0.2 (1 actual contact) is in a quiet regime. On average (over time, for any individual, and over individuals at any instant), the level is 1 and actual contacts are \(\bar{c}\).
  • The tail exponent \(\alpha_{\!P}\) controls the heaviness of the upper tail. Small \(\alpha_{\!P}\) means rare but very extreme spikes (superspreader events); large \(\alpha_{\!P}\) means more uniformity. For \(\alpha_{\!P}>2\) the second moment is finite, which is what you need for the analytical \(k\) derivation to make sense.
  • The switching rate \(\lambda\) determines how long each regime lasts. If \(1/\lambda\) is much longer than the infectiousness profile \(b_i(\tau)\), each person's infectiousness profile is multiplied by a single level \(c^*\), and the overdispersion pattern depends sensitively on that draw. If \(1/\lambda\) is much shorter than \(b_i\), the contact process time-averages out within a single infection window and contributes little overdispersion. The interaction between \(\lambda\) and \(b_i\) is analogous to the interaction between \(\omega\) and \(b_i\) in the periodic model.

4. Comparison, and recommendation.

The practical question is whether you want a hard floor on person-time contact rates, or a soft floor at zero with most mass above some positive threshold. They give qualitatively different contact-space behaviors:

Pareto Type I (hard floor)Lomax / Type II (soft floor at 0)
Support\([\bar{c}(1-1/\alpha_{\!P}),\infty)\)\([0,\infty)\)
Reach low values (~1 contact at \(\bar{c}=5\))?No, floored at ~2.5–4Yes (~25% mass below 1 at \(\alpha_{\!P}=3\))
Reach high values (~50 contacts)?Only in extreme tail (q99.9)Around q99 at \(\alpha_{\!P}\in[2.5,3]\)
"1 to 50" spanNot achievableAchievable, covering ~70–80% of mass
Interpretation of floorBaseline always-on contact rateQuiet periods, possibly near-zero
Free parameters under mean-11 (\(\alpha_{\!P}\))1 (\(\alpha_{\!P}\))
Finite variance\(\alpha_{\!P}>2\), \(\mathrm{Var}=1/(\alpha_{\!P}(\alpha_{\!P}-2))\)\(\alpha_{\!P}>2\), \(\mathrm{Var}=\alpha_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P}-2)\)

Recommendation: given that you explicitly want contact rates to span something like 1–50 (an order-of-magnitude spread around \(\bar{c}=5\)), switch the "Pareto contact model" to a Lomax (Pareto Type II) distribution with mean 1. With \(\sigma=\alpha_{\!P}-1\), a single parameter \(\alpha_{\!P}\in(2,\infty)\) covers the full range you want:

  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\approx 3\) is a good default: median actual contacts 2.6, IQR [1.0, 5.9], 95%ile 17, 99%ile 36, 99.9%ile 90. Finite variance (SD\(\cdot\bar{c}=8.66\)).
  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\approx 5\) for a "tamer" heavy-tail scenario: IQR [1.2, 6.4], 99%ile 30, SD\(\cdot\bar{c}=6.46\).
  • \(\alpha_{\!P}\approx 2.1\) for a heavy-tail / near-superspreader scenario: 99%ile 44, 99.9%ile 142 (close to variance divergence).

All three sit within your "2 to 20" (via bulk) to "1 to 50" (via tails) target range, and the meaning of \(\alpha_{\!P}\) as a tail-heaviness dial survives. The price is that ~25% of person-time is below 1 contact, which actually seems physically reasonable for a stochastic social-contact model (quiet periods).

If you really want a hard floor above zero (nobody ever dips below some baseline), the cleanest generalization is a shifted Lomax with two free parameters: \(c^*=c_0+Y\) with \(Y\sim\mathrm{Lomax}(\alpha_{\!P},\sigma)\) and \(c_0+\sigma/(\alpha_{\!P}-1)=1\), so \(\sigma=(1-c_0)(\alpha_{\!P}-1)\). Then \(c_0\) is the hard floor (e.g.\ \(c_0=0.2\) means min = 1 actual contact with \(\bar{c}=5\)) and \(\alpha_{\!P}\) independently tunes the tail. But this adds a parameter and a small amount of algebraic fuss for the \(k(\psi)\) derivation downstream; the one-parameter Lomax is probably the right place to start.

Derivation of the negative binomial \(k\) under the Lomax contact model

Goal. Derive the Lloyd-Smith (2005) overdispersion parameter \(k\) for the secondary-infection distribution when the biological profile is the time-shifted Gamma model with punctuation parameter \(\psi\) and the contact modulator is a Poisson-switching piecewise-constant process with Lomax(\(\alpha_{\!P}\), \(\sigma=\alpha_{\!P}-1\)) levels.

Step 0. Lloyd-Smith bridge: \(k=R_0^2/\mathrm{Var}[M_i]\)

In the Lloyd-Smith framework, the number of secondary infections from person \(i\) is \(Z_i\sim\mathrm{Poisson}(M_i)\) where \(M_i\) is a random individual reproduction number. The marginal distribution of \(Z_i\) is \(\mathrm{NegBin}(R_0,k)\) in the Lloyd-Smith parameterization (mean \(R_0\), variance \(R_0(1+R_0/k)\)) exactly when \(M_i\) is Gamma-distributed; for other \(M_i\) distributions, \(\mathrm{NegBin}(R_0,k)\) is a two-moment approximation. Either way, matching moments gives

$$ k \;=\; \frac{R_0^{\,2}}{\mathrm{Var}[M_i]}. $$

From the decomposition \(a_i(t_i,\tau)=\nu_i\,b_i(\tau)\,c_i(t_i+\tau)\), the conditional-on-\((b_i,c_i,\nu_i)\) mean offspring count is

$$ M_i \;=\; \int_0^\infty a_i(t_i,\tau)\,d\tau \;=\; \nu_i\,\tilde c_i,\qquad \tilde c_i \;=\; \int_0^\infty b_i(\tau)\,c_i(t_i+\tau)\,d\tau. $$

In this section we isolate the effect of punctuation and contact variability by holding \(\nu_i\equiv R_0\) (no extra individual intensity variation), so \(M_i=R_0\,\tilde c_i\) and

$$ k \;=\; \frac{R_0^{\,2}}{R_0^{\,2}\,\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]} \;=\; \frac{1}{\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]}. $$

All the work is in computing \(\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]\).

Mean of \(\tilde c_i\) (sanity check). Condition on \(b_i\) (treated as a random density):

$$ E[\tilde c_i\mid b_i] \;=\; \int_0^\infty b_i(\tau)\,E[c_i(t_i+\tau)]\,d\tau \;=\; \int_0^\infty b_i(\tau)\cdot 1\,d\tau \;=\; 1, $$

using stationarity of \(c_i(\cdot)\) with mean \(E[c^*]=1\), and the fact that \(b_i\) is a probability density. Hence \(E[\tilde c_i]=1\) and \(E[M_i]=R_0\), as required.

Step 1. Variance of \(\tilde c_i\) via conditioning

Since \(E[\tilde c_i\mid b_i]=1\) is nonrandom, the law of total variance collapses:

$$ \mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i] \;=\; E\!\bigl[\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i\mid b_i]\bigr] \;+\; \mathrm{Var}\!\bigl[E[\tilde c_i\mid b_i]\bigr] \;=\; E\!\bigl[\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i\mid b_i]\bigr]. $$

Conditional on \(b_i\), \(\tilde c_i\) is a weighted integral of a stationary process, so

$$ \mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i\mid b_i] \;=\; \int_0^\infty\!\int_0^\infty b_i(\tau)\,b_i(\tau')\,K_c(\tau-\tau')\,d\tau\,d\tau', $$

where \(K_c(s)=\mathrm{Cov}[c_i(t),c_i(t+s)]\) is the (stationary) autocovariance of the contact process.

Step 2. Autocovariance of the Lomax-Poisson contact process

The process \(c_i(\cdot)\) is piecewise constant with switching epochs from a Poisson process of rate \(\lambda\), and at each epoch a fresh iid draw from the Lomax distribution is assigned. At time \(t\), let \(c^*\) be the current level and let \(N(s)\) be the number of switches in \([t,t+s]\). Because levels are iid,

$$ \mathrm{Cov}[c_i(t),\,c_i(t+s)] \;=\; \mathrm{Var}[c^*]\cdot P(\text{no switch in }[t,t+s]) \;=\; V_c\,e^{-\lambda|s|}, $$

where \(V_c:=\mathrm{Var}[c^*]=\dfrac{\alpha_{\!P}}{\alpha_{\!P}-2}\) is the variance of a mean-1 Lomax with tail exponent \(\alpha_{\!P}>2\). (Intuitively: conditional on \textit{not} having switched by time \(t+s\), both \(c_i(t)\) and \(c_i(t+s)\) equal the same draw, contributing \(V_c\); if there has been at least one switch, the two draws are independent and contribute zero to the covariance.)

Therefore

$$ \mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i\mid b_i] \;=\; V_c\,\underbrace{\int_0^\infty\!\!\int_0^\infty b_i(\tau)\,b_i(\tau')\,e^{-\lambda|\tau-\tau'|}\,d\tau\,d\tau'}_{\displaystyle =:\;I(b_i)}. $$

Step 3. \(I(b_i)\) does not depend on the latent period \(l_i\)

Recall \(b_i(\tau)=f_\epsilon(\tau-l_i)\) for \(\tau>l_i\), where \(f_\epsilon\) is the \(\mathrm{Gamma}(\psi\alpha,\beta)\) density and \(l_i\sim\mathrm{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha,\beta)\). Substitute \(u=\tau-l_i\), \(u'=\tau'-l_i\):

$$ I(b_i) \;=\; \int_0^\infty\!\!\int_0^\infty f_\epsilon(u)\,f_\epsilon(u')\,e^{-\lambda|u-u'|}\,du\,du' \;=\; E\!\left[e^{-\lambda|U-U'|}\right]\;=:\; I^*, $$

where \(U,U'\overset{\text{iid}}{\sim}\mathrm{Gamma}(\psi\alpha,\beta)\). The \(l_i\) drops out by translation invariance: shifting both \(\tau\) and \(\tau'\) by the same amount leaves \(|\tau-\tau'|\) unchanged. Consequently,

$$ \mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]\;=\;V_c\,I^*\qquad\text{and}\qquad k\;=\;\frac{1}{V_c\,I^*}\;=\;\frac{\alpha_{\!P}-2}{\alpha_{\!P}\,I^*}. $$

Step 4. Evaluating \(I^*\) in closed form

Write \(I^*\) using the Fourier representation \(e^{-\lambda|s|}=\dfrac{\lambda}{\pi}\displaystyle\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\dfrac{e^{i\omega s}}{\lambda^2+\omega^2}\,d\omega\). Interchanging integration order,

$$ I^* \;=\; \frac{\lambda}{\pi}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\frac{|\varphi_U(\omega)|^2}{\lambda^2+\omega^2}\,d\omega, \qquad \varphi_U(\omega)=E[e^{i\omega U}]=\Bigl(\frac{\beta}{\beta-i\omega}\Bigr)^{\!\psi\alpha}, $$

so \(|\varphi_U(\omega)|^2=(1+\omega^2/\beta^2)^{-\psi\alpha}\). Substitute \(t=\omega/\beta\) and let \(r:=\lambda/\beta\):

$$ I^* \;=\; \frac{2r}{\pi}\int_0^\infty\frac{dt}{(r^2+t^2)\,(1+t^2)^{\psi\alpha}}. $$

Change variable \(u=t^2\) (so \(dt=u^{-1/2}du/2\)):

$$ I^* \;=\; \frac{r}{\pi}\int_0^\infty\frac{u^{-1/2}\,du}{(r^2+u)\,(1+u)^{\psi\alpha}}. $$

This is an instance of Gradshteyn–Ryzhik 3.197.1, which reduces it to a Gauss hypergeometric function. Factoring \(r^2+u=r^2(1+u/r^2)\) and applying the identity with \(\mu=\tfrac12\), \(\nu=1\), \(\rho=\psi\alpha\), \(\beta_{\rm GR}=1/r^2\), \(\gamma_{\rm GR}=1\):

$$ \int_0^\infty\frac{u^{-1/2}\,du}{(r^2+u)\,(1+u)^{\psi\alpha}} \;=\; \frac{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma\!\left(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12\right)}{r\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)}\;{_2F_1}\!\left(\psi\alpha,\tfrac12;\,\psi\alpha+1;\,1-r^2\right). $$

Plugging back,

$$ \boxed{\; I^* \;=\; \frac{\Gamma\!\left(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12\right)}{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)}\;{_2F_1}\!\left(\psi\alpha,\,\tfrac12;\,\psi\alpha+1;\,1-\Bigl(\tfrac{\lambda}{\beta}\Bigr)^{\!2}\right). \;} $$

Step 5. Assembling \(k\)

Combining Steps 3 and 4,

$$ \boxed{\; k \;=\; \underbrace{\frac{\alpha_{\!P}-2}{\alpha_{\!P}}}_{\text{(i) contact-tail factor}}\;\cdot\; \underbrace{\frac{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)}{\Gamma\!\left(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12\right)}}_{\text{(ii) profile-width factor}}\;\cdot\; \underbrace{\frac{1}{{_2F_1}\!\left(\psi\alpha,\tfrac12;\,\psi\alpha+1;\,1-(\lambda/\beta)^2\right)}}_{\text{(iii) switching-rate factor}}. \;} $$

The three factors admit distinct interpretations:

  1. Contact-tail factor \((\alpha_{\!P}-2)/\alpha_{\!P}\). Depends only on the Lomax tail exponent: equals \(1-2/\alpha_{\!P}\in(0,1)\) and drops to \(0\) as \(\alpha_{\!P}\searrow 2\) (variance-divergence limit of \(c^*\)). Heavier contact tails \(\Rightarrow\) smaller \(k\) \(\Rightarrow\) more overdispersion.
  2. Profile-width factor \(\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)/\Gamma(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12)\). Depends only on the effective biological-profile shape \(\psi\alpha\), equals \(1\) when \(\psi=0\) (spike), and by Stirling grows like \(\sqrt{\pi\psi\alpha}\) for large \(\psi\alpha\). Wider individual profiles \(\Rightarrow\) larger \(k\) \(\Rightarrow\) less overdispersion.
  3. Switching-rate factor \({_2F_1}(\psi\alpha,\tfrac12;\psi\alpha+1;\,1-r^2)^{-1}\), \(r=\lambda/\beta\). This captures the interaction between the timescale of biological infectiousness (\(1/\beta\)) and the timescale of contact-regime sojourns (\(1/\lambda\)).

Sanity checks and limiting cases

(a) Spike limit \(\psi\to 0\)

With \(\psi\alpha\to 0\):

  • Factor (ii): \(\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(1)/\Gamma(\tfrac12)=\sqrt{\pi}/\sqrt{\pi}=1\).
  • Factor (iii): \({_2F_1}(0,\tfrac12;1;\,x)=1\) (the series terminates at the \(n=0\) term because \((0)_n=0\) for \(n\ge 1\)), so the inverse is \(1\).

Hence \(k\to(\alpha_{\!P}-2)/\alpha_{\!P}\). This matches the direct argument: when \(b_i\to\delta_{\tau_i^*}\), \(\tilde c_i=c_i(t_i+\tau_i^*)\) is a single draw from the stationary distribution of \(c_i\), so \(\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]=V_c\) and \(k=1/V_c=(\alpha_{\!P}-2)/\alpha_{\!P}\). ✓

(b) Slow-switching limit \(\lambda\to 0\), any \(\psi\)

Here \(r\to 0\), so \(1-r^2\to 1\). Use Gauss's summation theorem:

$$ {_2F_1}(a,b;c;1)=\frac{\Gamma(c)\,\Gamma(c-a-b)}{\Gamma(c-a)\,\Gamma(c-b)},\qquad\mathrm{Re}(c-a-b)>0. $$

With \(a=\psi\alpha\), \(b=\tfrac12\), \(c=\psi\alpha+1\), we get \(c-a-b=\tfrac12>0\) and

$$ {_2F_1}(\psi\alpha,\tfrac12;\psi\alpha+1;1)=\frac{\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)\,\Gamma(\tfrac12)}{\Gamma(1)\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12)}=\frac{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)}{\Gamma(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12)}, $$

which exactly cancels factor (ii). So in the slow-switching limit, \(k\to(\alpha_{\!P}-2)/\alpha_{\!P}\) for any \(\psi\). ✓

Interpretation. When contact regimes are held for much longer than the infection window (\(1/\lambda\gg 1/\beta\)), each person sees effectively a single \(c^*\) draw over their entire infectious interval; the temporal shape of \(b_i\) becomes irrelevant. All the overdispersion comes from the Lomax level distribution, regardless of \(\psi\).

(c) Fast-switching limit \(\lambda\to\infty\), any \(\psi\)

Here \(r\to\infty\). The integrand in \(I^*\) is dominated by \(r^{-2}\), giving \(I^*=O(r^{-2})\to 0\), so \(\mathrm{Var}[\tilde c_i]\to 0\) and \(k\to\infty\). ✓

Interpretation. When regimes flip much faster than the infection window (\(1/\lambda\ll 1/\beta\)), the contact process time-averages to its mean \(1\) over each person's infection window, so \(\tilde c_i\to 1\) deterministically. There is no contact-driven overdispersion; the offspring distribution is Poisson (\(k=\infty\)).

(d) Smooth (non-punctuated) limit \(\psi\to 1\)

Here \(b_i\equiv g\), so \(\tilde c_i\) is the same deterministic integral of \(c_i\) (shifted by \(l_i=0\)) for every person. All the variation in \(\tilde c_i\) is temporal averaging of a single \(c_i\). The formula specializes to

$$ k_{\psi=1}=\frac{\alpha_{\!P}-2}{\alpha_{\!P}}\cdot\frac{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\alpha+1)}{\Gamma(\alpha+\tfrac12)}\cdot{_2F_1}\!\bigl(\alpha,\tfrac12;\alpha+1;1-(\lambda/\beta)^2\bigr)^{-1}. $$

Monotonicity in \(\psi\) (at fixed \(\alpha,\alpha_{\!P},\lambda/\beta\))

Physically, \(\tilde c_i\) is a weighted temporal average of \(c_i\) using the kernel \(b_i\). Narrower kernels (smaller \(\psi\)) perform less averaging, retaining more of \(c_i\)'s variance; wider kernels perform more averaging, reducing variance. Therefore \(I^*\) is monotonically decreasing in \(\psi\) (at fixed \(\lambda,\beta,\alpha\)), and \(k\) is monotonically increasing in \(\psi\): more punctuated biological profiles produce more overdispersion, with the contact-variance amplitude \(V_c=\alpha_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P}-2)\) setting the overall scale.

A more formal argument: factor (ii) is strictly increasing in \(\psi\) (since \(\Gamma(x+1)/\Gamma(x+\tfrac12)\) is increasing for \(x\ge 0\), by properties of the digamma function), and factor (iii) is also increasing in \(\psi\) for every fixed \(r\) (intuitively: the hypergeometric in the denominator interpolates monotonically between \(1\) at \(\psi=0\) and \(\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)/\Gamma(\psi\alpha+\tfrac12)\) at \(r=0\), and all three factors conspire so that the \(r\)-dependence fades out as \(\psi\to 0\)). A fully rigorous proof is deferred to a separate note.

Note on the two-moment closure

The expression \(k=R_0^2/\mathrm{Var}[M_i]\) holds exactly whenever \(M_i\) is Gamma-distributed; otherwise, it is a two-moment approximation to the mean and variance of the Lloyd-Smith \(\mathrm{NegBin}(R_0,k)\) model. Under our setup, \(M_i=R_0\tilde c_i\) is a nonlinear functional of the stationary contact process and the random biological kernel, so \(M_i\) is not exactly Gamma; nonetheless, the two-moment match is the standard convention in this literature \citep{lloyd-smith_superspreading_2005} and gives a closed-form answer that respects all the limiting cases above.

Placement in the manuscript

  • Main text (writeup/uncontrolled.tex). Place the boxed final expression for \(k\) from Step 5 alongside the existing periodic-contact \(k\) formula, and reference the supplementary section for the derivation.
  • Supplementary (writeup/supplementary.tex). Add a new subsection "Overdispersion under the Lomax contact model" containing Steps 0–5 (analogous to the existing placeholder for the periodic contact derivation at line 304).
Why is the log-linear growth rate estimate (cases 10–100) biased downward for omicron and measles but not for influenza?

Sub-asymptotic growth bias in early epidemic growth rate estimation

Setup

The code estimates the Malthusian growth rate \(r\) by fitting \(\log n = a + b\, t_n\) for infection events \(n = 10, 11, \dots, 100\), where \(t_n\) is the time of the \(n\)-th infection. The theoretical rate \(r\) satisfies the Lotka–Euler equation for a \(\mathrm{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\) generation interval:

$$R_0 \left(\frac{\beta}{\beta + r}\right)^\alpha = 1 \qquad \Longrightarrow \qquad R_0 = \left(1 + \frac{rT}{\alpha}\right)^\alpha$$

where \(\beta = \alpha/T\). For the three pathogens:

Pathogen\(R_0\)\(\alpha\)\(T\)\(r\)\(rT\)Window (gens)\(R_0/e^{rT}\)
Influenza22.323.20.2520.812.850.89
Omicron62.397.050.3782.670.860.42
Measles1211.3612.20.2282.780.830.75

The key quantity: generations in the fitting window

The time spanned by cases 10–100 is approximately \(\log(100/10)/r = \log 10 / r\). Dividing by the mean generation time \(T\) gives the number of transmission generations in the fitting window:

$$\text{generations in window} = \frac{\log 10}{rT}$$

For influenza this is ≈ 2.85 generations, but for omicron and measles it is < 1 generation.

This matters because the Malthusian rate \(r\) is an asymptotic property of the branching process — it describes the growth rate after the process has gone through enough generations for the "compounding" to stabilize. In the first generation or two, growth is governed by the generation time CDF \(G(\tau)\), not by \(e^{r\tau}\).

Why early growth is slower than \(e^{rt}\)

In the first generation, the expected cumulative infections from the index case are:

$$E[C(t)] \approx 1 + R_0\, G(t)$$

The asymptotic exponential growth predicts \(C(t) \propto e^{rt}\), which at \(t = T\) gives a per-generation multiplier of \(e^{rT}\). Meanwhile, the actual per-generation multiplier is \(R_0\). The standard inequality \((1 + x/n)^n < e^x\) gives us:

$$\boxed{R_0 = \left(1 + \frac{rT}{\alpha}\right)^\alpha < e^{rT}}$$

So the true branching-process multiplier per generation (\(R_0\)) is always less than the exponential prediction (\(e^{rT}\)). The Malthusian rate \(r\) effectively "borrows" growth from future generations — it describes the exponential envelope that the process converges to, not the actual growth in any single generation.

The ratio \(R_0 / e^{rT}\) measures the magnitude of this discrepancy:

  • Influenza: \(R_0/e^{rT} = 0.89\) — mild discrepancy, and the fitting window spans ~3 generations (enough time to converge).
  • Omicron: \(R_0/e^{rT} = 0.42\) — severe discrepancy (the exponential predicts 2.4× more growth per generation than actually occurs), and the window spans < 1 generation.
  • Measles: \(R_0/e^{rT} = 0.75\) — moderate discrepancy, and the window spans < 1 generation.

When is the bias worst?

The bias depends on two factors:

  1. How many generations the fitting window spans — fewer generations means less time to converge to asymptotic growth. This is \(\log 10 / (rT)\), which is small when \(rT\) is large.
  2. How much the per-generation growth departs from the exponential — the ratio \(R_0/e^{rT} = (1 + rT/\alpha)^\alpha / e^{rT}\) is furthest from 1 when \(rT\) is large and \(\alpha\) is small (high generation-time variance).

Both factors point in the same direction: the bias is worst when \(rT\) is large (many "doublings" per generation). Omicron (\(rT = 2.67\)) and measles (\(rT = 2.78\)) both have large \(rT\), while influenza (\(rT = 0.81\)) does not.

Additionally, low \(\alpha\) (high generation-time variance) slows the convergence to exponential growth, which is why omicron (\(\alpha = 2.39\)) shows more bias than measles (\(\alpha = 11.36\)) despite similar \(rT\) values.

Summary

The log-linear regression underestimates the Malthusian rate because the fitting window (cases 10–100) spans < 1 generation for omicron and measles, and the epidemic hasn't yet converged to its asymptotic exponential growth. The per-generation multiplier \(R_0\) is strictly less than the asymptotic prediction \(e^{rT}\) by the inequality \((1 + rT/\alpha)^\alpha < e^{rT}\), so the early-phase growth is systematically slower than \(e^{rt}\). For influenza, the window spans ~3 generations, which is enough for convergence — hence minimal bias.

How is the Malthusian growth rate normally estimated in practice? Have others noted this bias? What should we do instead?

Growth rate estimation: methods, pitfalls, and best practices

1. Is log-linear regression on cumulative cases the standard?

No. Fitting to cumulative case counts is actively discouraged by methodologists. The core problem is that cumulative counts are a running sum, so successive observations are positively autocorrelated by construction. Standard regression assumes independent errors, leading to illusory precision — point estimates may be reasonable, but confidence intervals are far too narrow.

This was demonstrated rigorously by King, Domenech de Cellès, Magpantay & Rohani (2015), "Avoidable errors in the modelling of outbreaks of emerging pathogens, with special reference to Ebola" (Proc R Soc B, 282:20150347). They showed via simulation that fitting deterministic models to cumulative incidence produces "errors that mask their own presence."

The recommended standard is to fit to incidence data (new cases per time unit), not cumulative cases.

2. Has the sub-asymptotic bias been noted?

Yes, though not typically framed in exactly these terms.

  • A 2025 paper in Infectious Disease Modelling (PMC12908057) systematically found that for short fitting windows, both negative binomial regression and Hidden Markov Model approaches underestimate the growth rate, with estimates approaching the true value only as the fitting window grows.
  • Ma, Dushoff, Bolker & Earn (2014), "Estimating initial epidemic growth rates" (Bull Math Biol, 76:245–260), compared phenomenological models and found that simple exponential fits underestimate the growth rate as the fitting window extends beyond the pure exponential phase.
  • Chowell et al. (2016), "Mathematical models to characterize early epidemic growth" (Physics of Life Reviews, 18:66–97), documented that many real epidemics show sub-exponential growth in the first few generations.
  • Champredon & Dushoff (2015), "Intrinsic and realized generation intervals" (Proc R Soc B, 282:20152026), described how the distinction between intrinsic and realized generation intervals relates to the transient dynamics before the age-of-infection distribution stabilizes.

However, the specific framing we identified — connecting the bias to the number of generation times in the fitting window and the inequality \(R_0 = (1 + rT/\alpha)^\alpha < e^{rT}\) — does not appear to have been stated explicitly in the literature. This could be a useful methodological point to formalize.

3. Better approaches

a) Log-linear or Poisson regression on incidence

The simplest improvement: fit \(\log I_t = rt + b\) to incidence \(I_t\) (new cases per time unit), or better yet, use a Poisson/negative binomial GLM:

$$I_t \sim \mathrm{Poisson}(e^{rt + b})$$

This is implemented in the R incidence package (RECON) and the R0 package (est.R0.EG() with method="poisson"). The GLM approach properly handles count data, avoids log(0) issues, and can accommodate overdispersion via negative binomial.

b) Renewal equation estimators

Rather than estimating \(r\) directly, one can estimate \(R_t\) using the renewal equation:

  • Cori et al. (2013) — Bayesian estimation of the instantaneous reproduction number \(R_t\), implemented in the widely-used EpiEstim R package. Assumes a Poisson observation model with a Gamma prior on \(R_t\).
  • Wallinga & Teunis (2004) — Estimates the case reproduction number by computing relative likelihoods of who-infected-whom, weighted by the serial interval distribution.

c) The Wallinga–Lipsitch framework (Euler–Lotka inversion)

The key reference: Wallinga & Lipsitch (2007), "How generation intervals shape the relationship between growth rates and reproductive numbers" (Proc R Soc B, 274:599–604). They showed that different models relating \(r\) to \(R\) are all special cases of:

$$R = \frac{1}{M_g(-r)}$$

where \(M_g\) is the moment generating function of the generation interval distribution. Special cases:

AssumptionFormula
Delta function at \(T\)\(R = e^{rT}\) (upper bound)
Exponential (SIR)\(R = 1 + r/\gamma\)
SEIR\(R = (1 + r/\sigma)(1 + r/\gamma)\)
Gamma(\(\alpha, \beta\))\(R = (1 + rT/\alpha)^\alpha\)
Normal(\(T, \sigma^2\))\(R = e^{rT - r^2\sigma^2/2}\)

In practice, one estimates \(r\) from incidence data, then solves the Euler–Lotka equation for \(R\) given an assumed generation interval distribution. This is what R0::R.from.r() does. Park, Champredon, Weitz & Dushoff (2019) showed the Gamma approximation (using just the mean and CV of the generation interval) is practical and accurate.

d) Important subtlety: intrinsic vs. realized generation intervals

Park, Sun, Champredon et al. (2021) showed in PNAS that using improperly defined serial intervals in early COVID-19 estimates "biased estimates of initial reproduction number by up to a factor of 2.6." During a growing epidemic, backward-looking generation intervals are systematically shorter (recent infectors are overrepresented), biasing \(R\) estimates downward if not corrected.

4. What should we do?

For our simulations, the sub-asymptotic bias is a real artifact of fitting a log-linear model over a window that spans too few generation times. Two practical options:

  1. Fit to incidence rather than cumulative cases. Aggregate infection times into daily bins and fit \(I_t \sim \mathrm{Poisson}(e^{rt + b})\). This doesn't eliminate the sub-asymptotic issue but avoids the autocorrelation problem of cumulative fitting.
  2. Solve the Euler–Lotka equation directly from \(R_0\). Since we know \(R_0\) and the generation interval distribution in the simulations, we can simply compute \(r\) analytically via \(R_0(\beta/(\beta+r))^\alpha = 1\) — this is already done for the theoretical reference line. The simulated growth rates can then be compared to this known value without needing regression at all.
  3. If estimation from trajectories is the goal, extend the fitting window to span at least 2–3 generation times: use cases up to \(\sim R_0^3\) rather than a fixed threshold of 100. For measles this would require \(N \gg 1000\).

5. Key references

  • King AA et al. (2015). Avoidable errors in the modelling of outbreaks. Proc R Soc B, 282:20150347.
  • Wallinga J, Lipsitch M (2007). How generation intervals shape the relationship between growth rates and reproductive numbers. Proc R Soc B, 274:599–604.
  • Ma J, Dushoff J, Bolker BM, Earn DJD (2014). Estimating initial epidemic growth rates. Bull Math Biol, 76:245–260.
  • Cori A et al. (2013). A new framework and software to estimate time-varying reproduction numbers during epidemics. Am J Epidemiol, 178:1505–1512.
  • Champredon D, Dushoff J (2015). Intrinsic and realized generation intervals. Proc R Soc B, 282:20152026.
  • Park SW et al. (2019). A practical generation-interval-based approach to inferring the strength of epidemics from their speed. Epidemics, 27:12–18.
  • Park SW et al. (2021). Forward-looking serial intervals correctly link epidemic growth to reproduction numbers. PNAS, 118:e2011548118.
  • Chowell G et al. (2016). Mathematical models to characterize early epidemic growth. Physics of Life Reviews, 18:66–97.
  • Obadia T et al. (2012). The R0 package. BMC Med Inform Decis Mak, 12:147.
Why does k grow so fast in ψ? Do the line plots and heatmaps reflect the same information?

Exponential growth of k in ψ: line plots vs heatmaps

The formula is exponential in ψ

From Eq. 6 in the paper, the overdispersion parameter under periodic contacts is:

$$k = \frac{2}{A^2}\left(1 + \frac{\omega^2}{\beta^2}\right)^{\psi\alpha}$$

This has the form \(k = c_0 \cdot b^{\psi}\) where:

  • Prefactor: \(c_0 = 2/A^2\) — sets the baseline overdispersion at \(\psi = 0\) (purely from the contact process). For \(A = 0.5\), we get \(c_0 = 8\).
  • Base: \(b = \left(1 + \omega^2/\beta^2\right)^{\alpha}\) — the exponential growth rate in \(\psi\). This depends on the pathogen's generation interval parameters \((\alpha, \beta)\) and the contact frequency \(\omega\).

The base \(b\) is the squared characteristic function of the Gamma\((\alpha, \beta)\) generation interval evaluated at frequency \(\omega\), raised to a power. Pathogens with higher \(\alpha\) (more concentrated generation intervals) or lower \(\beta\) (longer generation times relative to variance) see \(k\) explode faster in \(\psi\).

Growth rates by pathogen

With \(A = 0.5\), period = 1 day (\(\omega = 2\pi\)):

Pathogen\(\alpha\)\(\beta\)base \(b\)\(\psi\) where \(k = 50\)
Influenza2.320.726\(2.3 \times 10^4\)0.18
Omicron2.390.339\(1.2 \times 10^6\)0.13
Measles11.360.931\(8.8 \times 10^{18}\)0.04

At \(\psi = 1\), the values are astronomical: \(k \approx 1.9 \times 10^5\) (influenza), \(9.3 \times 10^6\) (omicron), \(7 \times 10^{19}\) (measles). These are effectively infinite — the offspring distribution is essentially Poisson, with no meaningful overdispersion from the contact process.

Line plots and heatmaps show the same thing

The cross-pathogen line plot (\(k\) vs \(\psi\) at fixed \(A = 0.5\)) is a single horizontal slice through each pathogen's heatmap at \(A = 0.5\). On a log scale, the curves are straight lines with slopes \(\alpha \cdot \log(1 + \omega^2/\beta^2)\). Measles has the steepest slope (largest \(\alpha\)).

The heatmaps show the same formula over the full \((A, \psi)\) plane. When capped at \(k = 50\):

  • The cap saturates across most of the \((\psi, A)\) domain, because \(k\) exceeds 50 very quickly.
  • For measles, essentially only \(\psi \lesssim 0.04\) shows any color variation — the rest is solid at the cap.
  • For influenza, the interesting region extends to \(\psi \approx 0.18\).
  • For omicron, up to \(\psi \approx 0.13\).

The contour lines in the heatmap are level curves of \(k = \frac{2}{A^2}(1 + \omega^2/\beta^2)^{\psi\alpha}\). Setting \(k = k_0\) and solving:

$$A = \sqrt{\frac{2}{k_0}} \cdot \left(1 + \frac{\omega^2}{\beta^2}\right)^{\psi\alpha / 2}$$

These are exponential curves in \(\psi\), explaining why the contours in the heatmap sweep steeply upward and compress toward the left edge.

Physical interpretation

At \(\psi = 0\) (fully punctuated), all of an individual's infection attempts occur at a single random time \(\ell_i\). This maximizes sensitivity to the contact process: whether \(\ell_i\) falls at a peak or trough of \(c(t)\) determines whether the individual produces many or few secondary cases. This creates maximal overdispersion (\(k = 2/A^2\), independent of pathogen).

As \(\psi\) increases, infection attempts spread out over a wider time window (Gamma\((\psi\alpha, \beta)\) spread around \(\ell_i\)). This averages over the contact oscillation, washing out the between-individual variability. The rate at which this averaging happens depends on how the generation interval's timescale compares to the contact period:

  • Measles (\(\alpha = 11.36\)): highly concentrated generation interval, so even a small spread (\(\psi = 0.05\)) covers many contact cycles, rapidly averaging them out.
  • Influenza (\(\alpha = 2.32\)): broader generation interval relative to its mean, so it takes more spreading before the contact oscillation averages out.

The bottom line: the contact-driven overdispersion is only meaningful for highly punctuated profiles (\(\psi\) near 0). For smooth profiles (\(\psi\) near 1), individuals inherently average over the contact cycle, and \(k \to \infty\) (Poisson offspring).

Should we switch from Lomax to lognormal for the switching contact model? Does it make the k derivation cleaner? Should we consider something else entirely?

Choice of level distribution for the Poisson-switching contact model

Key observation: the derivation is distribution-agnostic

Looking back at the Lomax derivation (Steps 0–5 above), the specific distribution of the contact levels \(c^*\) enters only through a single quantity: the variance \(V_c = \mathrm{Var}[c^*]\). Here's why.

For any Poisson-switching process with rate \(\lambda\) and iid level draws from a distribution with mean 1, the autocovariance is

$$K_c(s) = V_c\,e^{-\lambda|s|}$$

regardless of whether the levels are Lomax, lognormal, Gamma, or anything else. This exponential autocovariance is a property of the switching structure (Poisson holding times with iid refreshes), not of the level distribution.

Steps 1–4 of the derivation only use \(K_c(s)\), never the full distribution of \(c^*\). The result was:

$$k = \frac{1}{V_c \cdot I^*}$$

where

$$I^* = \frac{\Gamma\!\left(\psi\alpha+\tfrac{1}{2}\right)}{\sqrt{\pi}\,\Gamma(\psi\alpha+1)}\;{_2F_1}\!\left(\psi\alpha,\,\tfrac{1}{2};\,\psi\alpha+1;\,1-\Bigl(\tfrac{\lambda}{\beta}\Bigr)^{2}\right)$$

depends only on the biological profile parameters (\(\psi\alpha\), \(\beta\)) and the switching rate (\(\lambda\)). The level distribution is entirely absorbed into \(V_c\).

Bottom line: Switching from Lomax to lognormal (or anything else) does not change the derivation at all. It only changes the expression for \(V_c\).

Comparing candidates

Since the derivation is the same, the choice of level distribution is purely about (a) how cleanly \(V_c\) parameterizes and (b) modeling considerations. Here are the main options:

DistributionConstraint for \(E[c^*]=1\)\(V_c = \mathrm{Var}[c^*]\)Free parameter\(k\) formula
Lomax(\(\alpha_{\!P}, \sigma\)) \(\sigma = \alpha_{\!P}-1\) \(\dfrac{\alpha_{\!P}}{\alpha_{\!P}-2}\) \(\alpha_{\!P}>2\) \(\dfrac{\alpha_{\!P}-2}{\alpha_{\!P}\,I^*}\)
Lognormal(\(\mu, \sigma^2\)) \(\mu = -\sigma^2/2\) \(e^{\sigma^2}-1\) \(\sigma^2 > 0\) \(\dfrac{1}{(e^{\sigma^2}-1)\,I^*}\)
Gamma(\(k_c, k_c\)) shape = rate = \(k_c\) \(1/k_c\) \(k_c > 0\) \(\dfrac{k_c}{I^*}\)

The Gamma is the cleanest choice

With \(c^* \sim \mathrm{Gamma}(k_c, k_c)\) (shape = rate = \(k_c\)), we get \(E[c^*] = 1\) and \(V_c = 1/k_c\), giving:

$$\boxed{\;k = \frac{k_c}{I^*(\psi\alpha,\,\lambda/\beta)}\;}$$

This is the simplest possible formula: \(k\) is just the contact shape parameter \(k_c\) divided by the profile-width/switching factor. The advantages:

  1. Simplest parameterization. \(V_c = 1/k_c\) is cleaner than \(e^{\sigma^2}-1\) (lognormal) or \(\alpha_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P}-2)\) (Lomax).
  2. Clean nesting of constant contacts. As \(k_c \to \infty\), \(V_c \to 0\) and \(k \to \infty\) (Poisson offspring). The constant-contact model is smoothly nested as a limiting case. This is the same property you noted for the lognormal (\(\sigma^2 \to 0\)), but the Gamma parameterization is more transparent.
  3. Intuitive interpretation. \(k_c\) directly controls the shape of the contact-level distribution:
    • \(k_c = 1\): exponential (memoryless, moderate variation)
    • \(k_c < 1\): heavy-tailed, L-shaped (many quiet periods, occasional extreme contacts)
    • \(k_c > 1\): bell-shaped, increasingly concentrated around 1
    • \(k_c \to \infty\): point mass at 1 (constant contacts)
  4. The parameter \(k_c\) plays a familiar role. In the Lloyd-Smith framework, individual reproduction numbers follow \(M_i \sim \mathrm{Gamma}(k, R_0/k)\), where \(k\) is the dispersion parameter. Here, the contact levels follow \(c^* \sim \mathrm{Gamma}(k_c, k_c)\). The formula \(k = k_c / I^*\) then says: the offspring dispersion equals the contact dispersion, modulated by how much temporal averaging the biological profile performs. When \(I^* = 1\) (spike limit), you get \(k = k_c\) — the contact variability passes through to the offspring distribution unattenuated.
  5. Compound Poisson with Gamma levels is well studied. The Poisson-switching process with Gamma level draws connects to the Gamma–Poisson (negative binomial) framework used throughout the superspreading literature. The downstream distribution \(Z_i \sim \mathrm{Poisson}(M_i)\) with \(M_i\) driven by Gamma contact levels gives a natural hierarchy that mirrors the standard Lloyd-Smith setup.

Why not lognormal?

Lognormal works — it satisfies both of your requirements (mean 1, variance → 0 recovers constant contacts). But it's less clean:

  • \(V_c = e^{\sigma^2} - 1\) is a nonlinear function of the free parameter, so the \(k\) formula inherits this nonlinearity.
  • The parameter \(\sigma^2\) doesn't have as direct an interpretation as \(k_c\).
  • No conjugacy benefits with the Poisson offspring model.

The lognormal's main advantage — heavier tails than the Gamma for a given variance — is already handled by small \(k_c\) values in the Gamma family. The Gamma with \(k_c \ll 1\) produces extreme right skew (occasional very high contact rates), which captures the same "superspreading-from-contacts" phenomenon that motivates a heavy-tailed level distribution.

Why not Lomax?

The Lomax has the problem you identified: the mean-1 constraint pins \(\sigma = \alpha_{\!P} - 1\), and the requirement \(\alpha_{\!P} > 2\) for finite variance means \(V_c = \alpha_{\!P}/(\alpha_{\!P} - 2) > 1\) always — you cannot have \(V_c < 1\), so you can never get close to constant contacts. There is no smooth path from "Lomax variability" to "constant contacts" through the parameter space. The Gamma and lognormal both allow \(V_c\) to range over \((0, \infty)\), nesting the constant case.

Summary

LomaxLognormalGamma
Nests constant contacts?No (\(V_c > 1\) always)Yes (\(\sigma^2 \to 0\))Yes (\(k_c \to \infty\))
\(k\) formula\(\frac{\alpha_{\!P}-2}{\alpha_{\!P}\,I^*}\)\(\frac{1}{(e^{\sigma^2}-1)\,I^*}\)\(\frac{k_c}{I^*}\)
Derivation complexitySameSameSame
InterpretationTail exponentLog-varianceShape = dispersion
Support\([0, \infty)\)\((0, \infty)\)\([0, \infty)\)

Recommendation: use Gamma(\(k_c, k_c\)) for the contact level distribution. It gives the cleanest formula (\(k = k_c/I^*\)), nests the constant-contact model, and has the most intuitive parameterization. The lognormal is a reasonable alternative but offers no mathematical advantage and a less transparent formula.

Simple analytic results for \(k\) under the stochastic contact model

The overdispersion parameter under the stochastic (Poisson-switching, Gamma-level) contact model is \(k = k_c / I^*\), where

$$I^* = \mathbb{E}\!\left[e^{-\lambda|U - U'|}\right], \qquad U, U' \stackrel{\text{iid}}{\sim} \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)$$

and \(r \equiv \lambda/\beta\) is the switching-to-recovery rate ratio. The exact closed form involves a \({}_2F_1\) hypergeometric function, which, while correct, is not particularly illuminating. Here we identify several clean results that capture the essential behavior.

1. Spectral unification of both contact models

Both the periodic and stochastic contact models can be understood through a single spectral framework. The key identity is:

$$\frac{1}{k} = V_c \cdot \left\langle |\varphi_\varepsilon(\omega)|^2 \right\rangle_{S_c}$$

where \(\varphi_\varepsilon(\omega) = \mathbb{E}[e^{i\omega U}]\) is the characteristic function of \(U \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\), \(S_c(\omega)\) is the power spectral density of the contact process, and \(\langle \cdot \rangle_{S_c}\) denotes the spectral average \(\int_0^\infty (\cdot)\, S_c(\omega)\,d\omega / \int_0^\infty S_c(\omega)\,d\omega\).

The characteristic function of the Gamma distribution gives \(|\varphi_\varepsilon(\omega)|^2 = (1 + \omega^2/\beta^2)^{-\psi\alpha}\), and the two contact models differ only in their spectral content:

Contact modelPower spectral density \(S_c(\omega)\)Resulting \(k\)
Periodic \(c(t) = 1 - A\cos(\omega_0 t)\) Point mass at \(\omega_0\): \(S_c(\omega) = \frac{A^2}{2}\,\delta(\omega - \omega_0)\) \(k = \dfrac{2}{A^2}\!\left(1 + \dfrac{\omega_0^2}{\beta^2}\right)^{\!\psi\alpha}\)
Stochastic (Poisson-switching, rate \(\lambda\)) Lorentzian: \(S_c(\omega) \propto \dfrac{\lambda}{\lambda^2 + \omega^2}\) \(k = \dfrac{k_c}{I^*}\) where \(I^* = \displaystyle\int_0^\infty \frac{(1+\omega^2/\beta^2)^{-\psi\alpha}}{\pi(\lambda^2+\omega^2)/\lambda}\,d\omega\)

Physical interpretation: The periodic model concentrates all its spectral power at a single frequency \(\omega_0\). If \(\omega_0\) is high relative to \(\beta\), the infectious profile averages it away very effectively, making \(k\) large (little overdispersion). The stochastic model spreads power across all frequencies (Lorentzian spectrum), so even when high frequencies are averaged out, the low-frequency components survive. This is why the stochastic model generically produces more robust overdispersion than the periodic model at comparable parameters.

2. Clean exact result for \(\psi\alpha = 1\)

When \(\psi\alpha = 1\), the infectious-period draws \(U, U'\) become \(\text{Exp}(\beta)\), and a classical result gives \(|U - U'| \sim \text{Exp}(\beta)\) as well (this can be verified: if \(U, U' \stackrel{\text{iid}}{\sim} \text{Exp}(\beta)\), then \(|U - U'|\) has density \(\beta\,e^{-\beta x}\) for \(x \geq 0\)). Therefore:

$$I^* = \mathbb{E}[e^{-\lambda|U-U'|}] = \int_0^\infty \beta\,e^{-(\lambda+\beta)x}\,dx = \frac{\beta}{\beta + \lambda} = \frac{1}{1 + r}$$

and we get the beautifully simple result:

$$\boxed{k = k_c\!\left(1 + \frac{\lambda}{\beta}\right) \qquad \text{when } \psi\alpha = 1}$$

This says: the overdispersion parameter is the contact dispersion \(k_c\) multiplied by \((1 + r)\), where \(r = \lambda/\beta\) is the ratio of the switching rate to the generation-interval rate. When switching is fast relative to the generation interval (\(r \gg 1\)), each person's infectious window spans many contact regimes, so the contact heterogeneity is averaged out and \(k\) is large. When switching is slow (\(r \ll 1\)), each person is essentially stuck in one contact regime for their entire infectious period, so \(k \approx k_c\).

Numerical verification (Monte Carlo with \(10^6\) samples):

Pathogen\(\psi\) for \(\psi\alpha=1\)\(\lambda\)\(r\)\(I^*_{\text{exact}}\)\(I^*_{\text{MC}}\)\(k/k_c\)
Influenza0.43111.380.42050.42022.38
Influenza0.431\(2\pi\)8.660.10350.10349.66
Omicron0.418\(2\pi\)18.50.05120.051219.5
Measles0.088\(2\pi\)6.750.12900.12897.75

3. Exact result for \(\psi\alpha = 2\)

For \(\psi\alpha = 2\), a longer but still tractable calculation (convolving the density of \(|U - U'|\) when \(U \sim \text{Gamma}(2, \beta)\)) gives:

$$I^* = \frac{r + 2}{2(r+1)^2}, \qquad k = k_c \cdot \frac{2(1+r)^2}{r+2} \qquad \text{when } \psi\alpha = 2$$

This is still fairly clean: for \(r \gg 1\), \(k/k_c \approx 2r\), growing linearly; for \(r \ll 1\), \(k/k_c \approx 1\). Verified numerically to high accuracy.

4. Geometric-mean (Jensen) bound

A simple upper bound on \(k/k_c\) follows from Jensen's inequality applied to the convex function \(e^{-\lambda|\cdot|}\):

$$k \leq k_c \!\left(1 + \frac{\lambda}{\beta}\right)^{2\psi\alpha}$$

This has the attractive exponential-in-\(\psi\) structure that mirrors the periodic result, but its accuracy depends strongly on the regime:

Pathogen\(\psi\)\(\psi\alpha\)\(k/k_c\) (exact)BoundRatio
Influenza0.050.121.621.691.05
Influenza0.100.232.412.871.19
Influenza0.200.464.408.221.87
Measles0.050.574.5410.22.25
Measles0.101.148.7110512.0
Measles0.202.2715.110,965728

The bound is within ~5% for \(\psi\alpha \lesssim 0.15\) but becomes extremely loose for larger \(\psi\alpha\). This is because the true \(k/k_c\) grows much more slowly than exponentially in \(\psi\alpha\) under the stochastic model — the broadband spectrum means that low-frequency components are never fully averaged out, placing an effective ceiling on how large \(k/k_c\) can get.

5. Periodic vs. stochastic: quantitative comparison

At comparable frequency parameters (\(\omega_0 = \lambda = 2\pi\,\text{day}^{-1}\), \(A = 0.5\)):

Pathogen\(\psi\)Periodic \(k\)Stochastic \(k/k_c\)
Influenza0.0513.21.62
Influenza0.2059.84.40
Omicron0.1032.33.32
Omicron0.20130.67.58
Measles0.0570.84.54
Measles0.2049,19415.1

The stochastic model produces dramatically more overdispersion (smaller \(k/k_c\)) than the periodic model gives \(k\), especially for measles at moderate \(\psi\). The key reason: the periodic model's single-frequency noise is efficiently averaged away by a broad infectious profile, while the stochastic model's broadband noise always retains low-frequency power that cannot be averaged out.

6. Summary and recommended presentation

For the paper, we recommend presenting the stochastic contact model results as follows:

  1. Lead with the spectral interpretation: Both models are spectral averages of the infectious profile's characteristic function against the contact process's power spectral density. This unifies the two models conceptually.
  2. State the exact \(\psi\alpha = 1\) result prominently: \(k = k_c(1 + \lambda/\beta)\). This is as clean as the periodic result and has a transparent physical interpretation (how many contact-regime switches fit in one generation interval).
  3. Note the qualitative difference from periodic: Under stochastic contacts, \(k/k_c\) grows subexponentially in \(\psi\) (roughly as a low-degree polynomial for moderate \(\psi\alpha\)), in contrast to the exponential growth under periodic contacts. This means the stochastic model predicts more persistent overdispersion across a wider range of \(\psi\).
  4. Relegate the \({}_2F_1\) formula to supplementary material, presenting it as the exact closed form that can be computed for any parameters but emphasizing that the qualitative behavior is captured by the simpler results above.
In survival_theory.R, we calculate the extinction probability using the implicit function $q = e^{R_0(q-1)}$. In parameters.R, we use $p_{\text{est}} = 1 - 1/R_0$. Why the discrepancy? Do they agree under certain assumptions?

Extinction probability: exact vs. approximate

The exact result (survival_theory.R)

For a Poisson branching process with mean offspring $R_0 > 1$, the extinction probability $q$ is the smallest positive root of the probability generating function fixed-point equation:

$$q = G(q) = e^{R_0(q - 1)}$$

This comes from the PGF of the Poisson distribution: $G(s) = e^{R_0(s-1)}$. The extinction probability satisfies $q = G(q)$ by the theory of Galton-Watson processes. There is no closed-form solution — hence the iterative solver in extinction_prob().

The approximation (parameters.R)

The formula $p_{\text{est}} = 1 - 1/R_0$ (i.e., $q = 1/R_0$) is the exact extinction probability for a geometric offspring distribution with mean $R_0$. It is also the standard result from deterministic SIR/SIS theory, where the probability of a major epidemic equals $1 - 1/R_0$.

For the Poisson case, $q = 1/R_0$ is an approximation that becomes increasingly inaccurate as $R_0$ grows.

How close are they?

We can compare them by substituting $q = 1/R_0$ into the exact equation and checking the residual. If $q = 1/R_0$ were exact, we'd need:

$$\frac{1}{R_0} = e^{R_0(1/R_0 - 1)} = e^{1 - R_0}$$

i.e., $R_0 = e^{R_0 - 1}$, which only holds at $R_0 = 1$. So for $R_0 > 1$, there is always a gap. Let's quantify it by expanding around $R_0 = 1$. Write $R_0 = 1 + \epsilon$. The exact equation $q = e^{R_0(q-1)}$ has the small-$\epsilon$ solution:

$$q_{\text{exact}} = 1 - \frac{2\epsilon}{R_0} + O(\epsilon^2) = 1 - 2\epsilon + O(\epsilon^2)$$

while the approximation gives:

$$q_{\text{approx}} = \frac{1}{R_0} = \frac{1}{1+\epsilon} = 1 - \epsilon + O(\epsilon^2)$$

So near $R_0 = 1$, both give $q \approx 1$ (extinction almost certain), but the leading-order corrections differ by a factor of 2: the exact establishment probability is $\approx 2\epsilon$ while the approximation gives $\approx \epsilon$. Concretely:

$R_0$$q_{\text{exact}}$ (Poisson)$q_{\text{approx}} = 1/R_0$$p_{\text{est, exact}}$$p_{\text{est, approx}}$
1.50.4170.6670.5830.333
2.00.2030.5000.7970.500
3.00.05950.3330.9400.667
12.0$\approx 6 \times 10^{-6}$0.083$\approx$ 10.917

The approximation substantially overestimates the extinction probability (underestimates establishment) for moderate-to-large $R_0$.

Why the difference?

The key is the offspring distribution. The $1/R_0$ formula is exact when each individual produces a geometrically distributed number of offspring (which has variance $R_0(R_0+1)$, i.e., highly overdispersed). With Poisson offspring (variance = mean = $R_0$), there is less stochastic variability, so extinction is harder once $R_0 > 1$, and the exact extinction probability is lower than $1/R_0$.

More generally, for any offspring distribution with mean $R_0$ and PGF $G(s)$, the extinction probability satisfies $q = G(q)$. The more overdispersed the offspring distribution, the higher the extinction probability (since a few superspreaders compensate for many who infect no one). The $1/R_0$ result corresponds to maximal overdispersion (geometric), while the Poisson result $q = e^{R_0(q-1)}$ corresponds to the equidispersed case.

Recommendation

The p_establish() function in parameters.R should probably use the exact Poisson result (from extinction_prob() in survival_theory.R) for consistency with the simulation model, which draws Poisson offspring counts.

On the Mode of ς and whether modes transform under monotone maps

The claim in the writeup

The supplementary text derives \(\text{Mode}[\varsigma]\) by claiming: "The time-shift \(\varsigma = \log(W)/r\) is a monotone transformation of \(W\), so its modal value is obtained by transforming the mode of the Gamma approximation." This gives:

$$\text{Mode}[\varsigma]_{\text{writeup}} = \frac{1}{r}\log\!\left(\frac{k-1}{\lambda}\right) = \frac{1}{r}\log(\text{Mode}[W|\text{surv}])$$

where \(W|\text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\) with shape \(k\) and rate \(\lambda\).

This claim is wrong. Modes do not transform under nonlinear maps. If \(Y = g(X)\) with \(g\) monotone, the mode of \(Y\) is generally not \(g(\text{mode of } X)\), because the density transforms with a Jacobian factor:

$$f_Y(y) = f_X\!\bigl(g^{-1}(y)\bigr)\,\bigl|{(g^{-1})}'(y)\bigr|$$

The Jacobian \(|(g^{-1})'(y)|\) shifts the location of the maximum unless \(g\) is affine (linear). For \(g(w) = \log(w)/r\), the Jacobian is \(1/(ry)\), which is decidedly non-constant.

The correct mode of ς

Let \(W|\text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k,\lambda)\) and \(\varsigma = \log(W)/r\). Then \(W = e^{r\varsigma}\) and the density of \(\varsigma\) is:

$$f_\varsigma(s) = \frac{r\,\lambda^k}{\Gamma(k)}\,e^{krs}\,\exp\!\bigl(-\lambda\, e^{rs}\bigr)$$

The log-density is \(\ell(s) = \text{const} + krs - \lambda\,e^{rs}\), with derivative:

$$\ell'(s) = r\bigl(k - \lambda\, e^{rs}\bigr)$$

Setting \(\ell'(s^*) = 0\):

$$e^{rs^*} = \frac{k}{\lambda} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \boxed{\text{Mode}[\varsigma] = \frac{1}{r}\log\!\frac{k}{\lambda} = \frac{1}{r}\log E[W|\text{surv}] = \frac{1}{r}\log\frac{1}{p}}$$

since \(k/\lambda = E[W|\text{surv}] = 1/p\) where \(p = 1 - q\) is the survival probability.

Compare with the writeup's formula, which uses \((k{-}1)/\lambda\) instead of \(k/\lambda\). The difference is:

$$\text{Mode}[\varsigma]_{\text{correct}} - \text{Mode}[\varsigma]_{\text{writeup}} = \frac{1}{r}\log\!\frac{k}{k-1}$$

This is small when \(k \gg 1\) (approximately \(1/((k{-}1)r)\)), but diverges as \(k \to 1\).

Three consequences

1. The correct mode is always finite

The writeup's formula requires \(k > 1\) (i.e., \(\text{CV}^2[W|\text{surv}] < 1\)) for the argument of the logarithm to be positive. When \(k < 1\), the writeup gives \(-\infty\), leading to the paradox you identified.

The correct formula \(\log(1/p)/r\) is finite and positive for all \(R_0 > 1\), regardless of \(\psi\) or \(k\). There is no paradox: the density of \(\varsigma\) is always unimodal, even when the Gamma density of \(W|\text{surv}\) has no interior mode.

What happens geometrically: the Gamma density diverges like \(w^{k-1}\) as \(w \to 0^+\) when \(k < 1\), but the Jacobian contributes an extra factor of \(w = e^{rs}\), giving \(f_\varsigma(s) \propto e^{krs} \to 0\) as \(s \to -\infty\) (since \(k > 0\)). The singularity at \(W = 0\) is "stretched out" to \(\varsigma \to -\infty\) and simultaneously suppressed by the Jacobian.

2. The correct mode is \(\psi\)-independent

This is perhaps the most surprising consequence. Under the two-moment Gamma approximation, \(\text{Mode}[\varsigma] = \log(1/p)/r\), which depends only on \(R_0\) (through \(p\) and \(r\)) and not on \(\psi\) at all. The punctuation parameter \(\psi\) enters \(k\) and \(\lambda\) but not their ratio \(k/\lambda = 1/p\).

Numerical verification (1M Monte Carlo samples per case):

Pathogen\(\psi\)\(k\)WriteupCorrect\(E[\varsigma]\)Median
Influenza00.79\(-\infty\)0.90−2.07−0.98
Influenza0.50.92\(-\infty\)0.90−1.62−0.69
Influenza11.08−9.540.90−1.21−0.44
Omicron00.41\(-\infty\)0.01−4.17−2.57
Omicron0.50.76\(-\infty\)0.01−2.07−1.29
Omicron11.77−2.200.01−0.81−0.52
Measles01.25−7.030.00−1.98−1.25
Measles0.52.28−2.530.00−1.03−0.66
Measles16.67−0.710.00−0.34−0.22

The correct mode (column 5) is constant within each pathogen, while \(E[\varsigma]\) and the median (columns 6–7) vary strongly with \(\psi\).

3. The mode is a poor summary for these distributions

When \(k\) is near 1, the log-Gamma density of \(\varsigma\) is extremely flat near its peak (verified numerically: the density varies by <3% over several days around the mode for influenza \(\psi = 0\)). The mode is therefore a poorly determined and uninformative summary.

The mean and median are more useful:

$$E[\varsigma] = \frac{\psi(k) - \log\lambda}{r}$$

where \(\psi(k)\) is the digamma function. Unlike the mode, \(E[\varsigma]\) depends on \(k\) (and hence on \(\psi\)) through the digamma term. The qualitative pattern is consistent: smaller \(\psi\) (more punctuated) gives more negative \(E[\varsigma]\) (later typical establishment), with the effect amplified by \(R_0\).

Bottom line

The writeup's Mode[\(\varsigma\)] formula has a mathematical error (modes don't transform under \(\log\)), which creates the false impression that Mode[\(\varsigma\)] is undefined when \(k < 1\). The corrected mode is always finite but \(\psi\)-independent under the Gamma approximation, making it uninteresting as a summary. The mean \(E[\varsigma]\) (or median) captures the \(\psi\)-dependence and is the right quantity to plot and discuss.

Do the mean or median of the ς distribution have clean, closed-form expressions that reveal the ψ-dependence?

Closed-form expressions for E[ς], Var[ς], and Median[ς]

Under the two-moment Gamma approximation \(W \mid \text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\), the time shift is \(\varsigma = \frac{1}{r}\log W\). We can extract exact expressions for the moments of \(\varsigma\) using known formulas for the log-Gamma distribution.

1. The shape parameter k

Recall from earlier: \(k = E_{\text{cond}}^2 / \text{Var}_{\text{cond}}\) with \(E_{\text{cond}} = 1/p\) and \(\text{Var}_{\text{cond}} = (1 + \text{Var}(W))/p - 1/p^2\). Expanding:

$$k = \frac{1/p^2}{(1 + \text{Var}(W))/p - 1/p^2} = \frac{1}{p(1 + \text{Var}(W)) - 1} = \frac{1}{p\,\text{Var}(W) - q}$$

Substituting \(\text{Var}(W) = \frac{c^\alpha + \sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}{1 - c^\alpha}\):

\begin{align} p\,\text{Var}(W) - q &= \frac{p(c^\alpha + \sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1)}{1 - c^\alpha} - q \\ &= \frac{p\,c^\alpha + p\,\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - p - q(1 - c^\alpha)}{1 - c^\alpha} \\ &= \frac{c^\alpha + p\,\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}{1 - c^\alpha} \end{align}

where we used \(p + q = 1\) and \((p+q)\,c^\alpha = c^\alpha\). So:

$$\boxed{k = \frac{1 - c^\alpha}{c^\alpha + p\,\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}}$$

where \(c = \mu/(2\mu-1)\), \(\sigma = \mu^2/(2\mu-1)\), \(\mu = R_0^{1/\alpha}\), and \(p = 1 - q\) is the survival probability. All ψ-dependence in k enters through the single term \(\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\). Since \(\sigma > 1\) for any \(R_0 > 1\), this term is monotonically decreasing in \(\psi\), so \(k\) is monotonically increasing in \(\psi\) (more punctuated = smaller k = more skewed W).

2. Mean of ς: digamma formula

For \(X \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\), the classical result is \(E[\log X] = \psi(k) - \log\lambda\), where \(\psi(\cdot)\) is the digamma function. Since \(\varsigma = \frac{1}{r}\log W\):

$$E[\varsigma] = \frac{\psi(k) - \log\lambda}{r}$$

Now use \(k/\lambda = E_{\text{cond}} = 1/p\), so \(\log\lambda = \log k + \log p\):

$$\boxed{E[\varsigma] = \underbrace{\frac{\log(1/p)}{r}}_{\text{Mode}[\varsigma]} + \underbrace{\frac{\psi(k) - \log k}{r}}_{\text{correction } (<0)}}$$

This is a clean decomposition:

  • The first term is exactly Mode[ς] = log(1/p)/r — ψ-independent, depending only on \(R_0\).
  • The second term is \((\psi(k) - \log k)/r\), which carries all the ψ-dependence through \(k\).

Properties of the correction term \(\psi(k) - \log k\):

  • Always negative, so \(E[\varsigma] < \text{Mode}[\varsigma]\) always.
  • For large \(k\): \(\psi(k) - \log k \approx -\frac{1}{2k} - \frac{1}{12k^2} + \cdots\), so the correction vanishes. This happens at large ψ (smooth profiles).
  • As \(k \to 0^+\): \(\psi(k) - \log k \to -\infty\), so \(E[\varsigma] \to -\infty\). This happens at extreme punctuation (ψ → 0) with moderate \(R_0\).

Interpretation: more punctuated profiles (smaller ψ → smaller k) push the expected epidemic timing later (larger E[ς] in absolute value means the stochastic epidemic is more delayed relative to the deterministic one).

3. Variance and SD of ς: trigamma formula

For \(X \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\), \(\text{Var}[\log X] = \psi'(k)\), the trigamma function. So:

$$\boxed{\text{Var}[\varsigma] = \frac{\psi'(k)}{r^2}, \qquad \text{SD}[\varsigma] = \frac{\sqrt{\psi'(k)}}{r}}$$

For large \(k\): \(\psi'(k) \approx 1/k + 1/(2k^2) + \cdots\), so \(\text{SD}[\varsigma] \approx 1/(r\sqrt{k})\). Again, all ψ-dependence enters through \(k\).

4. Median of ς

The median of a Gamma distribution has no closed-form expression. However, there is a good approximation due to Wilson–Hilferty (1931): for \(X \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\) with \(k \geq 1\):

$$\text{Median}[X] \approx \frac{k}{\lambda}\left(1 - \frac{1}{9k}\right)^3$$

Since \(k/\lambda = 1/p\), this gives:

$$\text{Median}[\varsigma] \approx \frac{1}{r}\log\!\left[\frac{1}{p}\left(1 - \frac{1}{9k}\right)^3\right] = \frac{\log(1/p)}{r} + \frac{3\log(1 - 1/(9k))}{r}$$

The correction term \(3\log(1-1/(9k))/r \approx -1/(3kr)\) for large \(k\), which is similar in magnitude to the mean's correction \(\approx -1/(2kr)\). However, this approximation degrades for \(k < 1\), precisely where we most need it. In practice, numerical evaluation via qgamma(0.5, k, λ) is simplest.

5. Summary table

QuantityExpressionClosed form?ψ-dependent?
Mode[ς]\(\frac{1}{r}\log\frac{1}{p}\)Yes (elementary)No
E[ς]\(\frac{1}{r}\!\left[\log\frac{1}{p} + \psi(k) - \log k\right]\)Yes (digamma)Yes, through \(k(\psi)\)
Var[ς]\(\psi'(k)/r^2\)Yes (trigamma)Yes, through \(k(\psi)\)
Median[ς]\(\frac{1}{r}\log F_\Gamma^{-1}(0.5; k, \lambda)\)NoYes, through \(k(\psi)\)

All ψ-dependence enters through the single parameter:

$$k(\psi) = \frac{1 - c^\alpha}{c^\alpha + p\,\sigma^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}$$

which is monotonically increasing in ψ (smooth → large k → small corrections → ς distribution concentrates near Mode). The digamma and trigamma functions are standard special functions available in R as digamma() and trigamma().

6. What to plot

Recommended: Plot \(E[\varsigma]\) vs \(\psi\) for each pathogen. The formula \(E[\varsigma] = (\psi(k) - \log\lambda)/r\) is exact (not an approximation beyond the initial Gamma moment-matching), reveals the ψ-dependence cleanly, and has the nice interpretation as Mode + correction. Alternatively, plotting \(E[\varsigma] \pm \text{SD}[\varsigma]\) as a ribbon would show both the shift and spread.

The supplement says "since ς and W are monotonically related" we can infer Var[ς] behaviour from Var[W]. Is that correct? And how should the supplement be restructured?

Restructuring the supplement: from Var[W] to E[ς] and Var[ς]

1. Is the monotone-relationship claim correct?

The conclusion is correct but the reasoning is incomplete. The claim on line 60 of supplementary.tex is:

"Since \(\varsigma\) and \(W\) are monotonically related, we can derive an expression for \(\text{Var}[W]\) and will interpret its implications for \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) at the end."

Monotone transformations do not generally preserve variance ordering. Counterexample: let \(X \sim \text{Exp}(1)\) and \(Y \sim \text{Exp}(2)\). Then \(\text{Var}[X] = 1 > \text{Var}[Y] = 1/4\). But \(\text{Var}[\log X] = \text{Var}[\log Y] = \pi^2/6\) — the variance of a log-Exponential is always \(\pi^2/6\) regardless of the rate. So more variance in \(W\) does not automatically imply more variance in \(\log W\).

In our setting, the claim happens to be true for a specific reason: as \(\psi\) varies, \(W|\text{surv}\) traces a one-parameter curve through the Gamma family, parameterised by \(k\) alone (since \(\lambda = kp\) with \(1/p\) fixed). Along this curve:

  • \(\text{Var}[W|\text{surv}] = k/\lambda^2 = 1/(p^2 k)\), decreasing in \(k\)
  • \(\text{Var}[\varsigma] = \psi'(k)/r^2\), also decreasing in \(k\) (trigamma is monotonically decreasing)

Since \(k\) is monotonically increasing in \(\psi\), both variances decrease in \(\psi\). But this follows from the Gamma moment-matching and the specific way \(\psi\) enters \(k\) — not from the monotone relationship between \(W\) and \(\varsigma\) per se.

2. What the supplement already gives you

The existing derivation does almost all the work. After the \(\text{Var}[W]\) derivation (lines 62–157) and the moment-matching (lines 161–216), the supplement has:

  • \(k = 1/\text{CV}^2[W|\text{surv}]\) — line 200
  • \(\lambda = kp\) — line 201
  • Explicit \(\text{CV}^2\) in terms of \(z\) and \(\psi\) — lines 208–216

From these, \(E[\varsigma]\) and \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) are each a one-line derivation. The heavy lifting is done.

3. Proposed restructuring

The supplement currently ends the moment-matching section with the (incorrect) Mode[\(\varsigma\)] derivation (lines 220–228). Replace those ~8 lines with:

New text (LaTeX):

    \label{eq:mean_varsigma} \\[6pt]
    \text{Var}[\varsigma]
