E[W] = 1 always, so why isn't E[ς] = log(1)/r = 0 and ψ-independent? Why does the expectation not map cleanly through the logarithm?

Why E[ς] ≠ log(E[W])/r: Jensen's inequality

Yes — this is exactly right, and it's the same underlying issue as the mode problem, but for means instead of modes. The expectation doesn't commute with nonlinear functions.

The core identity

For any random variable \(X > 0\) and any concave function \(g\) (like \(\log\)):

$$E[g(X)] \leq g(E[X]) \qquad\text{(Jensen's inequality)}$$

Applied here: \(g = \log\), \(X = W\):

$$E[\log W] \leq \log E[W] = \log 1 = 0$$

with equality only if \(W\) is constant (i.e., zero variance). The gap is:

$$\log E[W] - E[\log W] = -E[\log W] \geq 0$$

This gap is sometimes called the Jensen gap, and it depends on the curvature of \(\log\) weighted by the spread of \(W\). More spread in \(W\) → bigger gap → more negative \(E[\log W]\) → more negative \(E[\varsigma]\).

Making it precise for the Gamma approximation

Under \(W|\text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\):

$$E[\log W|\text{surv}] = \psi(k) - \log\lambda$$

and \(E[W|\text{surv}] = k/\lambda = 1/p\), so \(\log E[W|\text{surv}] = \log(1/p)\). The Jensen gap is:

$$\log E[W|\text{surv}] - E[\log W|\text{surv}] = \log(1/p) - \psi(k) + \log\lambda = \log k - \psi(k)$$

This is always positive (since \(\log k > \psi(k)\) for all \(k > 0\)), and it's exactly the negative of our correction term. So:

$$E[\varsigma] = \frac{\log(1/p)}{r} - \frac{\text{Jensen gap}}{r} = \frac{\log(1/p)}{r} - \frac{\log k - \psi(k)}{r}$$

The Jensen gap \(\log k - \psi(k)\) depends on \(k\), which depends on \(\psi\). That's where the \(\psi\)-dependence comes from.

Intuition: why more punctuation → bigger Jensen gap

The Jensen gap for a log-Gamma scales as \(\log k - \psi(k) \approx 1/(2k)\) for large \(k\). More punctuated profiles (smaller \(\psi\)) produce smaller \(k\) (more variable \(W|\text{surv}\)), which means:

  • The distribution of \(W\) is more spread out and right-skewed
  • The \(\log\) transformation "compresses" the large values more than it "stretches" the small values
  • So \(E[\log W]\) gets pulled further below \(\log E[W]\)
  • Hence \(E[\varsigma]\) becomes more negative (epidemic delayed more)

Epidemiological interpretation: with more punctuation, the average \(W\) is still 1 (the martingale limit is unbiased), but the distribution of \(W\) becomes more variable. The nonlinearity of \(\varsigma = \log(W)/r\) converts this symmetric-in-mean variability into an asymmetric shift in timing: the typical epidemic (as measured by \(E[\varsigma]\)) is systematically delayed, even though the mean of \(W\) itself is unaffected.

An analogy

This is the same reason that the geometric mean is always ≤ the arithmetic mean. If you invest in a stock that doubles (+100%) or halves (−50%) with equal probability, the arithmetic mean return is +25% per period, but the geometric mean (which governs long-run growth) is \(\sqrt{2 \times 0.5} = 1\) — zero growth. The "volatility drag" is exactly the Jensen gap of the log. Here, punctuation plays the role of volatility.

Is there a simple expression for E[W^n] in terms of ρ₁, ρ₂, ...?

Computing E[Wn]: the compound Poisson recursion

1. Setup: factoring out the shared latent period

From the distributional fixed-point equation \(W \stackrel{d}{=} \sum_{j=1}^{N} e^{-r\tau_j} W_j\), with \(\tau_j = s + \varepsilon_j\) where \(s \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha, \beta)\) and \(\varepsilon_j \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\) are independent:

$$W \stackrel{d}{=} e^{-rs} \underbrace{\sum_{j=1}^{N} e^{-r\varepsilon_j} W_j}_{=:\, S}$$

The key observation: \(S\) does not depend on \(s\), and \(s\) is independent of \(S\). So:

$$m_n := E[W^n] = \underbrace{E[e^{-nrs}]}_{\rho_n^{(1-\psi)\alpha}} \cdot E[S^n]$$

where \(\rho_k = \beta/(\beta + kr) = 1/(1+kz)\).

2. S is a compound Poisson sum

Conditional on \(s\), the terms \(Y_j = e^{-r\varepsilon_j} W_j\) are iid (the \(\varepsilon_j\) are iid and independent of the \(W_j\)), and \(N \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0)\). So \(S = \sum_{j=1}^N Y_j\) is a compound Poisson sum.

The fundamental property of compound Poisson sums is that their cumulants are trivially related to the summand moments:

$$\kappa_n(S) = R_0 \cdot E[Y^n] = R_0 \cdot E[e^{-nr\varepsilon}] \cdot E[W^n] = R_0 \cdot \rho_n^{\psi\alpha} \cdot m_n$$

This is the key simplification. No Bell polynomials are needed to compute the cumulants — they're just \(R_0 \cdot \rho_n^{\psi\alpha} \cdot m_n\).

3. Converting cumulants to moments (the standard formula)

The \(n\)-th moment of any random variable can be recovered from its cumulants via the standard identity:

$$E[S^n] = \sum_{\text{partitions } \lambda \text{ of } n} B(\lambda) \prod_{i} \kappa_{\lambda_i}$$

where the sum is over all integer partitions of \(n\), and \(B(\lambda)\) is the combinatorial weight for partition \(\lambda = 1^{a_1} 2^{a_2} \cdots\):

$$B(\lambda) = \frac{n!}{\prod_j (j!)^{a_j} \cdot a_j!}$$

For small \(n\), this is just:

\(n\)Partitions\(E[S^n]\) in terms of cumulants
1(1)\(\kappa_1\)
2(2), (1,1)\(\kappa_2 + \kappa_1^2\)
3(3), (2,1), (1,1,1)\(\kappa_3 + 3\kappa_1\kappa_2 + \kappa_1^3\)
4...\(\kappa_4 + 4\kappa_1\kappa_3 + 3\kappa_2^2 + 6\kappa_1^2\kappa_2 + \kappa_1^4\)
5...\(\kappa_5 + 5\kappa_1\kappa_4 + 10\kappa_2\kappa_3 + 10\kappa_1^2\kappa_3 + 15\kappa_1\kappa_2^2 + 10\kappa_1^3\kappa_2 + \kappa_1^5\)

These are the complete Bell polynomials \(B_n(\kappa_1, \ldots, \kappa_n)\). The coefficients (1, 3, 6, 10, 15, ...) are the same for any distribution — they're purely combinatorial.

4. Solving the self-referential equation

Now we have a circularity: \(m_n\) appears on both sides of the equation, because \(\kappa_n = R_0 \rho_n^{\psi\alpha} m_n\) appears in \(E[S^n]\). But \(\kappa_n\) appears only in the single-part partition \(\lambda = (n)\), which contributes exactly \(\kappa_n\). Separating this term:

$$E[S^n] = \kappa_n + D_n$$

where \(D_n\) is the sum over all partitions with ≥ 2 parts (which involves only \(\kappa_1, \ldots, \kappa_{n-1}\), hence only \(m_1, \ldots, m_{n-1}\)).

Substituting \(m_n = \rho_n^{(1-\psi)\alpha} \cdot (\kappa_n + D_n)\) and \(\kappa_n = R_0 \rho_n^{\psi\alpha} m_n\):

$$m_n = R_0 \rho_n^\alpha \cdot m_n + \rho_n^{(1-\psi)\alpha} \cdot D_n$$
$$\boxed{m_n = \frac{\rho_n^{(1-\psi)\alpha} \cdot D_n}{1 - R_0\,\rho_n^\alpha}, \qquad n \geq 2}$$

with \(m_1 = 1\), \(\kappa_k = R_0 \rho_k^{\psi\alpha} m_k\), and \(D_n = B_n(\kappa_1, \ldots, \kappa_{n-1}, 0)\) (the Bell polynomial with the last cumulant set to 0).

5. Explicit formulas for n = 2, ..., 5

Writing \(\kappa_k\) as a shorthand for \(R_0 \rho_k^{\psi\alpha} m_k\):

\begin{align} m_2 &= \frac{\rho_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1 - R_0\rho_2^\alpha} \cdot \kappa_1^2 \\[8pt] m_3 &= \frac{\rho_3^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1 - R_0\rho_3^\alpha} \cdot (\kappa_1^3 + 3\kappa_1\kappa_2) \\[8pt] m_4 &= \frac{\rho_4^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1 - R_0\rho_4^\alpha} \cdot (\kappa_1^4 + 6\kappa_1^2\kappa_2 + 4\kappa_1\kappa_3 + 3\kappa_2^2) \\[8pt] m_5 &= \frac{\rho_5^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1 - R_0\rho_5^\alpha} \cdot (\kappa_1^5 + 10\kappa_1^3\kappa_2 + 10\kappa_1^2\kappa_3 + 15\kappa_1\kappa_2^2 + 5\kappa_1\kappa_4 + 10\kappa_2\kappa_3) \end{align}

Each is computed sequentially: \(m_1 \to \kappa_1 \to m_2 \to \kappa_2 \to m_3 \to \cdots\)

6. Summary

The "Poisson moment recursion with Bell polynomial coefficients" in the existing code is doing exactly the above. The three ingredients are:

  1. Factor out the shared latent period \(s\), giving the prefactor \(\rho_n^{(1-\psi)\alpha}\)
  2. Compound Poisson cumulants: \(\kappa_k = R_0 \rho_k^{\psi\alpha} m_k\) (one line, no combinatorics)
  3. Cumulant → moment conversion via the standard Bell polynomial formula, then solve for \(m_n\) by isolating the self-referential \(\kappa_n\) term

The only "hard-coded" part is the Bell polynomial coefficients (1, 3, 6, 10, 15, ...), which are universal combinatorial constants — the same for any compound Poisson, not specific to our model. They count the number of ways to partition a set of \(n\) elements into groups of specified sizes.

Verification of supplementary.tex: k, λ, and the path to E[ς] and Var[ς]

Verifying k = (1 − σ₁α) / (p·σ₂(1−ψ)α + σ₁α − 1)

Starting from the moment-matching equations:

$$k = \frac{E[W|\text{surv}]^2}{\text{Var}[W|\text{surv}]}, \qquad \lambda = \frac{E[W|\text{surv}]}{\text{Var}[W|\text{surv}]}$$

We have \(E[W|\text{surv}] = 1/p\) and

$$\text{Var}[W|\text{surv}] = \frac{1+\text{Var}[W]}{p} - \frac{1}{p^2}$$

So the denominator of \(k\) is:

$$\text{Var}[W|\text{surv}] = \frac{p(1 + \text{Var}[W]) - 1}{p^2}$$

and therefore

$$k = \frac{1/p^2}{(p(1+\text{Var}[W]) - 1)/p^2} = \frac{1}{p(1 + \text{Var}[W]) - 1}$$

Now substituting \(\text{Var}[W] = \frac{\sigma_1^\alpha + \sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha}\):

$$p(1 + \text{Var}[W]) - 1 = p \cdot \frac{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha + \sigma_1^\alpha + \sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1}{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha} - 1 = \frac{p \cdot \sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha}}{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha} - 1$$ $$= \frac{p \cdot \sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} - 1 + \sigma_1^\alpha}{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha}$$

Therefore:

$$\boxed{k = \frac{1 - \sigma_1^\alpha}{p \cdot \sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} + \sigma_1^\alpha - 1}} \qquad \checkmark$$

And \(\lambda = E[W|\text{surv}] / \text{Var}[W|\text{surv}] = (1/p) \cdot p^2 / (p(1+\text{Var}[W])-1) = p \cdot k\), so:

$$\boxed{\lambda = pk} \qquad \checkmark$$

The clean path to E[ς] and Var[ς]

Since \(W|\text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k, \lambda)\) and \(\varsigma = \log(W)/r\), the log-Gamma transformation gives:

$$E[\varsigma|\text{surv}] = \frac{\psi(k) - \log \lambda}{r}, \qquad \text{Var}[\varsigma|\text{surv}] = \frac{\psi'(k)}{r^2}$$

where \(\psi(\cdot)\) is the digamma function and \(\psi'(\cdot)\) the trigamma function. Since \(\lambda = pk\):

$$\log \lambda = \log p + \log k$$

The mean decomposes as:

$$\boxed{E[\varsigma|\text{surv}] = \underbrace{\frac{\log(1/p)}{r}}_{\text{baseline (}\psi\text{-independent)}} + \underbrace{\frac{\psi(k) - \log k}{r}}_{\text{Jensen gap (}\psi\text{-dependent)}}}$$

Interpretation of the two terms:

  • The baseline term \(\log(1/p)/r\) reflects the "head start" from conditioning on survival — epidemics that survive tend to grow faster early on, arriving \(\log(1/p)/r\) days sooner than the deterministic prediction.
  • The Jensen gap \((\psi(k) - \log k)/r\) is always negative (since \(\psi(k) < \log k\) for all \(k > 0\)) and captures the asymmetry of the log transformation: higher variance in \(W\) (lower \(k\)) leads to a more negative gap, meaning epidemics arrive even earlier on average. This is the "volatility drag" effect.

The entire \(\psi\)-dependence flows through \(k\) alone:

  • As \(\psi \to 0\) (spike): \(\sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} \to \sigma_2^\alpha > 1\), making the denominator larger, so \(k\) decreases. This increases both \(|\text{Jensen gap}|\) and \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\).
  • As \(\psi \to 1\) (smooth): \(\sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} \to 1\), so \(k \to (1 - \sigma_1^\alpha)/(p + \sigma_1^\alpha - 1)\), the maximum value of \(k\).

Minor notational issue on line 202

The supplement writes:

$$E[W^2|\text{surv}] - E[W|\text{surv}]^2 = \frac{1}{p}E[W^2] - \frac{1}{p^2}E[W]$$

The second term should be \((E[W])^2/p^2 = 1/p^2\), not \(E[W]/p^2\). These coincide when \(E[W] = 1\), but algebraically \(E[W|\text{surv}]^2 = (E[W]/p)^2 = (E[W])^2/p^2 \neq E[W]/p^2\) in general. Suggested fix: simply write \(1/p^2\) since we've already established \(E[W] = 1\).

Properties of the Jensen gap g(k) = ψ(0)(k) − log k

The mean time-shift decomposes as:

$$E[\varsigma|\text{surv}] = \frac{\log(1/p)}{r} + \frac{g(k)}{r}, \qquad g(k) := \psi^{(0)}(k) - \log k$$

where \(\psi^{(0)}\) is the digamma function (using superscript notation to avoid collision with the punctuation parameter \(\psi\)). We need four properties of \(g(k)\).

Property 1: g(k) < 0 for all k > 0 (the gap is always negative)

Proof via Jensen's inequality. Let \(G \sim \text{Gamma}(k, 1)\). Then \(E[G] = k\) and, by a standard identity, \(E[\log G] = \psi^{(0)}(k)\). Since \(\log\) is strictly concave and \(G\) is non-degenerate:

$$\psi^{(0)}(k) = E[\log G] < \log(E[G]) = \log k \qquad \blacksquare$$

This is exactly the "volatility drag" phenomenon: the expected log of a random variable is always less than the log of its expectation, with the gap controlled by the variance.

Property 2: g(k) is strictly increasing

We need to show \(g'(k) = \psi^{(1)}(k) - 1/k > 0\) for all \(k > 0\), where \(\psi^{(1)}\) is the trigamma function.

Proof via integral representation. Using the standard integral forms:

$$\psi^{(1)}(k) = \int_0^\infty \frac{t\, e^{-kt}}{1 - e^{-t}}\, dt, \qquad \frac{1}{k} = \int_0^\infty e^{-kt}\, dt$$

we obtain:

$$\psi^{(1)}(k) - \frac{1}{k} = \int_0^\infty e^{-kt} \left[\frac{t}{1 - e^{-t}} - 1\right] dt$$

The bracketed term is strictly positive for all \(t > 0\), since the inequality \(1 - e^{-t} < t\) (a standard result: \(e^{-t} > 1 - t\) for \(t > 0\)) rearranges to \(t/(1 - e^{-t}) > 1\). Since the integrand is strictly positive on \((0, \infty)\), the integral is strictly positive. \(\blacksquare\)

Property 3: g(k) → 0⁻ as k → ∞

From the asymptotic expansion of the digamma function:

$$\psi^{(0)}(k) = \log k - \frac{1}{2k} - \frac{1}{12k^2} + \frac{1}{120k^4} - \cdots$$

the leading correction is \(-1/(2k)\), so:

$$g(k) \sim -\frac{1}{2k} \quad \text{as } k \to \infty$$

This quantifies the gap for large \(k\) (low variance in \(W\)): the Jensen correction is small, of order \(1/k\).

Note: The asymptotic series involves Bernoulli numbers with alternating signs, so it cannot be used to prove \(g(k) < 0\) — that's what the Jensen argument in Property 1 is for. The series is only useful for quantifying the gap at large \(k\).

Property 4: g(k) → −∞ as k → 0⁺

For small \(k\), the digamma function has a simple pole:

$$\psi^{(0)}(k) = -\frac{1}{k} - \gamma + O(k) \qquad \text{as } k \to 0^+$$

where \(\gamma \approx 0.5772\) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Since \(\log k \to -\infty\) much more slowly than \(-1/k \to -\infty\):

$$g(k) = -\frac{1}{k} - \gamma - \log k + O(k) \to -\infty \qquad \text{as } k \to 0^+$$

Summary: the complete chain

Combining these four properties with the fact that \(k\) is increasing in \(\psi\) (established in the Var[\(W\)] derivation):

More punctuation → Lower \(\psi\) → Lower \(k\) → More negative \(g(k)\) → Earlier epidemic arrival
(established in
model definition)

Each link in the chain is justified:

  • \(\psi \to k\): the denominator of \(k = (1 - \sigma_1^\alpha)/(p\sigma_2^{(1-\psi)\alpha} + \sigma_1^\alpha - 1)\) grows as \(\psi\) decreases (since \(\sigma_2 > 1\))
  • \(k \to g(k)\): Properties 1–2 above (Jensen + strict monotonicity)
  • \(g(k) \to E[\varsigma]\): the decomposition \(E[\varsigma] = \log(1/p)/r + g(k)/r\), where only \(g(k)/r\) depends on \(\psi\)

For variance, the chain is even simpler: \(\text{Var}[\varsigma] = \psi^{(1)}(k)/r^2\), and \(\psi^{(1)}\) (trigamma) is a strictly decreasing function of \(k\), so lower \(k\) → higher \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\).

Model-free generality: Var[W] and the Cauchy-Schwarz argument

The timing effects of punctuated infectiousness do not depend on the Gamma generation interval or the time-shifted Gamma model. The following argument uses only the additive splitting \(\tau = l + \varepsilon\) (shared shift + independent jitter) and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality.

Setup

Let \(g\) be any generation interval distribution with Laplace transform \(M_g(s) = E[e^{-s\tau}]\). The Euler-Lotka equation gives \(R_0 M_g(r) = 1\). Suppose each index case's offspring share a common latent shift \(l\) and receive independent jitter \(\varepsilon_j\), so \(\tau_j = l + \varepsilon_j\) with \(\tau_j \sim g\). The distributions of \(l\) and \(\varepsilon\) are free, subject only to \(M_l \cdot M_\varepsilon = M_g\).

Model-free Var[W] formula

The law-of-total-variance derivation proceeds identically to the Gamma case. The only splitting-dependent step is the cross-term for \(j \neq k\):

$$E[e^{-r\tau_j} e^{-r\tau_k}] = E[e^{-2rl}] \cdot (E[e^{-r\varepsilon}])^2 = M_l(2r) \cdot M_\varepsilon(r)^2$$

Using \(M_l(2r) = M_g(2r)/M_\varepsilon(2r)\) (since \(M_g = M_l \cdot M_\varepsilon\)), this becomes \(M_g(2r) \cdot Q\), where:

$$\boxed{Q := \frac{M_\varepsilon(r)^2}{M_\varepsilon(2r)}}$$

Assembling the full formula (all steps identical to the Gamma derivation):

$$\text{Var}[W] = \frac{A(1 + R_0 Q) - 1}{1 - A} \qquad \text{where } A = R_0 M_g(2r)$$

Here \(A\) depends only on \(g\) (not on the splitting), and \(Q\) is the only place the splitting enters. Since Var[W] is affine in \(Q\) with positive slope \(R_0 A/(1-A)\), it is strictly increasing in \(Q\).

Three key properties of Q

Property 1: \(Q \leq 1\), with equality iff \(\varepsilon\) is degenerate (spike).

This is Cauchy-Schwarz: \(E[e^{-2r\varepsilon}] = E[(e^{-r\varepsilon})^2] \geq (E[e^{-r\varepsilon}])^2 = M_\varepsilon(r)^2\), so \(M_\varepsilon(2r) \geq M_\varepsilon(r)^2\), giving \(Q \leq 1\). Equality holds iff \(e^{-r\varepsilon}\) is a.s. constant, i.e., \(\varepsilon\) is degenerate. \(\blacksquare\)

Property 2: Q is multiplicative under convolution.

If \(\varepsilon_2 = \varepsilon_1 + \delta\) with \(\delta\) independent, then \(M_{\varepsilon_2}(s) = M_{\varepsilon_1}(s) \cdot M_\delta(s)\), so:

$$Q_2 = \frac{M_{\varepsilon_1}(r)^2 M_\delta(r)^2}{M_{\varepsilon_1}(2r) M_\delta(2r)} = Q_1 \cdot Q_\delta \leq Q_1$$

with strict inequality iff \(\delta\) is non-degenerate. So adding independent jitter to \(\varepsilon\) strictly decreases \(Q\). \(\blacksquare\)

Property 3: The spike-vs-smooth gap is controlled by a second application of Cauchy-Schwarz.

For the spike (\(\varepsilon = 0\)): \(Q_{\text{spike}} = 1\).
For the smooth (\(\varepsilon = \tau\)): \(Q_{\text{smooth}} = M_g(r)^2/M_g(2r) = 1/(R_0^2 M_g(2r)) = 1/(R_0 A)\).

The gap is:

$$\text{Var}[W]_{\text{spike}} - \text{Var}[W]_{\text{smooth}} = \frac{R_0 A(1 - Q_{\text{smooth}})}{1 - A} = \frac{R_0 A - 1}{1 - A}$$

Is \(R_0 A > 1\)? Yes: \(R_0 A = R_0^2 M_g(2r) \geq R_0^2 M_g(r)^2 = 1\) by Cauchy-Schwarz applied to \(X = e^{-r\tau}\), with strict inequality for non-degenerate \(g\). \(\blacksquare\)

Extreme cases for Q

Splitting\(Q\)Var[W]
Spike (\(\varepsilon = 0\)) \(1\) \(\frac{A(1 + R_0) - 1}{1 - A}\)   (maximum)
Smooth (\(\varepsilon = \tau\)) \(\frac{1}{R_0 A}\) \(\frac{A}{1 - A}\)   (minimum)

Monotonicity for parameterized families

For a one-parameter family \(\varepsilon_\psi\) indexed by \(\psi \in [0,1]\) with \(\varepsilon_0 = 0\) (spike) and \(\varepsilon_1 = \tau\) (smooth), if the family is ordered by convolution — meaning \(\varepsilon_{\psi_2} \overset{d}{=} \varepsilon_{\psi_1} + \delta\) for some independent \(\delta \geq 0\) whenever \(\psi_2 > \psi_1\) — then \(Q(\psi)\) is strictly decreasing and \(\text{Var}[W](\psi)\) is strictly decreasing in \(\psi\).

The Gamma family satisfies this: \(\varepsilon \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\), and \(\text{Gamma}(\psi_2 \alpha, \beta) \overset{d}{=} \text{Gamma}(\psi_1 \alpha, \beta) + \text{Gamma}((\psi_2 - \psi_1)\alpha, \beta)\). But any infinitely divisible \(g\) admits such a family, as does any \(g\) that can be embedded in a convolution semigroup.

The full model-free chain

Combining with the (model-free) conditioning step and the (Gamma-approximation) digamma/trigamma properties:

  1. (Model-free, Cauchy-Schwarz) More punctuation → higher \(Q\) → higher Var[\(W\)]
  2. (Model-free) Higher Var[\(W\)] → higher Var[\(W|\text{surv}\)] = \((1 + \text{Var}[W])/p - 1/p^2\) → lower \(k\)
  3. (Under Gamma approx., Jensen + monotonicity) Lower \(k\) → more negative \(E[\varsigma]\) and higher Var[\(\varsigma\)]

Steps 1–2 are completely general: they hold for any non-degenerate generation interval distribution and any splitting ordered by convolution. Step 3 uses the Gamma moment-matching approximation, but only for the quantitative relationship — the qualitative direction (lower \(k\) → more variable and more negative time shift) follows from standard properties of the digamma and trigamma functions.

Bottom line: The only distributional assumption needed is that \(g\) is non-degenerate (so that Cauchy-Schwarz is strict). Unimodality, the Gamma shape, and infinite divisibility are not required for the spike-vs-smooth comparison. Monotonicity in a continuous parameter \(\psi\) additionally requires the convolution ordering, which holds for any infinitely divisible \(g\).

Is convex ordering a strong requirement? Is there a nice intuitive proof?

Convex ordering of \(W\) distributions across \(\psi\)

What convex ordering means

Two non-negative random variables satisfy \(W_1 \geq_{cx} W_2\) (convex order, or mean-preserving spread) if \(E[W_1] = E[W_2]\) and $$E[f(W_1)] \geq E[f(W_2)] \quad \text{for every convex } f$$ for which the expectations exist. Equivalently, \(W_1\) is "more spread out" than \(W_2\) in the strongest sense: every risk-averse agent prefers \(W_2\).

Convex ordering implies variance ordering (\(\text{Var}[W_1] \geq \text{Var}[W_2]\), since \(x^2\) is convex), but the converse fails in general. Two distributions with the same mean and ordered variances need not be convex-ordered.

Why we want it

For the \(E[\varsigma]\) result: since \(-\log\) is convex, convex ordering gives us $$W_{\psi_1} \geq_{cx} W_{\psi_2} \quad (\psi_1 < \psi_2) \quad \Longrightarrow \quad E[-\log W_{\psi_1}] \geq E[-\log W_{\psi_2}]$$ which translates directly to \(E[\varsigma_{\psi_1}] \leq E[\varsigma_{\psi_2}]\) (more punctuation → more negative time shift).

Under the Gamma approximation, it's free

This is the key point: if we're already using the Gamma approximation \(W|\text{surv} \sim \text{Gamma}(k_\psi, p \cdot k_\psi)\) with mean \(1/p\) for all \(\psi\), then convex ordering is a classical property of the Gamma family.

Theorem. For Gamma distributions with the same mean \(\mu\): if \(k_1 < k_2\), then $$\text{Gamma}(k_1, k_1/\mu) \geq_{cx} \text{Gamma}(k_2, k_2/\mu).$$

Intuitive proof (integer \(k\)). When \(k\) is a positive integer, \(\text{Gamma}(k, k/\mu)\) has the representation $$W \stackrel{d}{=} \frac{\mu}{k} \sum_{i=1}^{k} E_i$$ where \(E_1, \ldots, E_k\) are iid \(\text{Exp}(1)\). This is \(\mu\) times the sample mean of \(k\) independent exponential draws.

By the law of large numbers, as \(k\) increases, this average concentrates around its expectation \(\mu\). More averaging → less spread → smaller in convex order. This is precisely the temporal diversification argument in another guise:

  • Small \(k\) (punctuated, low \(\psi\)): few independent "draws" → high variability, heavy tails
  • Large \(k\) (smooth, high \(\psi\)): many independent "draws" → tight concentration around the mean

Formal proof sketch. If \(k_2 > k_1\) and both are integers, we can write $$\text{Gamma}(k_2, k_2/\mu) \stackrel{d}{=} E\!\left[\text{Gamma}(k_1, k_1/\mu) \,\big|\, \mathcal{F}\right]$$ where \(\mathcal{F}\) is a suitable \(\sigma\)-algebra — i.e., the more concentrated distribution is a conditional expectation (martingale projection) of the more spread one. This is equivalent to saying the less concentrated one is a mean-preserving spread. For non-integer \(k\), the result follows by continuity of the Gamma family in the shape parameter and the closure of convex order under weak limits.

What convex ordering does and doesn't buy us

ResultNeeds convex ordering?Needs Gamma approx?
\(\text{Var}[W]\) ordered by \(\psi\)No (Cauchy-Schwarz suffices)No (model-free)
\(E[\varsigma]\) ordered by \(\psi\)Sufficient (via \(-\log\) convex)Alternative route (via digamma)
\(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) ordered by \(\psi\)Not sufficientYes, needed

The critical observation for \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\): even with convex ordering, we cannot conclude that \(\text{Var}[\log W]\) is ordered. This is because \(\text{Var}[\log W] = E[(\log W)^2] - (E[\log W])^2\), and while \((\log x)^2\) happens to be convex near 0, it is not globally convex on \((0, \infty)\). So the Gamma approximation (which gives us \(\text{Var}[\varsigma] = \Psi^{(1)}(k)/r^2\) with trigamma decreasing in \(k\)) remains essential for the variance result.

The model-free case

Without the Gamma approximation, establishing convex ordering of the full \(W\) distributions across \(\psi\) would require specific structural assumptions about the branching process martingale limit — this is hard in general.

However, this is somewhat moot because:

  1. The \(\text{Var}[W]\) result is already model-free (no convex ordering needed)
  2. The \(E[\varsigma]\) direction is strongly supported by Jensen + the concavity of \(\log\): higher \(\text{Var}[W]\) heuristically deepens the Jensen gap \(E[\log W] - \log E[W]\), even without convex ordering
  3. The \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) result needs the Gamma approximation regardless of whether we have convex ordering

Summary

Convex ordering is a strong condition in the abstract, but in our setting it adds no extra burden. Under the Gamma approximation (which we already need for the \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) result), convex ordering is an automatic classical property of the Gamma family with fixed mean. The intuitive proof — larger shape parameter means averaging more independent terms, hence more concentration — is the same diversification argument that motivates the entire theory.

Does the generalized gamma also give Var[ς] monotone in Var[W]? And does the Var[W] → Var[ς] story depend only on the distribution of W, not on the specific l and ε?

Robustness of the Var[\(W\)] → Var[\(\varsigma\)] mapping

The hierarchy of assumptions

It helps to separate the argument into two distinct steps:

StepWhat it doesWhat it assumes
Step 1 \((R_0, g, \psi) \;\longrightarrow\; \text{Var}[W]\) Model-free (Cauchy-Schwarz + Euler-Lotka). Depends on \(g\) and \(\psi\) through \(Q = M_\varepsilon(r)^2/M_\varepsilon(2r)\)
Step 2 \(\text{Var}[W] \;\longrightarrow\; E[\varsigma],\; \text{Var}[\varsigma]\) Distributional assumption about \(W\) (Gamma, GenGamma, etc.) + deterministic transform \(\varsigma = \log(W)/r\)

The key insight: Step 2 depends only on the distribution of \(W\). The specific distributions of \(l\) (latent period) and \(\varepsilon\) (jitter) — indeed, the entire infectiousness model — enter only through Step 1, where they determine \(\text{Var}[W]\). Once \(\text{Var}[W]\) is known, the \(\varsigma\) results follow from properties of \(W\) alone.

This means:

  • Any infectiousness model (not just the time-shifted Gamma) that produces the same \(\text{Var}[W]\) will give the same \(E[\varsigma]\) and \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\)
  • The factorization \(M_l \cdot M_\varepsilon = M_\tau\) is used in Step 1 to compute \(\text{Var}[W]\), but plays no role in Step 2
  • If we need to assume something about the distribution of \(W\), that assumption is about a branching-process limit — not about the shape of individual infectiousness profiles

Why this is a strong robustness statement

The distribution of \(W\) (the martingale limit) is a property of the branching process as a whole, not of any individual infection event. By Kesten-Stigum-type theorems, \(W\) is determined by the offspring point process, but its distribution is "smoothed" across many generations. This means:

  1. Different infectiousness models that generate the same first few moments of \(W\) will produce nearly identical \(\varsigma\) distributions
  2. The Gamma (or GenGamma) approximation to \(W\) is relatively robust because \(W\) inherits regularity from the averaging across generations
  3. The assumption "\(W|\text{surv}\) is approximately Gamma" is much milder than "the generation interval is Gamma" — it's a downstream consequence of a limit theorem, not a structural model choice

The generalized gamma

For \(W \sim \text{GenGamma}(a, d, p)\), a key structural property is:

$$\log W = \log a + \frac{1}{p}\,\log G, \qquad G \sim \text{Gamma}(d/p,\, 1)$$

This gives clean formulas:

$$E[\log W] = \log a + \frac{\Psi^{(0)}(d/p)}{p}, \qquad \text{Var}[\log W] = \frac{\Psi^{(1)}(d/p)}{p^2}$$

and therefore:

$$\text{Var}[\varsigma] = \frac{\Psi^{(1)}(d/p)}{p^2 \, r^2}$$

For the regular Gamma (\(p = 1\)): this reduces to \(\Psi^{(1)}(k)/r^2\), depending only on \(k\). Since \(k\) is monotone in \(\text{Var}[W]\), and \(\Psi^{(1)}\) is monotone decreasing, we get clean monotonicity.

For the generalized Gamma: \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) depends on two parameters, \(d/p\) and \(p\). The question of monotonicity reduces to: as \(\text{Var}[W]\) increases (punctuation increases), does \(\Psi^{(1)}(d/p)/p^2\) also increase?

This is not guaranteed by pure analysis — it depends on the specific path \((d(\psi), p(\psi))\) through parameter space determined by the moment-matching conditions. However:

  • \(\Psi^{(1)}(d/p)/p^2\) is decreasing in both \(d/p\) and \(p\). So if both \(d/p\) and \(p\) increase as \(\psi\) increases (more smooth → more "Gaussian-like"), monotonicity is preserved.
  • The regular Gamma is the special case \(p = 1\). If the true \(W\) distribution is close to Gamma, the GenGamma fit will have \(p \approx 1\) and the extra parameter creates no problems.
  • The GenGamma gives a more accurate approximation to \(W\), so any monotonicity that holds for the true \(W\) distribution will be better captured by the GenGamma than by the Gamma.

What could go wrong?

For \(\text{Var}[\log W]\) to decrease when \(\text{Var}[W]\) increases, the extra variance in \(W\) would have to be concentrated in a way that specifically compresses the distribution on the log scale. This would require the right tail of \(W\) to stretch without the left tail moving — possible for pathological distributions, but extremely unlikely for branching process martingale limits, which are unimodal and right-skewed on \((0, \infty)\).

Summary: the robustness picture

ResultDepends on infectiousness model?Depends on distribution of \(W\)?
\(\text{Var}[W]\) increases with punctuation Only through \(Q = M_\varepsilon(r)^2/M_\varepsilon(2r)\) No (model-free)
\(E[\varsigma]\) decreases with punctuation No Jensen gap: yes (direction model-free; magnitude needs Gamma/GenGamma)
\(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) increases with punctuation No Yes: needs \(W \approx \text{Gamma}\) or similar. Proven for Gamma; strongly supported for GenGamma.

Bottom line: The specific infectiousness model (time-shifted Gamma vs. anything else) matters only for computing \(\text{Var}[W]\) in Step 1. Everything downstream — the \(E[\varsigma]\) and \(\text{Var}[\varsigma]\) results — depends only on the distribution of the branching-process martingale limit \(W\), which is a much more generic object. The assumption "\(W|\text{surv}\) is approximately Gamma" is a statement about a limit theorem, not about the shape of individual infectiousness profiles, and is therefore much less fragile than it might appear.

Does more punctuated infectiousness (\(\psi \to 0\)) cause daily incidence to be more variable?

Overdispersion of daily incidence under punctuated profiles

We show that more punctuated individual infectiousness profiles (\(\psi \to 0\)) produce overdispersed daily incidence counts, with the index of dispersion interpolating monotonically from \(1\) (Poisson) at \(\psi = 1\) to approximately \(1 + R_0\) at \(\psi = 0\).

Setup

A single infector \(i\) produces \(\chi_i \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0)\) secondary infections at times \(\tau_j = l_i + \epsilon_j\), where:

  • \(l_i \sim \text{Gamma}((1-\psi)\alpha, \beta)\) is the shared latent period (common to all offspring of \(i\)), and
  • \(\epsilon_j \sim \text{Gamma}(\psi\alpha, \beta)\) is the independent jitter for each offspring \(j\).

Marginally, \(\tau_j \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\), so the generation interval distribution \(g(\tau)\) is invariant in \(\psi\). Let \(N_i(t)\) denote the number of offspring of infector \(i\) whose infection times fall on calendar day \([t, t+1)\), and let \(q_t = P(\tau \in [t, t+1))\) be the probability that a generation interval lands on day \(t\).

Smooth case (\(\psi = 1\)): Poisson daily counts

When \(\psi = 1\), we have \(l_i = 0\) (degenerate) and \(\epsilon_j = \tau_j\). Offspring times are mutually independent. Conditional on \(\chi_i\), the count on day \(t\) is \(\text{Binomial}(\chi_i, q_t)\). Marginalising over \(\chi_i \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0)\) gives

$$N_i(t) \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0 \, q_t)$$

which has index of dispersion (variance-to-mean ratio)

$$\text{ID}_{\text{smooth}} = \frac{\text{Var}[N_i(t)]}{E[N_i(t)]} = 1.$$

Spike case (\(\psi = 0\)): overdispersed counts

When \(\psi = 0\), the jitter is degenerate (\(\epsilon_j = 0\)) and all \(\chi_i\) offspring land at the same random instant \(\tau^* = l_i \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\). The count on day \(t\) is

$$N_i(t) = \begin{cases} \chi_i & \text{if } \tau^* \in [t, t+1) \quad (\text{prob } q_t) \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \quad (\text{prob } 1 - q_t) \end{cases}$$

Computing the moments:

\begin{align} E[N_i(t)] &= E[\chi_i] \cdot q_t = R_0 \, q_t \\ E[N_i(t)^2] &= E[\chi_i^2] \cdot q_t = (R_0 + R_0^2) \, q_t \end{align}

where \(E[\chi_i^2] = \text{Var}[\chi_i] + (E[\chi_i])^2 = R_0 + R_0^2\) since \(\chi_i \sim \text{Poisson}(R_0)\). Therefore

$$\text{Var}[N_i(t)] = R_0 \, q_t + R_0^2 \, q_t(1 - q_t)$$

and the index of dispersion is

$$\text{ID}_{\text{spike}} = 1 + R_0(1 - q_t).$$

Since \(q_t \ll 1\) for any single day (the generation interval spans multiple days), \(\text{ID}_{\text{spike}} \approx 1 + R_0\).

Intuition: In the spike model, an infector either contributes all of their offspring to a single day (with probability \(q_t\)), or none (with probability \(1 - q_t\)). This all-or-nothing contribution creates positive correlations among the offspring landing on the same day, inflating the variance beyond the Poisson level. In the smooth model, offspring times are independent, so these correlations vanish.

General \(\psi\): monotone interpolation

For intermediate \(\psi\), conditional on the shared latent period \(l_i\), the offspring jitter terms \(\epsilon_j\) are independent, so the count on day \(t\) given \(l_i\) is \(\text{Poisson}(R_0 \cdot p(l_i, t))\), where \(p(l, t) = P(\epsilon \in [t - l,\, t - l + 1))\). Applying the law of total variance over \(l_i\):

\begin{align} \text{Var}[N_i(t)] &= E_{l_i}[\text{Var}[N_i(t) \mid l_i]] + \text{Var}_{l_i}[E[N_i(t) \mid l_i]] \\ &= E_{l_i}[R_0 \, p(l_i, t)] + R_0^2 \, \text{Var}_{l_i}[p(l_i, t)] \\ &= R_0 \, q_t + R_0^2 \, \text{Var}_{l_i}[p(l_i, t)] \end{align}

since \(E_{l_i}[p(l_i, t)] = q_t\). The index of dispersion is therefore

$$\text{ID}(\psi) = 1 + R_0 \cdot \frac{\text{Var}_{l_i}[p(l_i, t)]}{q_t}.$$

The quantity \(\text{Var}_{l_i}[p(l_i, t)] / q_t\) interpolates monotonically between the two extremes:

  • At \(\psi = 0\): \(p(l_i, t) = \mathbf{1}(l_i \in [t, t+1))\) is nearly Bernoulli with mean \(q_t\), so \(\text{Var}_{l_i}[p] / q_t = (1 - q_t) \approx 1\), giving \(\text{ID}(0) \approx 1 + R_0\).
  • At \(\psi = 1\): \(l_i = 0\) is degenerate, so \(p(0, t)\) is a constant and \(\text{Var}_{l_i}[p] = 0\), giving \(\text{ID}(1) = 1\).

The monotonicity follows because increasing \(\psi\) transfers mass from \(l_i\) to \(\epsilon_j\): the shared component \(l_i\) has less variance, so \(p(l_i, t)\) fluctuates less across infectors, and the day-\(t\) count becomes closer to Poisson.

Aggregation across infectors

Total daily incidence on day \(t\) is \(\Delta Z(t) = \sum_i N_i(t)\), summing over all previously infected individuals. Conditional on the infection history \(\{t_i\}\), the contributions from distinct infectors are independent (each infector's offspring process is independent). By additivity of variances:

$$\text{Var}[\Delta Z(t) \mid \{t_i\}] = \sum_i \text{Var}[N_i(t)] = \sum_i \bigl[R_0 \, q_{t-t_i} + R_0^2 \, \text{Var}_l[p(l, t - t_i)]\bigr]$$

The aggregate index of dispersion is

$$\phi(\psi) = 1 + R_0 \cdot \frac{\sum_i \text{Var}_l[p(l, t - t_i)]}{\sum_i q_{t - t_i}}$$

which is a weighted average of the per-infector overdispersion factors. The overdispersion does not wash out with aggregation, because each infector's contribution is independently overdispersed by the same mechanism.

Result: Daily incidence \(\Delta Z(t)\) is quasi-Poisson with dispersion parameter \(\phi(\psi)\) that increases monotonically from \(\phi(1) = 1\) (equidispersed) to \(\phi(0) \approx 1 + R_0\) (overdispersed) as \(\psi\) decreases. Since the variance of a growth rate estimator \(\hat{r}\) obtained by regressing log-incidence on time is directly inflated by the dispersion of the daily counts, more punctuated profiles necessarily yield more uncertain growth rate estimates.
